\nSYLLABUS
\nOCTOBER TERM, 2005
\nHAMDAN V. RUMSFELD
\n
\n
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\n
\n

HAMDAN v. RUMSFELD, SECRETARY OF DEFENSE, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the district of columbia circuit

\n

No. 05\xe2\x80\x93184.\xe2\x80\x83Argued March 28, 2006\xe2\x80\x94Decided June 29, 2006

\n

Pursuant to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 Joint Resolution authorizing the President to \xe2\x80\x9cuse all necessary and appropriate force against those nations, organizations, or persons he determines planned, authorized, committed or aided\xe2\x80\x9d the September 11, 2001, al Qaeda terrorist attacks (AUMF), U. S. Armed Forces invaded Afghanistan. During the hostilities, in 2001, militia forces captured petitioner Hamdan, a Yemeni national, and turned him over to the U. S. military, which, in 2002, transported him to prison in Guantanamo Bay, Cuba. Over a year later, the President deemed Hamdan eligible for trial by military commission for then-unspecified crimes. After another year, he was charged with conspiracy \xe2\x80\x9cto commit \xe2\x80\xa6 offenses triable by military commission.\xe2\x80\x9d In habeas and mandamus petitions, Hamdan asserted that the military commission lacks authority to try him because (1) neither congressional Act nor the common law of war supports trial by this commission for conspiracy, an offense that, Hamdan says, is not a violation of the law of war; and (2) the procedures adopted to try him violate basic tenets of military and international law, including the principle that a defendant must be permitted to see and hear the evidence against him.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The District Court granted habeas relief and stayed the commission\xe2\x80\x99s proceedings, concluding that the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority to establish military commissions extends only to offenders or offenses triable by such a commission under the law of war; that such law includes the Third Geneva Convention; that Hamdan is entitled to that Convention\xe2\x80\x99s full protections until adjudged, under it, not to be a prisoner of war; and that, whether or not Hamdan is properly classified a prisoner of war, the commission convened to try him was established in violation of both the Uniform Code of Military Justice (UCMJ), 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7801 et seq., and Common Article 3 of the Third Geneva Convention because it had the power to convict based on evidence the accused would never see or hear. The D. C. Circuit reversed. Although it declined the Government\xe2\x80\x99s invitation to abstain from considering Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s challenge, cf. Schlesinger v. Councilman, 420 U. S. 738, the appeals court ruled, on the merits, that Hamdan was not entitled to relief because the Geneva Conventions are not judicially enforceable. The court also concluded that Ex parte Quirin, 317 U. S. 1, foreclosed any separation-of-powers objection to the military commission\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction, and that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial before the commission would violate neither the UCMJ nor Armed Forces regulations implementing the Geneva Conventions.

\n

Held:\xc2\xa0The judgment is reversed, and the case is remanded.

\n

415 F.\xc2\xa03d 33, reversed and remanded.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Stevens delivered the opinion of the Court, except as to Parts V and VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93iv, concluding:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa01.\xc2\xa0The Government\xe2\x80\x99s motion to dismiss, based on the Detainee Treatment Act of 2005 (DTA), is denied. DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) provides that \xe2\x80\x9cno court \xe2\x80\xa6 shall have jurisdiction to hear or consider \xe2\x80\xa6 an application for \xe2\x80\xa6 habeas corpus filed by \xe2\x80\xa6 an alien detained \xe2\x80\xa6 at Guantanamo Bay.\xe2\x80\x9d Section 1005(h)(2) provides that \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71005(e)(2) and (3)\xe2\x80\x94which give the D. C. Circuit \xe2\x80\x9cexclusive\xe2\x80\x9d jurisdiction to review the final decisions of, respectively, combatant status review tribunals and military commissions\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cshall apply with respect to any claim whose review is \xe2\x80\xa6 pending on\xe2\x80\x9d the DTA\xe2\x80\x99s effective date, as was Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s case. The Government\xe2\x80\x99s argument that \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71005(e)(1) and (h) repeal this Court\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction to review the decision below is rebutted by ordinary principles of statutory construction. A negative inference may be drawn from Congress\xe2\x80\x99 failure to include \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) within the scope of \xc2\xa71005(h)(2). Cf., e.g., Lindh v. Murphy, 521 U. S. 320, 330. \xe2\x80\x9cIf \xe2\x80\xa6 Congress was reasonably concerned to ensure that [\xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71005(e)(2) and (3)] be applied to pending cases, it should have been just as concerned about [\xc2\xa71005(e)(1)], unless it had the different intent that the latter [section] not be applied to the general run of pending cases.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 329. If anything, the evidence of deliberate omission is stronger here than it was in Lindh. The legislative history shows that Congress not only considered the respective temporal reaches of \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71005(e)(1), (2), and (3) together at every stage, but omitted paragraph (1) from its directive only after having rejected earlier proposed versions of the statute that would have included what is now paragraph (1) within that directive\xe2\x80\x99s scope. Congress\xe2\x80\x99 rejection of the very language that would have achieved the result the Government urges weighs heavily against the Government\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation. See Doe v. Chao, 540 U. S. 614, 621\xe2\x80\x93623. Pp.\xc2\xa07\xe2\x80\x9320.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa02.\xc2\xa0The Government argues unpersuasively that abstention is appropriate under Councilman, which concluded that, as a matter of comity, federal courts should normally abstain from intervening in pending courts-martial against service members, see 420 U. S., at 740. Neither of the comity considerations Councilman identified weighs in favor of abstention here. First, the assertion that military discipline and, therefore, the Armed Forces\xe2\x80\x99 efficient operation, are best served if the military justice system acts without regular interference from civilian courts, see id., at 752, is inapt because Hamdan is not a service member. Second, the view that federal courts should respect the balance Congress struck when it created \xe2\x80\x9can integrated system of military courts and review procedures\xe2\x80\x9d is inapposite, since the tribunal convened to try Hamdan is not part of that integrated system. Rather than Councilman, the most relevant precedent is Ex parte Quirin, where the Court, far from abstaining pending the conclusion of ongoing military proceedings, expedited its review because of (1) the public importance of the questions raised, (2) the Court\xe2\x80\x99s duty, in both peace and war, to preserve the constitutional safeguards of civil liberty, and (3) the public interest in a decision on those questions without delay, 317 U. S. at 19. The Government has identified no countervailing interest that would permit federal courts to depart from their general duty to exercise the jurisdiction Congress has conferred on them. Pp.\xc2\xa020\xe2\x80\x9325.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa03.\xc2\xa0The military commission at issue is not expressly authorized by any congressional Act. Quirin held that Congress had, through Article of War 15, sanctioned the use of military commissions to try offenders or offenses against the law of war. 317 U. S., at 28. UCMJ Art. 21, which is substantially identical to the old Art. 15, reads: \xe2\x80\x9cThe jurisdiction [of] courts-martial shall not be construed as depriving military commissions \xe2\x80\xa6 of concurrent jurisdiction in respect of offenders or offenses that by statute or by the law of war may be tried by such \xe2\x80\xa6 commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821. Contrary to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s assertion, even Quirin did not view that authorization as a sweeping mandate for the President to invoke military commissions whenever he deems them necessary. Rather, Quirin recognized that Congress had simply preserved what power, under the Constitution and the common law of war, the President already had to convene military commissions\xe2\x80\x94with the express condition that he and those under his command comply with the law of war. See 317 U. S., at 28\xe2\x80\x9329. Neither the AUMF nor the DTA can be read to provide specific, overriding authorization for the commission convened to try Hamdan. Assuming the AUMF activated the President\xe2\x80\x99s war powers, see Hamdi v. Rumsfeld, 542 U. S. 507, and that those powers include authority to convene military commissions in appropriate circumstances, see, e.g., id., at 518, there is nothing in the AUMF\xe2\x80\x99s text or legislative history even hinting that Congress intended to expand or alter the authorization set forth in UCMJ Art. 21. Cf. Ex parte Yerger, 8 Wall. 85, 105. Likewise, the DTA cannot be read to authorize this commission. Although the DTA, unlike either Art. 21 or the AUMF, was enacted after the President convened Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission, it contains no language authorizing that tribunal or any other at Guantanamo Bay. Together, the UCMJ, the AUMF, and the DTA at most acknowledge a general Presidential authority to convene military commissions in circumstances where justified under the Constitution and laws, including the law of war. Absent a more specific congressional authorization, this Court\xe2\x80\x99s task is, as it was in Quirin, to decide whether Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission is so justified. Pp. 25\xe2\x80\x9330.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa04.\xc2\xa0The military commission at issue lacks the power to proceed because its structure and procedures violate both the UCMJ and the four Geneva Conventions signed in 1949. Pp. 49\xe2\x80\x9372.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(a)\xc2\xa0The commission\xe2\x80\x99s procedures, set forth in Commission Order No. 1, provide, among other things, that an accused and his civilian counsel may be excluded from, and precluded from ever learning what evidence was presented during, any part of the proceeding the official who appointed the commission or the presiding officer decides to \xe2\x80\x9cclose.\xe2\x80\x9d Grounds for closure include the protection of classified information, the physical safety of participants and witnesses, the protection of intelligence and law enforcement sources, methods, or activities, and \xe2\x80\x9cother national security interests.\xe2\x80\x9d Appointed military defense counsel must be privy to these closed sessions, but may, at the presiding officer\xe2\x80\x99s discretion, be forbidden to reveal to the client what took place therein. Another striking feature is that the rules governing Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission permit the admission of any evidence that, in the presiding officer\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, would have probative value to a reasonable person. Moreover, the accused and his civilian counsel may be denied access to classified and other \xe2\x80\x9cprotected information,\xe2\x80\x9d so long as the presiding officer concludes that the evidence is \xe2\x80\x9cprobative\xe2\x80\x9d and that its admission without the accused\xe2\x80\x99s knowledge would not result in the denial of a full and fair trial. Pp. 49\xe2\x80\x9352.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(b)\xc2\xa0The Government objects to this Court\xe2\x80\x99s consideration of a procedural challenge at this stage on the grounds, inter alia, that Hamdan will be able to raise such a challenge following a final decision under the DTA, and that there is no basis to presume, before the trial has even commenced, that it will not be conducted in good faith and according to law. These contentions are unsound. First, because Hamdan apparently is not subject to the death penalty (at least as matters now stand) and may receive a prison sentence shorter than 10 years, he has no automatic right to federal-court review of the commission\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision\xe2\x80\x9d under DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(3). Second, there is a basis to presume that the procedures employed during Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial will violate the law: He will be, and indeed already has been, excluded from his own trial. Thus, review of the procedures in advance of a \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision\xe2\x80\x9d is appropriate. Pp. 52\xe2\x80\x9353.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(c)\xc2\xa0Because UCMJ Article 36 has not been complied with here, the rules specified for Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission trial are illegal. The procedures governing such trials historically have been the same as those governing courts-martial. Although this uniformity principle is not inflexible and does not preclude all departures from courts-martial procedures, any such departure must be tailored to the exigency that necessitates it. That understanding is reflected in Art. 36(b), which provides that the procedural rules the President promulgates for courts-martial and military commissions alike must be \xe2\x80\x9cuniform insofar as practicable,\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(b). The \xe2\x80\x9cpracticability\xe2\x80\x9d determination the President has made is insufficient to justify variances from the procedures governing courts-martial. The President here has determined, pursuant to the requirement of Art. 36(a), that it is impracticable to apply the rules and principles of law that govern \xe2\x80\x9cthe trial of criminal cases in the United States district courts\xe2\x80\x9d to Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission. The President has not, however, made a similar official determination that it is impracticable to apply the rules for courts-martial. And even if subsection (b)\xe2\x80\x99s requirements could be satisfied without an official practicability determination, that subsection\xe2\x80\x99s requirements are not satisfied here. Nothing in the record demonstrates that it would be impracticable to apply court-martial rules here. There is no suggestion, e.g., of any logistical difficulty in securing properly sworn and authenticated evidence or in applying the usual principles of relevance and admissibility. It is not evident why the danger posed by international terrorism, considerable though it is, should require, in the case of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial, any variance from the courts-martial rules. The absence of any showing of impracticability is particularly disturbing when considered in light of the clear and admitted failure to apply one of the most fundamental protections afforded not just by the Manual for Courts-Martial but also by the UCMJ itself: The right to be present. See 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7839(c). Because the jettisoning of so basic a right cannot lightly be excused as \xe2\x80\x9cpracticable,\xe2\x80\x9d the courts-martial rules must apply. Since it is undisputed that Commission Order No. 1 deviates in many significant respects from those rules, it necessarily violates Art. 36(b). Pp. 53\xe2\x80\x9362.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(d)\xc2\xa0The procedures adopted to try Hamdan also violate the Geneva Conventions. The D. C. Circuit dismissed Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s challenge in this regard on the grounds, inter alia, that the Conventions are not judicially enforceable and that, in any event, Hamdan is not entitled to their protections. Neither of these grounds is persuasive. Pp. 62\xe2\x80\x9368.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(i)\xc2\xa0The appeals court relied on a statement in Johnson v. Eisentrager, 339 U. S. 763, 789, n.\xc2\xa014, suggesting that this Court lacked power even to consider the merits of a Convention argument because the political and military authorities had sole responsibility for observing and enforcing prisoners\xe2\x80\x99 rights under the Convention. However, Eisentrager does not control here because, regardless of the nature of the rights conferred on Hamdan, cf. United States v. Rauscher, 119 U. S. 407, they are indisputably part of the law of war, see Hamdi, 542 U. S., at 520\xe2\x80\x93521, compliance with which is the condition upon which UCMJ Art. 21 authority is granted. Pp. 63\xe2\x80\x9365.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(ii)\xc2\xa0Alternatively, the appeals court agreed with the Government that the Conventions do not apply because Hamdan was captured during the war with al Qaeda, which is not a Convention signatory, and that conflict is distinct from the war with signatory Afghanistan. The Court need not decide the merits of this argument because there is at least one provision of the Geneva Conventions that applies here even if the relevant conflict is not between signatories. Common Article 3, which appears in all four Conventions, provides that, in a \xe2\x80\x9cconflict not of an international character occurring in the territory of one of the High Contracting Parties [i.e., signatories], each Party to the conflict shall be bound to apply, as a minimum,\xe2\x80\x9d certain provisions protecting \xe2\x80\x9c[p]ersons \xe2\x80\xa6 placed hors de combat by \xe2\x80\xa6 detention,\xe2\x80\x9d including a prohibition on \xe2\x80\x9cthe passing of sentences \xe2\x80\xa6 without previous judgment \xe2\x80\xa6 by a regularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees \xe2\x80\xa6 recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d The D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit ruled Common Article 3 inapplicable to Hamdan because the conflict with al Qaeda is international in scope and thus not a \xe2\x80\x9cconflict not of an international character.\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d That reasoning is erroneous. That the quoted phrase bears its literal meaning and is used here in contradistinction to a conflict between nations is demonstrated by Common Article 2, which limits its own application to any armed conflict between signatories and provides that signatories must abide by all terms of the Conventions even if another party to the conflict is a nonsignatory, so long as the nonsignatory \xe2\x80\x9caccepts and applies\xe2\x80\x9d those terms. Common Article 3, by contrast, affords some minimal protection, falling short of full protection under the Conventions, to individuals associated with neither a signatory nor even a nonsignatory who are involved in a conflict \xe2\x80\x9cin the territory of\xe2\x80\x9d a signatory. The latter kind of conflict does not involve a clash between nations (whether signatories or not). Pp. 65\xe2\x80\x9368.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(iii)\xc2\xa0While Common Article 3 does not define its \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x9d phrase, other sources define the words to mean an \xe2\x80\x9cordinary military cour[t]\xe2\x80\x9d that is \xe2\x80\x9cestablished and organized in accordance with the laws and procedures already in force in a country.\xe2\x80\x9d The regular military courts in our system are the courts-martial established by congressional statute. At a minimum, a military commission can be \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d only if some practical need explains deviations from court-martial practice. No such need has been demonstrated here. Pp. 69\xe2\x80\x9370.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(iv)\xc2\xa0Common Article 3\xe2\x80\x99s requirements are general, crafted to accommodate a wide variety of legal systems, but they are requirements nonetheless. The commission convened to try Hamdan does not meet those requirements. P. 72.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(d)\xc2\xa0Even assuming that Hamden is a dangerous individual who would cause great harm or death to innocent civilians given the opportunity, the Executive nevertheless must comply with the prevailing rule of law in undertaking to try him and subject him to criminal punishment. P. 72.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Stevens, joined by Justice Souter, Justice Ginsburg, and Justice Breyer, concluded in Parts V and VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93iv:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa01.\xc2\xa0The Government has not charged Hamdan with an \xe2\x80\x9coffense \xe2\x80\xa6 that by the law of war may be tried by military commission,\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821. Of the three sorts of military commissions used historically, the law-of-war type used in Quirin and other cases is the only model available to try Hamdan. Among the preconditions, incorporated in Article of War 15 and, later, UCMJ Art. 21, for such a tribunal\xe2\x80\x99s exercise of jurisdiction are, inter alia, that it must be limited to trying offenses committed within the convening commander\xe2\x80\x99s field of command, i.e., within the theater of war, and that the offense charged must have been committed during, not before or after, the war. Here, Hamdan is not alleged to have committed any overt act in a theater of war or on any specified date after September 11, 2001. More importantly, the offense alleged is not triable by law-of-war military commission. Although the common law of war may render triable by military commission certain offenses not defined by statute, Quirin, 317 U. S., at 30, the precedent for doing so with respect to a particular offense must be plain and unambiguous, cf., e.g., Loving v. United States, 517 U. S. 748, 771. That burden is far from satisfied here. The crime of \xe2\x80\x9cconspiracy\xe2\x80\x9d has rarely if ever been tried as such in this country by any law-of-war military commission not exercising some other form of jurisdiction, and does not appear in either the Geneva Conventions or the Hague Conventions\xe2\x80\x94the major treaties on the law of war. Moreover, that conspiracy is not a recognized violation of the law of war is confirmed by other international sources, including, e.g., the International Military Tribunal at Nuremberg, which pointedly refused to recognize conspiracy to commit war crimes as such a violation. Because the conspiracy charge does not support the commission\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction, the commission lacks authority to try Hamdan. Pp. 30\xe2\x80\x9349.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa02.\xc2\xa0The phrase \xe2\x80\x9call the guarantees \xe2\x80\xa6 recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples\xe2\x80\x9d in Common Article 3 of the Geneva Conventions is not defined, but it must be understood to incorporate at least the barest of the trial protections recognized by customary international law. The procedures adopted to try Hamdan deviate from those governing courts-martial in ways not justified by practical need, and thus fail to afford the requisite guarantees. Moreover, various provisions of Commission Order No. 1 dispense with the principles, which are indisputably part of customary international law, that an accused must, absent disruptive conduct or consent, be present for his trial and must be privy to the evidence against him. Pp. 70\xe2\x80\x9372.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy, agreeing that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission is unauthorized under the Uniform Code of Military Justice, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7836 and 821, and the Geneva Conventions, concluded that there is therefore no need to decide whether Common Article 3 of the Conventions requires that the accused have the right to be present at all stages of a criminal trial or to address the validity of the conspiracy charge against Hamdan. Pp.\xc2\xa017\xe2\x80\x9319.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Stevens, J., announced the judgment of the Court and delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Parts I through IV, VI through VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93iii, VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93v, and VII, in which Kennedy, Souter, Ginsburg, and Breyer, JJ., joined, and an opinion with respect to Parts V and VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93iv, in which Souter, Ginsburg, and Breyer, JJ., joined. Breyer, J., filed a concurring opinion, in which Kennedy, Souter, and Ginsburg, JJ., joined. Kennedy, J., filed an opinion concurring in part, in which Souter, Ginsburg, and Breyer, JJ., joined as to Parts I and II. Scalia, J., filed a dissenting opinion, in which Thomas and Alito, JJ., joined. Thomas, J., filed a dissenting opinion, in which Scalia, J., joined, and in which Alito, J., joined as to all but Parts I, II\xe2\x80\x93C\xe2\x80\x931, and III\xe2\x80\x93B\xe2\x80\x932. Alito, J., filed a dissenting opinion, in which Scalia and Thomas, JJ., joined as to Parts I through III. Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J., took no part in the consideration or decision of the case.

\n
\n
\n
\n
\n\n
\nOPINION OF THE COURT
\nHAMDAN V. RUMSFELD
\n548 U. S. ____ (2006)
\n
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 05-184
\n
\n

SALIM AHMED HAMDAN, PETITIONER v. DONALD\nH. RUMSFELD, SECRETARY OF DEFENSE, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the district of columbia circuit

\n

[June 29, 2006]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Stevens announced the judgment of the Court and delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Parts I through IV, Parts VI through VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93iii, Part VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93v, and Part VII, and an opinion with respect to Parts V and VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93iv, in which Justice Souter, Justice Ginsburg, and Justice Breyer join.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Petitioner Salim Ahmed Hamdan, a Yemeni national, is in custody at an American prison in Guantanamo Bay, Cuba. In November 2001, during hostilities between the United States and the Taliban (which then governed Afghanistan), Hamdan was captured by militia forces and turned over to the U. S. military. In June 2002, he was transported to Guantanamo Bay. Over a year later, the President deemed him eligible for trial by military commission for then-unspecified crimes. After another year had passed, Hamdan was charged with one count of conspiracy \xe2\x80\x9cto commit \xe2\x80\xa6 offenses triable by military commission.\xe2\x80\x9d App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Hamdan filed petitions for writs of habeas corpus and mandamus to challenge the Executive Branch\xe2\x80\x99s intended means of prosecuting this charge. He concedes that a court-martial constituted in accordance with the Uniform Code of Military Justice (UCMJ), 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7801 et seq. (2000 ed. and Supp. III), would have authority to try him. His objection is that the military commission the President has convened lacks such authority, for two principal reasons: First, neither congressional Act nor the common law of war supports trial by this commission for the crime of conspiracy\xe2\x80\x94an offense that, Hamdan says, is not a violation of the law of war. Second, Hamdan contends, the procedures that the President has adopted to try him violate the most basic tenets of military and international law, including the principle that a defendant must be permitted to see and hear the evidence against him.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The District Court granted Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s request for a writ of habeas corpus. 344 F. Supp. 2d 152 (DC 2004). The Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit reversed. 415 F.\xc2\xa03d 33 (2005). Recognizing, as we did over a half-century ago, that trial by military commission is an extraordinary measure raising important questions about the balance of powers in our constitutional structure, Ex\xc2\xa0parte Quirin, 317 U. S. 1, 19 (1942), we granted certiorari. 546 U. S. ___ (2005).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the reasons that follow, we conclude that the military commission convened to try Hamdan lacks power to proceed because its structure and procedures violate both the UCMJ and the Geneva Conventions. Four of us also conclude, see Part V, infra, that the offense with which Hamdan has been charged is not an \xe2\x80\x9coffens[e] that by \xe2\x80\xa6 the law of war may be tried by military commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0On September 11, 2001, agents of the al Qaeda terrorist organization hijacked commercial airplanes and attacked the World Trade Center in New York City and the national headquarters of the Department of Defense in Arlington, Virginia. Americans will never forget the devastation wrought by these acts. Nearly 3,000 civilians were killed.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Congress responded by adopting a Joint Resolution authorizing the President to \xe2\x80\x9cuse all necessary and appropriate force against those nations, organizations, or persons he determines planned, authorized, committed, or aided the terrorist attacks \xe2\x80\xa6 in order to prevent any future acts of international terrorism against the United States by such nations, organizations or persons.\xe2\x80\x9d Authorization for Use of Military Force (AUMF), 115 Stat. 224, note following 50 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71541 (2000 ed., Supp. III). Acting pursuant to the AUMF, and having determined that the Taliban regime had supported al Qaeda, the President ordered the Armed Forces of the United States to invade Afghanistan. In the ensuing hostilities, hundreds of individuals, Hamdan among them, were captured and eventually detained at Guantanamo Bay.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0On November 13, 2001, while the United States was still engaged in active combat with the Taliban, the President issued a comprehensive military order intended to govern the \xe2\x80\x9cDetention, Treatment, and Trial of Certain Non-Citizens in the War Against Terrorism,\xe2\x80\x9d 66 Fed. Reg. 57833 (hereinafter November 13 Order or Order). Those subject to the November 13 Order include any noncitizen for whom the President determines \xe2\x80\x9cthere is reason to believe\xe2\x80\x9d that he or she (1) \xe2\x80\x9cis or was\xe2\x80\x9d a member of al Qaeda or (2) has engaged or participated in terrorist activities aimed at or harmful to the United States. Id., at 57834. Any such individual \xe2\x80\x9cshall, when tried, be tried by military commission for any and all offenses triable by military commission that such individual is alleged to have committed, and may be punished in accordance with the penalties provided under applicable law, including imprisonment or death.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. The November 13 Order vested in the Secretary of Defense the power to appoint military commissions to try individuals subject to the Order, but that power has since been delegated to John D. Altenberg, Jr., a retired Army major general and longtime military lawyer who has been designated \xe2\x80\x9cAppointing Authority for Military Commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0On July 3, 2003, the President announced his determination that Hamdan and five other detainees at Guantanamo Bay were subject to the November 13 Order and thus triable by military commission. In December 2003, military counsel was appointed to represent Hamdan. Two months later, counsel filed demands for charges and for a speedy trial pursuant to Article 10 of the UCMJ, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7810. On February 23, 2004, the legal adviser to the Appointing Authority denied the applications, ruling that Hamdan was not entitled to any of the protections of the UCMJ. Not until July 13, 2004, after Hamdan had commenced this action in the United States District Court for the Western District of Washington, did the Government finally charge him with the offense for which, a year earlier, he had been deemed eligible for trial by military commission.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The charging document, which is unsigned, contains 13 numbered paragraphs. The first two paragraphs recite the asserted bases for the military commission\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction\xe2\x80\x94namely, the November 13 Order and the President\xe2\x80\x99s July 3, 2003, declaration that Hamdan is eligible for trial by military commission. The next nine paragraphs, collectively entitled \xe2\x80\x9cGeneral Allegations,\xe2\x80\x9d describe al Qaeda\xe2\x80\x99s activities from its inception in 1989 through 2001 and identify Osama bin Laden as the group\xe2\x80\x99s leader. Hamdan is not mentioned in these paragraphs.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Only the final two paragraphs, entitled \xe2\x80\x9cCharge: Conspiracy,\xe2\x80\x9d contain allegations against Hamdan. Paragraph 12 charges that \xe2\x80\x9cfrom on or about February 1996 to on or about November 24, 2001,\xe2\x80\x9d Hamdan \xe2\x80\x9cwillfully and knowingly joined an enterprise of persons who shared a common criminal purpose and conspired and agreed with [named members of al Qaeda] to commit the following offenses triable by military commission: attacking civilians; attacking civilian objects; murder by an unprivileged belligerent; and terrorism.\xe2\x80\x9d App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a. There is no allegation that Hamdan had any command responsibilities, played a leadership role, or participated in the planning of any activity.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Paragraph 13 lists four \xe2\x80\x9covert acts\xe2\x80\x9d that Hamdan is alleged to have committed sometime between 1996 and November 2001 in furtherance of the \xe2\x80\x9centerprise and conspiracy\xe2\x80\x9d: (1) he acted as Osama bin Laden\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cbodyguard and personal driver,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cbeliev[ing]\xe2\x80\x9d all the while that bin Laden \xe2\x80\x9cand his associates were involved in\xe2\x80\x9d terrorist acts prior to and including the attacks of September 11, 2001; (2) he arranged for transportation of, and actually transported, weapons used by al Qaeda members and by bin Laden\xe2\x80\x99s bodyguards (Hamdan among them); (3) he \xe2\x80\x9cdrove or accompanied [O]sama bin Laden to various al Qaida-sponsored training camps, press conferences, or lectures,\xe2\x80\x9d at which bin Laden encouraged attacks against Americans; and (4) he received weapons training at al Qaeda-sponsored camps. Id., at 65a\xe2\x80\x9367a.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0After this formal charge was filed, the United States District Court for the Western District of Washington transferred Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s habeas and mandamus petitions to the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. Meanwhile, a Combatant Status Review Tribunal (CSRT) convened pursuant to a military order issued on July 7, 2004, decided that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s continued detention at Guantanamo Bay was warranted because he was an \xe2\x80\x9cenemy combatant.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 1] Separately, proceedings before the military commission commenced.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0On November 8, 2004, however, the District Court granted Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s petition for habeas corpus and stayed the commission\xe2\x80\x99s proceedings. It concluded that the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority to establish military commissions extends only to \xe2\x80\x9coffenders or offenses triable by military [commission] under the law of war,\xe2\x80\x9d 344 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 158; that the law of war includes the Geneva Convention (III) Relative to the Treatment of Prisoners of War, Aug. 12, 1949, [1955] 6 U. S. T. 3316, T. I. A. S. No. 3364 (Third Geneva Convention); that Hamdan is entitled to the full protections of the Third Geneva Convention until adjudged, in compliance with that treaty, not to be a prisoner of war; and that, whether or not Hamdan is properly classified as a prisoner of war, the military commission convened to try him was established in violation of both the UCMJ and Common Article 3 of the Third Geneva Convention because it had the power to convict based on evidence the accused would never see or hear. 344 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 158\xe2\x80\x93172.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court of Appeals for the District of\xc2\xa0Columbia Circuit reversed. Like the District Court, the Court of Appeals declined the Government\xe2\x80\x99s invitation to abstain from considering Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s challenge. Cf. Schlesinger v. Councilman, 420 U. S. 738 (1975). On the merits, the panel rejected the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s further conclusion that Hamdan was entitled to relief under the Third Geneva Convention. All three judges agreed that the Geneva Conventions were not \xe2\x80\x9cjudicially enforceable,\xe2\x80\x9d 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 38, and two thought that the Conventions did not in any event apply to Hamdan, id., at 40\xe2\x80\x9342; but see id., at 44 (Williams, J., concurring). In other portions of its opinion, the court concluded that our decision in Quirin foreclosed any separation-of-powers objection to the military commission\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction, and held that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial before the contemplated commission would violate neither the UCMJ nor U. S. Armed Forces regulations intended to implement the Geneva Conventions. 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 38, 42\xe2\x80\x9343.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0On November 7, 2005, we granted certiorari to decide whether the military commission convened to try Hamdan has authority to do so, and whether Hamdan may rely on the Geneva Conventions in these proceedings.

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0On February 13, 2006, the Government filed a motion to dismiss the writ of certiorari. The ground cited for dismissal was the recently enacted Detainee Treatment Act of 2005 (DTA), Pub. L. 109\xe2\x80\x93148, 119 Stat. 2739. We postponed our ruling on that motion pending argument on the merits, 546 U. S. ___ (2006), and now deny it.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The DTA, which was signed into law on December 30, 2005, addresses a broad swath of subjects related to detainees. It places restrictions on the treatment and interrogation of detainees in U. S. custody, and it furnishes procedural protections for U. S. personnel accused of engaging in improper interrogation. DTA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71002\xe2\x80\x931004, 119 Stat. 2739\xe2\x80\x932740. It also sets forth certain \xe2\x80\x9cprocedures for status review of detainees outside the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005, id., at 2740. Subsections (a) through (d) of \xc2\xa71005 direct the Secretary of Defense to report to Congress the procedures being used by CSRTs to determine the proper classification of detainees held in Guantanamo Bay, Iraq, and Afghanistan, and to adopt certain safeguards as part of those procedures.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Subsection (e) of \xc2\xa71005, which is entitled \xe2\x80\x9cJudicial Review of Detention of Enemy Combatants,\xe2\x80\x9d supplies the basis for the Government\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdictional argument. The subsection contains three numbered paragraphs. The first paragraph amends the judicial code as follows:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(1)\xe2\x80\x82In general.\xe2\x80\x94Section 2241 of title 28, United States Code, is amended by adding at the end the following:

\n

.\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83.\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83.\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83.\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98(e)\xe2\x80\x82Except as provided in section 1005 of the Detainee Treatment Act of 2005, no court, justice, or judge shall have jurisdiction to hear or consider\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98(1)\xe2\x80\x82an application for a writ of habeas corpus filed by or on behalf of an alien detained by the Department of Defense at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba; or

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98(2)\xe2\x80\x82any other action against the United States or its agents relating to any aspect of the detention by the Department of Defense of an alien at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba, who\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98(A)\xe2\x80\x82is currently in military custody; or

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98(B)\xe2\x80\x82has been determined by the United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit in accordance with the procedures set forth in section 1005(e) of the Detainee Treatment Act of 2005 to have been properly detained as an enemy combatant.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005(e), id., at 2741\xe2\x80\x932742.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Paragraph (2) of subsection (e) vests in the Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit the \xe2\x80\x9cexclusive jurisdiction to determine the validity of any final decision of a [CSRT] that an alien is properly designated as an enemy combatant.\xe2\x80\x9d Paragraph (2) also delimits the scope of that review. See \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71005(e)(2)(C)(i)\xe2\x80\x93(ii), id., at 2742.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Paragraph (3) mirrors paragraph (2) in structure, but governs judicial review of final decisions of military commissions, not CSRTs. It vests in the Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit \xe2\x80\x9cexclusive jurisdiction to determine the validity of any final decision rendered pursuant to Military Commission Order No. 1, dated August 31, 2005 (or any successor military order).\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(A), id., at 2743.[Footnote 2] Review is as of right for any alien sentenced to death or a term of imprisonment of 10 years or more, but is at the Court of Appeals\xe2\x80\x99 discretion in all other cases. The scope of review is limited to the following inquiries:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(i)\xe2\x80\x82whether the final decision [of the military commission] was consistent with the standards and procedures specified in the military order referred to in subparagraph (A); and

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(ii)\xe2\x80\x82to the extent the Constitution and laws of the United States are applicable, whether the use of such standards and procedures to reach the final decision is consistent with the Constitution and laws of the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(D), ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, \xc2\xa71005 contains an \xe2\x80\x9ceffective date\xe2\x80\x9d provision, which reads as follows:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(1)\xe2\x80\x82In general.\xe2\x80\x94This section shall take effect on the date of the enactment of this Act.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(2)\xe2\x80\x82Review of Combatant Status Tribunal and Military Commission Decisions.\xe2\x80\x94Paragraphs (2) and (3) of subsection (e) shall apply with respect to any claim whose review is governed by one of such paragraphs and that is pending on or after the date of the enactment of this Act.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005(h), id., at 2743\xe2\x80\x932744.[Footnote 3]

\n

\nThe Act is silent about whether paragraph (1) of subsection (e) \xe2\x80\x9cshall apply\xe2\x80\x9d to claims pending on the date of enactment.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government argues that \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71005(e)(1) and 1005(h) had the immediate effect, upon enactment, of repealing federal jurisdiction not just over detainee habeas actions yet to be filed but also over any such actions then pending in any federal court\xe2\x80\x94including this Court. Accordingly, it argues, we lack jurisdiction to review the Court of Appeals\xe2\x80\x99 decision below.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Hamdan objects to this theory on both constitutional and statutory grounds. Principal among his constitutional arguments is that the Government\xe2\x80\x99s preferred reading raises grave questions about Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to impinge upon this Court\xe2\x80\x99s appellate jurisdiction, particularly in habeas cases. Support for this argument is drawn from Ex parte Yerger, 8 Wall. 85 (1869), in which, having explained that \xe2\x80\x9cthe denial to this court of appellate jurisdiction\xe2\x80\x9d to consider an original writ of habeas corpus would \xe2\x80\x9cgreatly weaken the efficacy of the writ,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 102\xe2\x80\x93103, we held that Congress would not be presumed to have effected such denial absent an unmistakably clear statement to the contrary. See id., at 104\xe2\x80\x93105; see also Felker v. Turpin, 518 U. S. 651 (1996); Durousseau v. United States, 6 Cranch 307, 314 (1810) (opinion for the Court by Marshall, C.\xc2\xa0J.) (The \xe2\x80\x9cappellate powers of this court\xe2\x80\x9d are not created by statute but are \xe2\x80\x9cgiven by the constitution\xe2\x80\x9d); United States v. Klein, 13 Wall. 128 (1872). Cf. Ex parte McCardle, 7 Wall. 506, 514 (1869) (holding that Congress had validly foreclosed one avenue of appellate review where its repeal of habeas jurisdiction, reproduced in the margin,[Footnote 4] could not have been \xe2\x80\x9ca plainer instance of positive exception\xe2\x80\x9d). Hamdan also suggests that, if the Government\xe2\x80\x99s reading is correct, Congress has unconstitutionally suspended the writ of habeas corpus.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We find it unnecessary to reach either of these arguments. Ordinary principles of statutory construction suffice to rebut the Government\xe2\x80\x99s theory\xe2\x80\x94at least insofar as this case, which was pending at the time the DTA was enacted, is concerned.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government acknowledges that only paragraphs (2) and (3) of subsection (e) are expressly made applicable to pending cases, see \xc2\xa71005(h)(2), 119 Stat. 2743\xe2\x80\x932744, but argues that the omission of paragraph (1) from the scope of that express statement is of no moment. This is so, we are told, because Congress\xe2\x80\x99 failure to expressly reserve federal courts\xe2\x80\x99 jurisdiction over pending cases erects a presumption against jurisdiction, and that presumption is rebutted by neither the text nor the legislative history of the DTA.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The first part of this argument is not entirely without support in our precedents. We have in the past \xe2\x80\x9capplied intervening statutes conferring or ousting jurisdiction, whether or not jurisdiction lay when the underlying conduct occurred or when the suit was filed.\xe2\x80\x9d Landgraf v. USI Film Products, 511 U. S. 244, 274 (1994) (citing Bruner v. United States, 343 U. S. 112 (1952); Hallowell v. Commons, 239 U. S. 506 (1916)); see Republic of Austria v. Altmann, 541 U. S. 677, 693 (2004). But the \xe2\x80\x9cpresumption\xe2\x80\x9d that these cases have applied is more accurately viewed as the nonapplication of another presumption\xe2\x80\x94viz., the presumption against retroactivity\xe2\x80\x94in certain limited circumstances.[Footnote 5] If a statutory provision \xe2\x80\x9cwould operate retroactively\xe2\x80\x9d as applied to cases pending at the time the provision was enacted, then \xe2\x80\x9cour traditional presumption teaches that it does not govern absent clear congressional intent favoring such a result.\xe2\x80\x9d Landgraf, 511 U. S., at 280. We have explained, however, that, unlike other intervening changes in the law, a jurisdiction-conferring or jurisdiction&nbhyph;stripping statute usually \xe2\x80\x9ctakes away no substantive right but simply changes the tribunal that is to hear the case.\xe2\x80\x9d Hallowell, 239 U. S., at 508. If that is truly all the statute does, no retroactivity problem arises because the change in the law does not \xe2\x80\x9cimpair rights a party possessed when he acted, increase a party\xe2\x80\x99s liability for past conduct, or impose new duties with respect to transactions already completed.\xe2\x80\x9d Landgraf, 511 U. S., at 280.[Footnote 6] And if a new rule has no retroactive effect, the presumption against retroactivity will not prevent its application to a case that was already pending when the new rule was enacted.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0That does not mean, however, that all jurisdiction-stripping provisions\xe2\x80\x94or even all such provisions that truly lack retroactive effect\xe2\x80\x94must apply to cases pending at the time of their enactment.[Footnote 7] \xe2\x80\x9c[N]ormal rules of construction,\xe2\x80\x9d including a contextual reading of the statutory language, may dictate otherwise. Lindh v. Murphy, 521 U. S. 320, 326 (1997).[Footnote 8] A familiar principle of statutory construction, relevant both in Lindh and here, is that a negative inference may be drawn from the exclusion of language from one statutory provision that is included in other provisions of the same statute. See id., at 330; see also, e.g., Russello v. United States, 464 U. S. 16, 23 (1983) (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[W]here Congress includes particular language in one section of a statute but omits it in another section of the same Act, it is generally presumed that Congress acts intentionally and purposely in the disparate inclusion or exclusion\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d). The Court in Lindh relied on this reasoning to conclude that certain limitations on the availability of habeas relief imposed by AEDPA applied only to cases filed after that statute\xe2\x80\x99s effective date. Congress\xe2\x80\x99 failure to identify the temporal reach of those limitations, which governed noncapital cases, stood in contrast to its express command in the same legislation that new rules governing habeas petitions in capital cases \xe2\x80\x9capply to cases pending on or after the date of enactment.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7107(c), 110 Stat. 1226; see Lindh, 521 U. S., at 329\xe2\x80\x93330. That contrast, combined with the fact that the amendments at issue \xe2\x80\x9caffect[ed] substantive entitlement to relief,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 327, warranted drawing a negative inference.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0A like inference follows a fortiori from Lindh in this case. \xe2\x80\x9cIf \xe2\x80\xa6 Congress was reasonably concerned to ensure that [\xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71005(e)(2) and (3)] be applied to pending cases, it should have been just as concerned about [\xc2\xa71005(e)(1)], unless it had the different intent that the latter [section] not be applied to the general run of pending cases.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 329. If anything, the evidence of deliberate omission is stronger here than it was in Lindh. In Lindh, the provisions to be contrasted had been drafted separately but were later \xe2\x80\x9cjoined together and \xe2\x80\xa6 considered simultaneously when the language raising the implication was inserted.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 330. We observed that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 tandem review and approval of the two sets of provisions strengthened the presumption that the relevant omission was deliberate. Id., at 331; see also Field v. Mans, 516 U. S. 59, 75 (1995) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe more apparently deliberate the contrast, the stronger the inference, as applied, for example, to contrasting statutory sections originally enacted simultaneously in relevant respects\xe2\x80\x9d). Here, Congress not only considered the respective temporal reaches of paragraphs (1), (2), and (3) of subsection (e) together at every stage, but omitted paragraph (1) from its directive that paragraphs (2) and (3) apply to pending cases only after having rejected earlier proposed versions of the statute that would have included what is now paragraph (1) within the scope of that directive. Compare DTA \xc2\xa71005(h)(2), 119 Stat. 2743\xe2\x80\x932744, with 151 Cong. Rec. S12655 (Nov. 10, 2005) (S. Amdt. 2515); see id., at S14257\xe2\x80\x93S14258 (Dec. 21, 2005) (discussing similar language proposed in both the House and the Senate).[Footnote 9] Congress\xe2\x80\x99 rejection of the very language that would have achieved the result the Government urges here weighs heavily against the Government\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation. See Doe v. Chao, 540 U. S. 614, 621\xe2\x80\x93623 (2004).[Footnote 10]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government nonetheless offers two reasons why, in its view, no negative inference may be drawn in favor of jurisdiction. First, it asserts that Lindh is inapposite because \xe2\x80\x9cSection 1005(e)(1) and (h)(1) remove jurisdiction, while Section 1005(e)(2), (3) and (h)(2) create an exclusive review mechanism and define the nature of that review.\xe2\x80\x9d Reply Brief in Support of Respondents\xe2\x80\x99 Motion to Dismiss 4. Because the provisions being contrasted \xe2\x80\x9caddress wholly distinct subject matters,\xe2\x80\x9d Martin v. Hadix, 527 U. S. 343, 356 (1999), the Government argues, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 different treatment of them is of no significance.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This argument must fail because it rests on a false distinction between the \xe2\x80\x9cjurisdictional\xe2\x80\x9d nature of subsection (e)(1) and the \xe2\x80\x9cprocedural\xe2\x80\x9d character of subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3). In truth, all three provisions govern jurisdiction over detainees\xe2\x80\x99 claims; subsection (e)(1) addresses jurisdiction in habeas cases and other actions \xe2\x80\x9crelating to any aspect of the detention,\xe2\x80\x9d while subsections (e)(2) and (3) vest exclusive,[Footnote 11] but limited, jurisdiction in the Court of Appeals for the District of\xc2\xa0Columbia Circuit to review \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision[s]\xe2\x80\x9d of CSRTs and military commissions.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0That subsection (e)(1) strips jurisdiction while subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) restore it in limited form is hardly a distinction upon which a negative inference must founder. Justice Scalia, in arguing to the contrary, maintains that Congress had \xe2\x80\x9cample reason\xe2\x80\x9d to provide explicitly for application of subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) to pending cases because \xe2\x80\x9cjurisdiction-stripping\xe2\x80\x9d provisions like subsection (e)(1) have been treated differently under our retroactivity jurisprudence than \xe2\x80\x9cjurisdiction-conferring\xe2\x80\x9d ones like subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3). Post, at 8 (dissenting opinion); see also Reply Brief in Support of Respondents\xe2\x80\x99 Motion to Dismiss 5\xe2\x80\x936. That theory is insupportable. Assuming arguendo that subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) \xe2\x80\x9cconfer new jurisdiction (in the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit) where there was none before,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 8 (emphasis in original); but see Rasul v. Bush, 542 U. S. 466 (2004), and that our precedents can be read to \xe2\x80\x9cstrongly indicat[e]\xe2\x80\x9d that jurisdiction-creating statutes raise special retroactivity concerns not also raised by jurisdiction-stripping statutes, post, at 8,[Footnote 12] subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) \xe2\x80\x9cconfer\xe2\x80\x9d jurisdiction in a manner that cannot conceivably give rise to retroactivity questions under our precedents. The provisions impose no additional liability or obligation on any private party or even on the United States, unless one counts the burden of litigating an appeal\xe2\x80\x94a burden not a single one of our cases suggests triggers retroactivity concerns.[Footnote 13] Moreover, it strains credulity to suggest that the desire to reinforce the application of subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) to pending cases drove Congress to exclude subsection (e)(1) from \xc2\xa71005(h)(2).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government\xe2\x80\x99s second objection is that applying subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) but not (e)(1) to pending cases \xe2\x80\x9cproduces an absurd result\xe2\x80\x9d because it grants (albeit only temporarily) dual jurisdiction over detainees\xe2\x80\x99 cases in circumstances where the statute plainly envisions that the District of Columbia Circuit will have \xe2\x80\x9cexclusive\xe2\x80\x9d and immediate jurisdiction over such cases. Reply Brief in Support of Respondents\xe2\x80\x99 Motion to Dismiss 7. But the premise here is faulty; subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) grant jurisdiction only over actions to \xe2\x80\x9cdetermine the validity of any final decision\xe2\x80\x9d of a CSRT or commission. Because Hamdan, at least, is not contesting any \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision\xe2\x80\x9d of a CSRT or military commission, his action does not fall within the scope of subsection (e)(2) or (e)(3). There is, then, no absurdity.[Footnote 14]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government\xe2\x80\x99s more general suggestion that Congress can have had no good reason for preserving habeas jurisdiction over cases that had been brought by detainees prior to enactment of the DTA not only is belied by the legislative history, see n. 10, supra, but is otherwise without merit. There is nothing absurd about a scheme under which pending habeas actions\xe2\x80\x94particularly those, like this one, that challenge the very legitimacy of the tribunals whose judgments Congress would like to have reviewed\xe2\x80\x94are preserved, and more routine challenges to final decisions rendered by those tribunals are carefully channeled to a particular court and through a particular lens of review.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, we cannot leave unaddressed Justice Scalia\xe2\x80\x99s contentions that the \xe2\x80\x9cmeaning of \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) is entirely clear,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 6, and that \xe2\x80\x9cthe plain import of a statute repealing jurisdiction is to eliminate the power to consider and render judgment\xe2\x80\x94in an already pending case no less than in a case yet to be filed,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 3 (emphasis in original). Only by treating the Bruner rule as an inflexible trump (a thing it has never been, see n. 7, supra) and ignoring both the rest of \xc2\xa71005\xe2\x80\x99s text and its drafting history can one conclude as much. Congress here expressly provided that subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) applied to pending cases. It chose not to so provide\xe2\x80\x94after having been presented with the option\xe2\x80\x94for subsection (e)(1). The omission is an integral part of the statutory scheme that muddies whatever \xe2\x80\x9cplain meaning\xe2\x80\x9d may be discerned from blinkered study of subsection (e)(1) alone. The dissent\xe2\x80\x99s speculation about what Congress might have intended by the omission not only is counterfactual, cf. n. 10, supra (recounting legislative history), but rests on both a misconstruction of the DTA and an erroneous view our precedents, see supra, at 17, and n. 12.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For these reasons, we deny the Government\xe2\x80\x99s motion to dismiss.[Footnote 15]

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Relying on our decision in Councilman, 420 U. S. 738, the Government argues that, even if we have statutory jurisdiction, we should apply the \xe2\x80\x9cjudge-made rule that civilian courts should await the final outcome of on-going military proceedings before entertaining an attack on those proceedings.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Respondents 12. Like the District Court and the Court of Appeals before us, we reject this argument.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In Councilman, an army officer on active duty was referred to a court-martial for trial on charges that he violated the UCMJ by selling, transferring, and possessing marijuana. 420 U. S., at 739\xe2\x80\x93740. Objecting that the alleged offenses were not \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98service connected,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 740, the officer filed suit in Federal District Court to enjoin the proceedings. He neither questioned the lawfulness of courts-martial or their procedures nor disputed that, as a serviceman, he was subject to court-martial jurisdiction. His sole argument was that the subject matter of his case did not fall within the scope of court-martial authority. See id., at 741, 759. The District Court granted his request for injunctive relief, and the Court of Appeals affirmed.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We granted certiorari and reversed. Id., at 761. We did not reach the merits of whether the marijuana charges were sufficiently \xe2\x80\x9cservice connected\xe2\x80\x9d to place them within the subject-matter jurisdiction of a court-martial. Instead, we concluded that, as a matter of comity, federal courts should normally abstain from intervening in pending court-martial proceedings against members of the Armed Forces,[Footnote 16] and further that there was nothing in the particular circumstances of the officer\xe2\x80\x99s case to displace that general rule. See id., at 740, 758.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Councilman identifies two considerations of comity that together favor abstention pending completion of ongoing court-martial proceedings against service personnel. See New v. Cohen, 129 F.\xc2\xa03d 639, 643 (CADC 1997); see also 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 36\xe2\x80\x9337 (discussing Councilman and New). First, military discipline and, therefore, the efficient operation of the Armed Forces are best served if the military justice system acts without regular interference from civilian courts. See Councilman, 420 U. S., at 752. Second, federal courts should respect the balance that Congress struck between military preparedness and fairness to individual service members when it created \xe2\x80\x9can integrated system of military courts and review procedures, a critical element of which is the Court of Military Appeals, consisting of civilian judges \xe2\x80\x98completely removed from all military influence or persuasion\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 758 (quoting H.\xc2\xa0R. Rep. No. 491, 81st Cong., 1st Sess., p. 7 (1949)). Just as abstention in the face of ongoing state criminal proceedings is justified by our expectation that state courts will enforce federal rights, so abstention in the face of ongoing court-martial proceedings is justified by our expectation that the military court system established by Congress\xe2\x80\x94with its substantial procedural protections and provision for appellate review by independent civilian judges\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwill vindicate servicemen\xe2\x80\x99s constitutional rights,\xe2\x80\x9d 420 U. S., at 758. See id., at 755\xe2\x80\x93758.[Footnote 17]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The same cannot be said here; indeed, neither of the comity considerations identified in Councilman weighs in favor of abstention in this case. First, Hamdan is not a member of our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s Armed Forces, so concerns about military discipline do not apply. Second, the tribunal convened to try Hamdan is not part of the integrated system of military courts, complete with independent review panels, that Congress has established. Unlike the officer in Councilman, Hamdan has no right to appeal any conviction to the civilian judges of the Court of Military Appeals (now called the United States Court of Appeals for the Armed Forces, see Pub. L. 103\xe2\x80\x93337, 108 Stat. 2831). Instead, under Dept. of Defense Military Commission Order No. 1 (Commission Order No. 1), which was issued by the President on March 21, 2002, and amended most recently on August 31, 2005, and which governs the procedures for Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission, any conviction would be reviewed by a panel consisting of three military officers designated by the Secretary of Defense. Commission Order No. 1 \xc2\xa76(H)(4). Commission Order No. 1 provides that appeal of a review panel\xe2\x80\x99s decision may be had only to the Secretary of Defense himself, \xc2\xa76(H)(5), and then, finally, to the President, \xc2\xa76(H)(6).[Footnote 18]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We have no doubt that the various individuals assigned review power under Commission Order No. 1 would strive to act impartially and ensure that Hamdan receive all protections to which he is entitled. Nonetheless, these review bodies clearly lack the structural insulation from military influence that characterizes the Court of Appeals for the Armed Forces, and thus bear insufficient conceptual similarity to state courts to warrant invocation of abstention principles.[Footnote 19]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, neither of the two comity considerations underlying our decision to abstain in Councilman applies to the circumstances of this case. Instead, this Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision in Quirin is the most relevant precedent. In Quirin, seven German saboteurs were captured upon arrival by submarine in New York and Florida. 317 U. S., at 21. The President convened a military commission to try the saboteurs, who then filed habeas corpus petitions in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia challenging their trial by commission. We granted the saboteurs\xe2\x80\x99 petition for certiorari to the Court of Appeals before judgment. See id., at 19. Far from abstaining pending the conclusion of military proceedings, which were ongoing, we convened a special Term to hear the case and expedited our review. That course of action was warranted, we explained, \xe2\x80\x9c[i]n view of the public importance of the questions raised by [the cases] and of the duty which rests on the courts, in time of war as well as in time of peace, to preserve unimpaired the constitutional safeguards of civil liberty, and because in our opinion the public interest required that we consider and decide those questions without any avoidable delay.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As the Court of Appeals here recognized, Quirin \xe2\x80\x9cprovides a compelling historical precedent for the power of civilian courts to entertain challenges that seek to interrupt the processes of military commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 36.[Footnote 20] The circumstances of this case, like those in Quirin, simply do not implicate the \xe2\x80\x9cobligations of comity\xe2\x80\x9d that, under appropriate circumstances, justify abstention. Quackenbush v. Allstate Ins. Co., 517 U. S. 706, 733 (1996) (Kennedy, J., concurring).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, the Government has identified no other \xe2\x80\x9cimportant countervailing interest\xe2\x80\x9d that would permit federal courts to depart from their general \xe2\x80\x9cduty to exercise the jurisdiction that is conferred upon them by Congress.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 716 (majority opinion). To the contrary, Hamdan and the Government both have a compelling interest in knowing in advance whether Hamdan may be tried by a military commission that arguably is without any basis in law and operates free from many of the procedural rules prescribed by Congress for courts-martial\xe2\x80\x94rules intended to safeguard the accused and ensure the reliability of any conviction. While we certainly do not foreclose the possibility that abstention may be appropriate in some cases seeking review of ongoing military commission proceedings (such as military commissions convened on the battlefield), the foregoing discussion makes clear that, under our precedent, abstention is not justified here. We therefore proceed to consider the merits of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s challenge.

\n

IV

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The military commission, a tribunal neither mentioned in the Constitution nor created by statute, was born of military necessity. See W. Winthrop, Military Law and Precedents 831 (rev. 2d ed. 1920) (hereinafter Winthrop). Though foreshadowed in some respects by earlier tribunals like the Board of General Officers that General Washington convened to try British Major John Andr\xc3\xa9 for spying during the Revolutionary War, the commission \xe2\x80\x9cas such\xe2\x80\x9d was inaugurated in 1847. Id., at 832; G. Davis, A Treatise on the Military Law of the United States 308 (2d ed. 1909) (hereinafter Davis). As commander of occupied Mexican territory, and having available to him no other tribunal, General Winfield Scott that year ordered the establishment of both \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98military commissions\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to try ordinary crimes committed in the occupied territory and a \xe2\x80\x9ccouncil of war\xe2\x80\x9d to try offenses against the law of war. Winthrop 832 (emphases in original).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0When the exigencies of war next gave rise to a need for use of military commissions, during the Civil War, the dual system favored by General Scott was not adopted. Instead, a single tribunal often took jurisdiction over ordinary crimes, war crimes, and breaches of military orders alike. As further discussed below, each aspect of that seemingly broad jurisdiction was in fact supported by a separate military exigency. Generally, though, the need for military commissions during this period\xe2\x80\x94as during the Mexican War\xe2\x80\x94was driven largely by the then very limited jurisdiction of courts-martial: \xe2\x80\x9cThe occasion for the military commission arises principally from the fact that the jurisdiction of the court-martial proper, in our law, is restricted by statute almost exclusively to members of the military force and to certain specific offences defined in a written code.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 831 (emphasis in original).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Exigency alone, of course, will not justify the establishment and use of penal tribunals not contemplated by Article I, \xc2\xa78 and Article\xc2\xa0III, \xc2\xa71 of the Constitution unless some other part of that document authorizes a response to the felt need. See Ex parte Milligan, 4 Wall. 2, 121 (1866) (\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly no part of the judicial power of the country was conferred on [military commissions]\xe2\x80\x9d); Ex parte Vallandigham, 1 Wall. 243, 251 (1864); see also Quirin, 317 U. S., at 25 (\xe2\x80\x9cCongress and the President, like the courts, possess no power not derived from the Constitution\xe2\x80\x9d). And that authority, if it exists, can derive only from the powers granted jointly to the President and Congress in time of war. See id., at 26\xe2\x80\x9329; In\xc2\xa0re Yamashita, 327 U. S. 1, 11 (1946).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Constitution makes the President the \xe2\x80\x9cCommander in Chief\xe2\x80\x9d of the Armed Forces, Art.\xc2\xa0II, \xc2\xa72, cl.\xc2\xa01, but vests in Congress the powers to \xe2\x80\x9cdeclare War \xe2\x80\xa6 and make Rules concerning Captures on Land and Water,\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl. 11, to \xe2\x80\x9craise and support Armies,\xe2\x80\x9d id., cl. 12, to \xe2\x80\x9cdefine and punish \xe2\x80\xa6 Offences against the Law of Nations,\xe2\x80\x9d id., cl. 10, and \xe2\x80\x9cTo make Rules for the Government and Regulation of the land and naval Forces,\xe2\x80\x9d id., cl. 14. The interplay between these powers was described by Chief Justice Chase in the seminal case of Ex parte Milligan:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cThe power to make the necessary laws is in Congress; the power to execute in the President. Both powers imply many subordinate and auxiliary powers. Each includes all authorities essential to its due exercise. But neither can the President, in war more than in peace, intrude upon the proper authority of Congress, nor Congress upon the proper authority of the President\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Congress cannot direct the conduct of campaigns, nor can the President, or any commander under him, without the sanction of Congress, institute tribunals for the trial and punishment of offences, either of soldiers or civilians, unless in cases of a controlling necessity, which justifies what it compels, or at least insures acts of indemnity from the justice of the legislature.\xe2\x80\x9d 4 Wall., at 139\xe2\x80\x93140.[Footnote 21]

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Whether Chief Justice Chase was correct in suggesting that the President may constitutionally convene military commissions \xe2\x80\x9cwithout the sanction of Congress\xe2\x80\x9d in cases of \xe2\x80\x9ccontrolling necessity\xe2\x80\x9d is a question this Court has not answered definitively, and need not answer today. For we held in Quirin that Congress had, through Article of War 15, sanctioned the use of military commissions in such circumstances. 317 U. S., at 28 (\xe2\x80\x9cBy the Articles of War, and especially Article 15, Congress has explicitly provided, so far as it may constitutionally do so, that military tribunals shall have jurisdiction to try offenders or offenses against the law of war in appropriate cases\xe2\x80\x9d). Article 21 of the UCMJ, the language of which is substantially identical to the old Article 15 and was preserved by Congress after World War II,[Footnote 22] reads as follows:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cJurisdiction of courts-martial not exclusive.

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe provisions of this code conferring jurisdiction upon courts-martial shall not be construed as depriving military commissions, provost courts, or other military tribunals of concurrent jurisdiction in respect of offenders or offenses that by statute or by the law of war may be tried by such military commissions, provost courts, or other military tribunals.\xe2\x80\x9d 64 Stat. 115.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We have no occasion to revisit Quirin\xe2\x80\x99s controversial characterization of Article of War 15 as congressional authorization for military commissions. Cf. Brief for Legal Scholars and Historians as Amici Curiae 12\xe2\x80\x9315. Contrary to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s assertion, however, even Quirin did not view the authorization as a sweeping mandate for the President to \xe2\x80\x9cinvoke military commissions when he deems them necessary.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Respondents 17. Rather, the Quirin Court recognized that Congress had simply preserved what power, under the Constitution and the common law of war, the President had had before 1916 to convene military commissions\xe2\x80\x94with the express condition that the President and those under his command comply with the law of war. See 317 U. S., at 28\xe2\x80\x9329.[Footnote 23] That much is evidenced by the Court\xe2\x80\x99s inquiry, following its conclusion that Congress had authorized military commissions, into whether the law of war had indeed been complied with in that case. See ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government would have us dispense with the inquiry that the Quirin Court undertook and find in either the AUMF or the DTA specific, overriding authorization for the very commission that has been convened to try Hamdan. Neither of these congressional Acts, however, expands the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority to convene military commissions. First, while we assume that the AUMF activated the President\xe2\x80\x99s war powers, see Hamdi v. Rumsfeld, 542 U. S. 507 (2004) (plurality opinion), and that those powers include the authority to convene military commissions in appropriate circumstances, see id., at 518; Quirin, 317 U. S., at 28\xe2\x80\x9329; see also Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 11, there is nothing in the text or legislative history of the AUMF even hinting that Congress intended to expand or alter the authorization set forth in Article 21 of the UCMJ. Cf. Yerger, 8 Wall., at 105 (\xe2\x80\x9cRepeals by implication are not favored\xe2\x80\x9d).[Footnote 24]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Likewise, the DTA cannot be read to authorize this commission. Although the DTA, unlike either Article 21 or the AUMF, was enacted after the President had convened Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission, it contains no language authorizing that tribunal or any other at Guantanamo Bay. The DTA obviously \xe2\x80\x9crecognize[s]\xe2\x80\x9d the existence of the Guantanamo Bay commissions in the weakest sense, Brief for Respondents 15, because it references some of the military orders governing them and creates limited judicial review of their \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision[s],\xe2\x80\x9d DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(3), 119 Stat. 2743. But the statute also pointedly reserves judgment on whether \xe2\x80\x9cthe Constitution and laws of the United States are applicable\xe2\x80\x9d in reviewing such decisions and whether, if they are, the \xe2\x80\x9cstandards and procedures\xe2\x80\x9d used to try Hamdan and other detainees actually violate the \xe2\x80\x9cConstitution and laws.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Together, the UCMJ, the AUMF, and the DTA at most acknowledge a general Presidential authority to convene military commissions in circumstances where justified under the \xe2\x80\x9cConstitution and laws,\xe2\x80\x9d including the law of war. Absent a more specific congressional authorization, the task of this Court is, as it was in Quirin, to decide whether Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission is so justified. It is to that inquiry we now turn.

\n

V

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The common law governing military commissions may be gleaned from past practice and what sparse legal precedent exists. Commissions historically have been used in three situations. See Bradley & Goldsmith, Congressional Authorization and the War on Terrorism, 118 Harv. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 2048, 2132\xe2\x80\x932133 (2005); Winthrop 831\xe2\x80\x93846; Hearings on H.\xc2\xa0R. 2498 before the Subcommittee of the House Committee on Armed Services, 81st Cong., 1st Sess., 975 (1949). First, they have substituted for civilian courts at times and in places where martial law has been declared. Their use in these circumstances has raised constitutional questions, see Duncan v. Kahanamoku, 327 U. S. 304 (1946); Milligan, 4 Wall., at 121\xe2\x80\x93122, but is well recognized.[Footnote 25] See Winthrop 822, 836\xe2\x80\x93839. Second, commissions have been established to try civilians \xe2\x80\x9cas part of a temporary military government over occupied enemy territory or territory regained from an enemy where civilian government cannot and does not function.\xe2\x80\x9d Duncan, 327 U. S., at 314; see Milligan, 4 Wall., at 141\xe2\x80\x93142 (Chase, C.\xc2\xa0J., concurring in judgment) (distinguishing \xe2\x80\x9cmartial law proper\xe2\x80\x9d from \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary government\xe2\x80\x9d in occupied territory). Illustrative of this second kind of commission is the one that was established, with jurisdiction to apply the German Criminal Code, in occupied Germany following the end of World War II. See Madsen v. Kinsella, 343 U. S. 341, 356 (1952).[Footnote 26]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The third type of commission, convened as an \xe2\x80\x9cincident to the conduct of war\xe2\x80\x9d when there is a need \xe2\x80\x9cto seize and subject to disciplinary measures those enemies who in their attempt to thwart or impede our military effort have violated the law of war,\xe2\x80\x9d Quirin, 317 U. S., at 28\xe2\x80\x9329, has been described as \xe2\x80\x9cutterly different\xe2\x80\x9d from the other two. Bickers, Military Commissions are Constitutionally Sound: A Response to Professors Katyal and Tribe, 34 Tex. Tech. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 899, 902 (2002\xe2\x80\x932003).[Footnote 27] Not only is its jurisdiction limited to offenses cognizable during time of war, but its role is primarily a factfinding one\xe2\x80\x94to determine, typically on the battlefield itself, whether the defendant has violated the law of war. The last time the U. S. Armed Forces

\n

used the law-of-war military commission was during World War II. In Quirin, this Court sanctioned President Roosevelt\xe2\x80\x99s use of such a tribunal to try Nazi saboteurs captured on American soil during the War. 317 U. S. 1. And in Yamashita, we held that a military commission had jurisdiction to try a Japanese commander for failing to prevent troops under his command from committing atrocities in the Philippines. 327 U. S. 1.

\n
