\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Quirin is the model the Government invokes most frequently to defend the commission convened to try Hamdan. That is both appropriate and unsurprising. Since Guantanamo Bay is neither enemy-occupied territory nor under martial law, the law-of-war commission is the only model available. At the same time, no more robust model of executive power exists; Quirin represents the high-water mark of military power to try enemy combatants for war crimes.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The classic treatise penned by Colonel William Winthrop, whom we have called \xe2\x80\x9cthe \xe2\x80\x98Blackstone of Military Law,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Reid v. Covert, 354 U. S. 1, 19, n.\xc2\xa038 (1957) (plurality opinion), describes at least four preconditions for exercise of jurisdiction by a tribunal of the type convened to try Hamdan. First, \xe2\x80\x9c[a] military commission, (except where otherwise authorized by statute), can legally assume jurisdiction only of offenses committed within the field of the command of the convening commander.\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 836. The \xe2\x80\x9cfield of command\xe2\x80\x9d in these circumstances means the \xe2\x80\x9ctheatre of war.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Second, the offense charged \xe2\x80\x9cmust have been committed within the period of the war.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 28] Id., at 837. No jurisdiction exists to try offenses \xe2\x80\x9ccommitted either before or after the war.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Third, a military commission not established pursuant to martial law or an occupation may try only \xe2\x80\x9c[i]ndividuals of the enemy\xe2\x80\x99s army who have been guilty of illegitimate warfare or other offences in violation of the laws of war\xe2\x80\x9d and members of one\xe2\x80\x99s own army \xe2\x80\x9cwho, in time of war, become chargeable with crimes or offences not cognizable, or triable, by the criminal courts or under the Articles of war.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 838. Finally, a law-of-war commission has jurisdiction to try only two kinds of offense: \xe2\x80\x9cViolations of the laws and usages of war cognizable by military tribunals only,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c[b]reaches of military orders or regulations for which offenders are not legally triable by court-martial under the Articles of war.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 839.[Footnote 29]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0All parties agree that Colonel Winthrop\xe2\x80\x99s treatise accurately describes the common law governing military commissions, and that the jurisdictional limitations he identifies were incorporated in Article of War 15 and, later, Article 21 of the UCMJ. It also is undisputed that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission lacks jurisdiction to try him unless the charge \xe2\x80\x9cproperly set[s] forth, not only the details of the act charged, but the circumstances conferring jurisdiction.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 842 (emphasis in original). The question is whether the preconditions designed to ensure that a military necessity exists to justify the use of this extraordinary tribunal have been satisfied here.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The charge against Hamdan, described in detail in Part I, supra, alleges a conspiracy extending over a number of years, from 1996 to November 2001.[Footnote 30] All but two months of that more than 5-year-long period preceded the attacks of September 11, 2001, and the enactment of the AUMF\xe2\x80\x94the Act of Congress on which the Government relies for exercise of its war powers and thus for its authority to convene military commissions.[Footnote 31] Neither the purported agreement with Osama bin Laden and others to commit war crimes, nor a single overt act, is alleged to have occurred in a theater of war or on any specified date after September 11, 2001. None of the overt acts that Hamdan is alleged to have committed violates the law of war.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These facts alone cast doubt on the legality of the charge and, hence, the commission; as Winthrop makes plain, the offense alleged must have been committed both in a theater of war and during, not before, the relevant conflict. But the deficiencies in the time and place allegations also underscore\xe2\x80\x94indeed are symptomatic of\xe2\x80\x94the most serious defect of this charge: The offense it alleges is not triable by law-of-war military commission. See Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 13 (\xe2\x80\x9cNeither congressional action nor the military orders constituting the commission authorized it to place petitioner on trial unless the charge proffered against him is of a violation of the law of war\xe2\x80\x9d).[Footnote 32]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There is no suggestion that Congress has, in exercise of its constitutional authority to \xe2\x80\x9cdefine and punish . . . Offences against the Law of Nations,\xe2\x80\x9d U. S. Const., Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa010, positively identified \xe2\x80\x9cconspiracy\xe2\x80\x9d as a war crime.[Footnote 33] As we explained in Quirin, that is not necessarily fatal to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s claim of authority to try the alleged offense by military commission; Congress, through Article 21 of the UCMJ, has \xe2\x80\x9cincorporated by reference\xe2\x80\x9d the common law of war, which may render triable by military commission certain offenses not defined by statute. 317 U. S., at 30. When, however, neither the elements of the offense nor the range of permissible punishments is defined by statute or treaty, the precedent must be plain and unambiguous. To demand any less would be to risk concentrating in military hands a degree of adjudicative and punitive power in excess of that contemplated either by statute or by the Constitution. Cf. Loving v. United States, 517 U. S. 748, 771 (1996) (acknowledging that Congress \xe2\x80\x9cmay not delegate the power to make laws\xe2\x80\x9d); Reid, 354 U. S., at 23\xe2\x80\x9324 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe Founders envisioned the army as a necessary institution, but one dangerous to liberty if not confined within its essential bounds\xe2\x80\x9d); The Federalist No. 47, p. 324 (J. Cooke ed. 1961) (J. Madison) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe accumulation of all powers legislative, executive and judiciary in the same hands \xe2\x80\xa6 may justly be pronounced the very definition of tyranny\xe2\x80\x9d).[Footnote 34]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This high standard was met in Quirin; the violation there alleged was, by \xe2\x80\x9cuniversal agreement and practice\xe2\x80\x9d both in this country and internationally, recognized as an offense against the law of war. 317 U. S., at 30; see id., at 35\xe2\x80\x9336 (\xe2\x80\x9cThis precept of the law of war has been so recognized in practice both here and abroad, and has so generally been accepted as valid by authorities on international law that we think it must be regarded as a rule or principle of the law of war recognized by this Government by its enactment of the Fifteenth Article of War\xe2\x80\x9d (footnote omitted)). Although the picture arguably was less clear in Yamashita, compare 327 U. S., at 16 (stating that the provisions of the Fourth Hague Convention of 1907, 36 Stat. 2306, \xe2\x80\x9cplainly\xe2\x80\x9d required the defendant to control the troops under his command), with 327 U. S., at 35 (Murphy, J., dissenting), the disagreement between the majority and the dissenters in that case concerned whether the historic and textual evidence constituted clear precedent\xe2\x80\x94not whether clear precedent was required to justify trial by law-of-war military commission.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At a minimum, the Government must make a substantial showing that the crime for which it seeks to try a defendant by military commission is acknowledged to be an offense against the law of war. That burden is far from satisfied here. The crime of \xe2\x80\x9cconspiracy\xe2\x80\x9d has rarely if ever been tried as such in this country by any law-of-war military commission not exercising some other form of jurisdiction,[Footnote 35] and does not appear in either the Geneva Conventions or the Hague Conventions\xe2\x80\x94the major treaties on the law of war.[Footnote 36] Winthrop explains that under the common law governing military commissions, it is not enough to intend to violate the law of war and commit overt acts in furtherance of that intention unless the overt acts either are themselves offenses against the law of war or constitute steps sufficiently substantial to qualify as an attempt. See Winthrop 841 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he jurisdiction of the military commission should be restricted to cases of offence consisting in overt acts, i.e., in unlawful commissions or actual attempts to commit, and not in intentions merely\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis in original)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government cites three sources that it says show otherwise. First, it points out that the Nazi saboteurs in Quirin were charged with conspiracy. See Brief for Respondents 27. Second, it observes that Winthrop at one point in his treatise identifies conspiracy as an offense \xe2\x80\x9cprosecuted by military commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (citing Winthrop 839, and n.\xc2\xa05). Finally, it notes that another military historian, Charles Roscoe Howland, lists conspiracy \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98to violate the laws of war by destroying life or property in aid of the enemy\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d as an offense that was tried as a violation of the law of war during the Civil War. Brief for Respondents 27\xe2\x80\x9328 (citing C. Howland, Digest of Opinions of the Judge Advocates General of the Army 1071 (1912) (hereinafter Howland)). On close analysis, however, these sources at best lend little support to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s position and at worst undermine it. By any measure, they fail to satisfy the high standard of clarity required to justify the use of a military commission.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0That the defendants in Quirin were charged with conspiracy is not persuasive, since the Court declined to address whether the offense actually qualified as a violation of the law of war\xe2\x80\x94let alone one triable by military commission. The Quirin defendants were charged with the following offenses:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c[I.]\xe2\x80\x82Violation of the law of war.

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c[II.]\xe2\x80\x82Violation of Article 81 of the Articles of War, defining the offense of relieving or attempting to relieve, or corresponding with or giving intelligence to, the enemy.

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c[III.]\xe2\x80\x82Violation of Article 82, defining the offense of spying.

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c[IV.]\xe2\x80\x82Conspiracy to commit the offenses alleged in charges [I, II, and III].\xe2\x80\x9d 317 U. S., at 23.

\n

\nThe Government, defending its charge, argued that the conspiracy alleged \xe2\x80\x9cconstitute[d] an additional violation of the law of war.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 15. The saboteurs disagreed; they maintained that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he charge of conspiracy can not stand if the other charges fall.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 8. The Court, however, declined to resolve the dispute. It concluded, first, that the specification supporting Charge I adequately alleged a \xe2\x80\x9cviolation of the law of war\xe2\x80\x9d that was not \xe2\x80\x9cmerely colorable or without foundation.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 36. The facts the Court deemed sufficient for this purpose were that the defendants, admitted enemy combatants, entered upon U. S. territory in time of war without uniform \xe2\x80\x9cfor the purpose of destroying property used or useful in prosecuting the war.\xe2\x80\x9d That act was \xe2\x80\x9ca hostile and warlike\xe2\x80\x9d one. Id., at 36, 37. The Court was careful in its decision to identify an overt, \xe2\x80\x9ccomplete\xe2\x80\x9d act. Responding to the argument that the saboteurs had \xe2\x80\x9cnot actually committed or attempted to commit any act of depredation or entered the theatre or zone of active military operations\xe2\x80\x9d and therefore had not violated the law of war, the Court responded that they had actually \xe2\x80\x9cpassed our military and naval lines and defenses or went behind those lines, in civilian dress and with hostile purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 38. \xe2\x80\x9cThe offense was complete when with that purpose they entered\xe2\x80\x94or, having so entered, they remained upon\xe2\x80\x94our territory in time of war without uniform or other appropriate means of identification.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Turning to the other charges alleged, the Court explained that \xe2\x80\x9c[s]ince the first specification of Charge I sets forth a violation of the law of war, we have no occasion to pass on the adequacy of the second specification of Charge I, or to construe the 81st and 82nd Articles of War for the purpose of ascertaining whether the specifications under Charges II and III allege violations of those Articles or whether if so construed they are constitutional.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 46. No mention was made at all of Charge IV\xe2\x80\x94the conspiracy charge.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If anything, Quirin supports Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s argument that conspiracy is not a violation of the law of war. Not only did the Court pointedly omit any discussion of the conspiracy charge, but its analysis of Charge I placed special emphasis on the completion of an offense; it took seriously the saboteurs\xe2\x80\x99 argument that there can be no violation of a law of war\xe2\x80\x94at least not one triable by military commission\xe2\x80\x94without the actual commission of or attempt to commit a \xe2\x80\x9chostile and warlike act.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 37\xe2\x80\x9338.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0That limitation makes eminent sense when one considers the necessity from whence this kind of military commission grew: The need to dispense swift justice, often in the form of execution, to illegal belligerents captured on the battlefield. See S.\xc2\xa0Rep. No. 130, 64th Cong., 1st Sess., p. 40 (1916) (testimony of Brig. Gen. Enoch H. Crowder) (observing that Article of War 15 preserves the power of \xe2\x80\x9cthe military commander in the field in time of war\xe2\x80\x9d to use military commissions (emphasis added)). The same urgency would not have been felt vis-\xc3\x80-vis enemies who had done little more than agree to violate the laws of war. Cf. 31 Op. Atty. Gen. 356, 357, 361 (1918) (opining that a German spy could not be tried by military commission because, having been apprehended before entering \xe2\x80\x9cany camp, fortification or other military premises of the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d he had \xe2\x80\x9ccommitted [his offenses] outside of the field of military operations\xe2\x80\x9d). The Quirin Court acknowledged as much when it described the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority to use law-of-war military commissions as the power to \xe2\x80\x9cseize and subject to disciplinary measures those enemies who in their attempt to thwart or impede our military effort have violated the law of war.\xe2\x80\x9d 317 U. S., at 28\xe2\x80\x9329 (emphasis added).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Winthrop and Howland are only superficially more helpful to the Government. Howland, granted, lists \xe2\x80\x9cconspiracy by two or more to violate the laws of war by destroying life or property in aid of the enemy\xe2\x80\x9d as one of over 20 \xe2\x80\x9coffenses against the laws and usages of war\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cpassed upon and punished by military commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d Howland 1071. But while the records of cases that Howland cites following his list of offenses against the law of war support inclusion of the other offenses mentioned, they provide no support for the inclusion of conspiracy as a violation of the law of war. See ibid. (citing Record Books of the Judge Advocate General Office, R. 2, 144; R. 3, 401, 589, 649; R. 4, 320; R. 5, 36, 590; R. 6, 20; R. 7, 413; R. 8, 529; R. 9, 149, 202, 225, 481, 524, 535; R. 10, 567; R. 11, 473, 513; R. 13, 125, 675; R. 16, 446; R. 21, 101, 280). Winthrop, apparently recognizing as much, excludes conspiracy of any kind from his own list of offenses against the law of war. See Winthrop 839\xe2\x80\x93840.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Winthrop does, unsurprisingly, include \xe2\x80\x9ccriminal conspiracies\xe2\x80\x9d in his list of \xe2\x80\x9c[c]rimes and statutory offenses cognizable by State or U. S. courts\xe2\x80\x9d and triable by martial law or military government commission. See id., at 839. And, in a footnote, he cites several Civil War examples of \xe2\x80\x9cconspiracies of this class, or of the first and second classes combined.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 839, n.\xc2\xa05 (emphasis added). The Government relies on this footnote for its contention that conspiracy was triable both as an ordinary crime (a crime of the \xe2\x80\x9cfirst class\xe2\x80\x9d) and, independently, as a war crime (a crime of the \xe2\x80\x9csecond class\xe2\x80\x9d). But the footnote will not support the weight the Government places on it.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As we have seen, the military commissions convened during the Civil War functioned at once as martial law or military government tribunals and as law-of-war commissions. See n. 27, supra. Accordingly, they regularly tried war crimes and ordinary crimes together. Indeed, as Howland observes, \xe2\x80\x9c[n]ot infrequently the crime, as charged and found, was a combination of the two species of offenses.\xe2\x80\x9d Howland 1071; see also Davis 310, n.\xc2\xa02; Winthrop 842. The example he gives is \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98murder in violation of the laws of war.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Howland 1071\xe2\x80\x931072. Winthrop\xe2\x80\x99s conspiracy \xe2\x80\x9cof the first and second classes combined\xe2\x80\x9d is, like Howland\xe2\x80\x99s example, best understood as a species of compound offense of the type tried by the hybrid military commissions of the Civil War. It is not a stand-alone offense against the law of war. Winthrop confirms this understanding later in his discussion, when he emphasizes that \xe2\x80\x9covert acts\xe2\x80\x9d constituting war crimes are the only proper subject at least of those military tribunals not convened to stand in for local courts. Winthrop 841, and nn. 22, 23 (emphasis in original) (citing W. Finlason, Martial Law 130 (1867)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas cites as evidence that conspiracy is a recognized violation of the law of war the Civil War indictment against Henry Wirz, which charged the defendant with \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[m]aliciously, willfully, and traitorously \xe2\x80\xa6 combining, confederating, and conspiring [with others] to injure the health and destroy the lives of soldiers in the military service of the United States \xe2\x80\xa6 to the end that the armies of the United States might be weakened and impaired, in violation of the laws and customs of war.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 24\xe2\x80\x9325 (dissenting opinion) (quoting H.\xc2\xa0R. Doc. No. 314, 55th Cong., 3d Sess., 785 (1865); emphasis deleted). As shown by the specification supporting that charge, however, Wirz was alleged to have personally committed a number of atrocities against his victims, including torture, injection of prisoners with poison, and use of \xe2\x80\x9cferocious and bloodthirsty dogs\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9cseize, tear, mangle, and maim the bodies and limbs\xe2\x80\x9d of prisoners, many of whom died as a result. Id., at 789\xe2\x80\x93790. Crucially, Judge Advocate General Holt determined that one of Wirz\xe2\x80\x99s alleged co-conspirators, R.\xc2\xa0B. Winder, should not be tried by military commission because there was as yet insufficient evidence of his own personal involvement in the atrocities: \xe2\x80\x9c[I]n the case of R.\xc2\xa0B. Winder, while the evidence at the trial of Wirz was deemed by the court to implicate him in the conspiracy against the lives of all Federal prisoners in rebel hands, no such specific overt acts of violation of the laws of war are as yet fixed upon him as to make it expedient to prefer formal charges and bring him to trial.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 783 (emphases added).[Footnote 37]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, international sources confirm that the crime charged here is not a recognized violation of the law of war.[Footnote 38] As observed above, see supra, at 40, none of the major treaties governing the law of war identifies conspiracy as a violation thereof. And the only \xe2\x80\x9cconspiracy\xe2\x80\x9d crimes that have been recognized by international war crimes tribunals (whose jurisdiction often extends beyond war crimes proper to crimes against humanity and crimes against the peace) are conspiracy to commit genocide and common plan to wage aggressive war, which is a crime against the peace and requires for its commission actual participation in a \xe2\x80\x9cconcrete plan to wage war.\xe2\x80\x9d 1 Trial of the Major War Criminals Before the International Military Tribunal: Nuremberg, 14 November 1945\xe2\x80\x931 October 1946, p. 225 (1947). The International Military Tribunal at Nuremberg, over the prosecution\xe2\x80\x99s objections, pointedly refused to recognize as a violation of the law of war conspiracy to commit war crimes, see, e.g., 22 id., at 469,[Footnote 39] and convicted only Hitler\xe2\x80\x99s most senior associates of conspiracy to wage aggressive war, see S. Pomorski, Conspiracy and Criminal Organization, in the Nuremberg Trial and International Law 213, 233\xe2\x80\x93235 (G. Ginsburgs & V. Kudriavtsev eds. 1990). As one prominent figure from the Nuremberg trials has explained, members of the Tribunal objected to recognition of conspiracy as a violation of the law of war on the ground that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he Anglo-American concept of conspiracy was not part of European legal systems and arguably not an element of the internationally recognized laws of war.\xe2\x80\x9d T. Taylor, Anatomy of the Nuremberg Trials: A Personal Memoir 36 (1992); see also id., at 550 (observing that Francis Biddle, who as Attorney General prosecuted the defendants in Quirin, thought the French judge had made a \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98persuasive argument that conspiracy in the truest sense is not known to international law\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d).[Footnote 40]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, the sources that the Government and Justice Thomas rely upon to show that conspiracy to violate the law of war is itself a violation of the law of war in fact demonstrate quite the opposite. Far from making the requisite substantial showing, the Government has failed even to offer a \xe2\x80\x9cmerely colorable\xe2\x80\x9d case for inclusion of conspiracy among those offenses cognizable by law-of-war military commission. Cf. Quirin, 317 U. S., at 36. Because the charge does not support the commission\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction, the commission lacks authority to try Hamdan.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The charge\xe2\x80\x99s shortcomings are not merely formal, but are indicative of a broader inability on the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s part here to satisfy the most basic precondition\xe2\x80\x94at least in the absence of specific congressional authorization\xe2\x80\x94for establishment of military commissions: military necessity. Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s tribunal was appointed not by a military commander in the field of battle, but by a retired major general stationed away from any active hostilities. Cf. Rasul v. Bush, 542 U. S., at 487 (Kennedy, J., concurring in judgment) (observing that \xe2\x80\x9cGuantanamo Bay is \xe2\x80\xa6 far removed from any hostilities\xe2\x80\x9d). Hamdan is charged not with an overt act for which he was caught redhanded in a theater of war and which military efficiency demands be tried expeditiously, but with an agreement the inception of which long predated the attacks of September 11, 2001 and the AUMF. That may well be a crime,[Footnote 41] but it is not an offense that \xe2\x80\x9cby the law of war may be tried by\xc2\xa0military commissio[n].\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821. None of the overt acts alleged to have been committed in furtherance of the agreement is itself a war crime, or even necessarily occurred during time of, or in a theater of, war. Any urgent need for imposition or execution of judgment is utterly belied by the record; Hamdan was arrested in November 2001 and he was not charged until mid-2004. These simply are not the circumstances in which, by any stretch of the historical evidence or this Court\xe2\x80\x99s precedents, a military commission established by Executive Order under the authority of Article 21 of the UCMJ may lawfully try a person and subject him to punishment.

\n

VI

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Whether or not the Government has charged Hamdan with an offense against the law of war cognizable by military commission, the commission lacks power to proceed. The UCMJ conditions the President\xe2\x80\x99s use of military commissions on compliance not only with the American common law of war, but also with the rest of the UCMJ itself, insofar as applicable, and with the \xe2\x80\x9crules and precepts of the law of nations,\xe2\x80\x9d Quirin, 317 U. S., at 28\xe2\x80\x94including, inter alia, the four Geneva Conventions signed in 1949. See Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 20\xe2\x80\x9321, 23\xe2\x80\x9324. The procedures that the Government has decreed will govern Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial by commission violate these laws.

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The commission\xe2\x80\x99s procedures are set forth in Commission Order No. 1, which was amended most recently on

\n

August 31, 2005\xe2\x80\x94after Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial had already begun. Every commission established pursuant to Commission Order No. 1 must have a presiding officer and at least three other members, all of whom must be commissioned officers. \xc2\xa74(A)(1). The presiding officer\xe2\x80\x99s job is to rule on questions of law and other evidentiary and interlocutory issues; the other members make findings and, if applicable, sentencing decisions. \xc2\xa74(A)(5). The accused is entitled to appointed military counsel and may hire civilian counsel at his own expense so long as such counsel is a U. S. citizen with security clearance \xe2\x80\x9cat the level SECRET or higher.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa74(C)(2)\xe2\x80\x93(3).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The accused also is entitled to a copy of the charge(s) against him, both in English and his own language (if different), to a presumption of innocence, and to certain other rights typically afforded criminal defendants in civilian courts and courts-martial. See \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa75(A)\xe2\x80\x93(P). These rights are subject, however, to one glaring condition: The accused and his civilian counsel may be excluded from, and precluded from ever learning what evidence was presented during, any part of the proceeding that either the Appointing Authority or the presiding officer decides to \xe2\x80\x9cclose.\xe2\x80\x9d Grounds for such closure \xe2\x80\x9cinclude the protection of information classified or classifiable \xe2\x80\xa6 ; information protected by law or rule from unauthorized disclosure; the physical safety of participants in Commission proceedings, including prospective witnesses; intelligence and law enforcement sources, methods, or activities; and other national security interests.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa76(B)(3).[Footnote 42] Appointed military defense counsel must be privy to these closed sessions, but may, at the presiding officer\xe2\x80\x99s discretion, be forbidden to reveal to his or her client what took place therein. Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Another striking feature of the rules governing Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission is that they permit the admission of any evidence that, in the opinion of the presiding officer, \xe2\x80\x9cwould have probative value to a reasonable person.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa76(D)(1). Under this test, not only is testimonial hearsay and evidence obtained through coercion fully admissible, but neither live testimony nor witnesses\xe2\x80\x99 written statements need be sworn. See \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa76(D)(2)(b), (3). Moreover, the accused and his civilian counsel may be denied access to evidence in the form of \xe2\x80\x9cprotected information\xe2\x80\x9d (which includes classified information as well as \xe2\x80\x9cinformation protected by law or rule from unauthorized disclosure\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cinformation concerning other national security interests,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa76(B)(3), 6(D)(5)(a)(v)), so long as the presiding officer concludes that the evidence is \xe2\x80\x9cprobative\xe2\x80\x9d under \xc2\xa76(D)(1) and that its admission without the accused\xe2\x80\x99s knowledge would not \xe2\x80\x9cresult in the denial of a full and fair trial.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa76(D)(5)(b).[Footnote 43] Finally, a presiding officer\xe2\x80\x99s determination that evidence \xe2\x80\x9cwould not have probative value to a reasonable person\xe2\x80\x9d may be overridden by a majority of the other commission members. \xc2\xa76(D)(1).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Once all the evidence is in, the commission members (not including the presiding officer) must vote on the accused\xe2\x80\x99s guilt. A two-thirds vote will suffice for both a verdict of guilty and for imposition of any sentence not including death (the imposition of which requires a unanimous vote). \xc2\xa76(F). Any appeal is taken to a three-member review panel composed of military officers and designated by the Secretary of Defense, only one member of which need have experience as a judge. \xc2\xa76(H)(4). The review panel is directed to \xe2\x80\x9cdisregard any variance from procedures specified in this Order or elsewhere that would not materially have affected the outcome of the trial before the Commission.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Once the panel makes its recommendation to the Secretary of Defense, the Secretary can either remand for further proceedings or forward the record to the President with his recommendation as to final disposition. \xc2\xa76(H)(5). The President then, unless he has delegated the task to the Secretary, makes the \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa76(H)(6). He may change the commission\xe2\x80\x99s findings or sentence only in a manner favorable to the accused. Ibid.

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Hamdan raises both general and particular objections to the procedures set forth in Commission Order No. 1. His general objection is that the procedures\xe2\x80\x99 admitted deviation from those governing courts-martial itself renders the commission illegal. Chief among his particular objections are that he may, under the Commission Order, be convicted based on evidence he has not seen or heard, and that any evidence admitted against him need not comply with the admissibility or relevance rules typically applicable in criminal trials and court-martial proceedings.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government objects to our consideration of any procedural challenge at this stage on the grounds that (1) the abstention doctrine espoused in Councilman, 420 U. S. 738, precludes pre-enforcement review of procedural rules, (2) Hamdan will be able to raise any such challenge following a \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision\xe2\x80\x9d under the DTA, and (3) \xe2\x80\x9cthere is \xe2\x80\xa6 no basis to presume, before the trial has even commenced, that the trial will not be conducted in good faith and according to law.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Respondents 45\xe2\x80\x9346, nn. 20\xe2\x80\x9321. The first of these contentions was disposed of in Part III, supra, and neither of the latter two is sound.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, because Hamdan apparently is not subject to the death penalty (at least as matters now stand) and may receive a sentence shorter than 10 years\xe2\x80\x99 imprisonment, he has no automatic right to review of the commission\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 44] before a federal court under the DTA. See \xc2\xa71005(e)(3), 119 Stat. 2743. Second, contrary to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s assertion, there is a \xe2\x80\x9cbasis to presume\xe2\x80\x9d that the procedures employed during Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial will violate the law: The procedures are described with particularity in Commission Order No. 1, and implementation of some of them has already occurred. One of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s complaints is that he will be, and indeed already has been, excluded from his own trial. See Reply Brief for Petitioner 12; App. to Pet. for Cert. 45a. Under these circumstances, review of the procedures in advance of a \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the timing of which is left entirely to the discretion of the President under the DTA\xe2\x80\x94is appropriate. We turn, then, to consider the merits of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s procedural challenge.

\n

C

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In part because the difference between military commissions and courts-martial originally was a difference of jurisdiction alone, and in part to protect against abuse and ensure evenhandedness under the pressures of war, the procedures governing trials by military commission historically have been the same as those governing courts-martial. See, e.g., 1 The War of the Rebellion 248 (2d series 1894) (General Order 1 issued during the Civil War required military commissions to \xe2\x80\x9cbe constituted in a similar manner and their proceedings be conducted according to the same general rules as courts-martial in order to prevent abuses which might otherwise arise\xe2\x80\x9d). Accounts of commentators from Winthrop through General Crowder\xe2\x80\x94who drafted Article of War 15 and whose views have been deemed \xe2\x80\x9cauthoritative\xe2\x80\x9d by this Court, Madsen, 343 U. S., at 353\xe2\x80\x94confirm as much.[Footnote 45] As recently as the Korean and Vietnam wars, during which use of military commissions was contemplated but never made, the principle of procedural parity was espoused as a background assumption. See Paust, Antiterrorism Military Commissions: Courting Illegality, 23 Mich. J. Int\xe2\x80\x99l L. 1, 3\xe2\x80\x935 (2001\xe2\x80\x932002).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There is a glaring historical exception to this general rule. The procedures and evidentiary rules used to try General Yamashita near the end of World War II deviated in significant respects from those then governing courts-martial. See 327 U. S. 1. The force of that precedent, however, has been seriously undermined by post-World War II developments.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Yamashita, from late 1944 until September 1945, was Commanding General of the Fourteenth Army Group of the Imperial Japanese Army, which had exercised control over the Philippine Islands. On September 3, 1945, after American forces regained control of the Philippines, Yamashita surrendered. Three weeks later, he was charged with violations of the law of war. A few weeks after that, he was arraigned before a military commission convened in the Philippines. He pleaded not guilty, and his trial lasted for two months. On December 7, 1945, Yamashita was convicted and sentenced to hang. See id., at 5; id., at 31\xe2\x80\x9334 (Murphy, J., dissenting). This Court upheld the denial of his petition for a writ of habeas corpus.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The procedures and rules of evidence employed during Yamashita\xe2\x80\x99s trial departed so far from those used in courts-martial that they generated an unusually long and vociferous critique from two Members of this Court. See id., at 41\xe2\x80\x9381 (Rutledge, J., joined by Murphy, J., dissenting).[Footnote 46] Among the dissenters\xe2\x80\x99 primary concerns was that the commission had free rein to consider all evidence \xe2\x80\x9cwhich in the commission\xe2\x80\x99s opinion \xe2\x80\x98would be of assistance in proving or disproving the charge,\xe2\x80\x99 without any of the usual modes of authentication.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 49 (Rutledge, J.).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority, however, did not pass on the merits of Yamashita\xe2\x80\x99s procedural challenges because it concluded that his status disentitled him to any protection under the Articles of War (specifically, those set forth in Article 38, which would become Article 36 of the UCMJ) or the Geneva Convention of 1929, 47 Stat. 2021 (1929 Geneva Convention). The Court explained that Yamashita was neither a \xe2\x80\x9cperson made subject to the Articles of War by Article 2\xe2\x80\x9d thereof, 327 U. S., at 20, nor a protected prisoner of war being tried for crimes committed during his detention, id., at 21.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At least partially in response to subsequent criticism of General Yamashita\xe2\x80\x99s trial, the UCMJ\xe2\x80\x99s codification of the Articles of War after World War II expanded the category of persons subject thereto to include defendants in Yamashita\xe2\x80\x99s (and Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s) position,[Footnote 47] and the Third Geneva Convention of 1949 extended prisoner-of-war protections to individuals tried for crimes committed before their capture. See 3 Int\xe2\x80\x99l Comm. of Red Cross,[Footnote 48] Commentary: Geneva Convention Relative to the Treatment of Prisoners of War 413 (1960) (hereinafter GCIII Commentary) (explaining that Article 85, which extends the Convention\xe2\x80\x99s protections to \xe2\x80\x9c[p]risoners of war prosecuted under the laws of the Detaining Power for acts committed prior to capture,\xe2\x80\x9d was adopted in response to judicial interpretations of the 1929 Convention, including this Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision in Yamashita). The most notorious exception to the principle of uniformity, then, has been stripped of its precedential value.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The uniformity principle is not an inflexible one; it does not preclude all departures from the procedures dictated for use by courts-martial. But any departure must be tailored to the exigency that necessitates it. See Winthrop 835, n. 81. That understanding is reflected in Article 36 of the UCMJ, which provides:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(a)\xe2\x80\x82The procedure, including modes of proof, in cases before courts-martial, courts of inquiry, military commissions, and other military tribunals may be prescribed by the President by regulations which shall, so far as he considers practicable, apply the principles of law and the rules of evidence generally recognized in the trial of criminal cases in the United States district courts, but which may not be contrary to or inconsistent with this chapter.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(b)\xe2\x80\x82All rules and regulations made under this article shall be uniform insofar as practicable and shall be reported to Congress.\xe2\x80\x9d 70A Stat. 50.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Article 36 places two restrictions on the President\xe2\x80\x99s power to promulgate rules of procedure for courts-martial and military commissions alike. First, no procedural rule he adopts may be \xe2\x80\x9ccontrary to or inconsistent with\xe2\x80\x9d the UCMJ\xe2\x80\x94however practical it may seem. Second, the rules adopted must be \xe2\x80\x9cuniform insofar as practicable.\xe2\x80\x9d That is, the rules applied to military commissions must be the same as those applied to courts-martial unless such uniformity proves impracticable.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Hamdan argues that Commission Order No. 1 violates both of these restrictions; he maintains that the procedures described in the Commission Order are inconsistent with the UCMJ and that the Government has offered no explanation for their deviation from the procedures governing courts-martial, which are set forth in the Manual for Courts-Martial, United States (2005 ed.) (Manual for Courts-Martial). Among the inconsistencies Hamdan identifies is that between \xc2\xa76 of the Commission Order, which permits exclusion of the accused from proceedings and denial of his access to evidence in certain circumstances, and the UCMJ\xe2\x80\x99s requirement that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]ll \xe2\x80\xa6 proceedings\xe2\x80\x9d other than votes and deliberations by courts-martial \xe2\x80\x9cshall be made a part of the record and shall be in the presence of the accused.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. A. \xc2\xa7839(c) (Supp. 2006). Hamdan also observes that the Commission Order dispenses with virtually all evidentiary rules applicable in courts-martial.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government has three responses. First, it argues, only 9 of the UCMJ\xe2\x80\x99s 158 Articles\xe2\x80\x94the ones that expressly mention \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary commissions\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 49]\xe2\x80\x94actually apply to commissions, and Commission Order No. 1 sets forth no procedure that is \xe2\x80\x9ccontrary to or inconsistent with\xe2\x80\x9d those 9 provisions. Second, the Government contends, military commissions would be of no use if the President were hamstrung by those provisions of the UCMJ that govern courts-martial. Finally, the President\xe2\x80\x99s determination that \xe2\x80\x9cthe danger to the safety of the United States and the nature of international terrorism\xe2\x80\x9d renders it impracticable \xe2\x80\x9cto apply in military commissions \xe2\x80\xa6 the principles of law and rules of evidence generally recognized in the trial of criminal cases in the United States district courts,\xe2\x80\x9d November 13 Order \xc2\xa71(f), is, in the Government\xe2\x80\x99s view, explanation enough for any deviation from court-martial procedures. See Brief for Respondents 43\xe2\x80\x9347, and n. 22.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Hamdan has the better of this argument. Without reaching the question whether any provision of Commission Order No. 1 is strictly \xe2\x80\x9ccontrary to or inconsistent with\xe2\x80\x9d other provisions of the UCMJ, we conclude that the \xe2\x80\x9cpracticability\xe2\x80\x9d determination the President has made is insufficient to justify variances from the procedures governing courts-martial. Subsection (b) of Article 36 was added after World War II, and requires a different showing of impracticability from the one required by subsection (a). Subsection (a) requires that the rules the President promulgates for courts-martial, provost courts, and military commissions alike conform to those that govern procedures in Article III courts, \xe2\x80\x9cso far as he considers practicable.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(a) (emphasis added). Subsection (b), by contrast, demands that the rules applied in courts-martial, provost courts, and military commissions\xe2\x80\x94whether or not they conform with the Federal Rules of Evidence\xe2\x80\x94be \xe2\x80\x9cuniform insofar as practicable.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7836(b) (emphasis added). Under the latter provision, then, the rules set forth in the Manual for Courts-Martial must apply to military commissions unless impracticable.[Footnote 50]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The President here has determined, pursuant to subsection (a), that it is impracticable to apply the rules and principles of law that govern \xe2\x80\x9cthe trial of criminal cases in the United States district courts,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7836(a), to Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission. We assume that complete deference is owed that determination. The President has not, however, made a similar official determination that it is impracticable to apply the rules for courts-martial.[Footnote 51] And even if subsection (b)\xe2\x80\x99s requirements may be satisfied without such an official determination, the requirements of that subsection are not satisfied here.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nothing in the record before us demonstrates that it would be impracticable to apply court-martial rules in this case. There is no suggestion, for example, of any logistical difficulty in securing properly sworn and authenticated evidence or in applying the usual principles of relevance and admissibility. Assuming arguendo that the reasons articulated in the President\xe2\x80\x99s Article 36(a) determination ought to be considered in evaluating the impracticability of applying court-martial rules, the only reason offered in support of that determination is the danger posed by international terrorism.[Footnote 52] Without for one moment underestimating that danger, it is not evident to us why it should require, in the case of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial, any variance from the rules that govern courts-martial.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The absence of any showing of impracticability is particularly disturbing when considered in light of the clear and admitted failure to apply one of the most fundamental protections afforded not just by the Manual for Courts-Martial but also by the UCMJ itself: the right to be present. See 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. A. \xc2\xa7839(c) (Supp. 2006). Whether or not that departure technically is \xe2\x80\x9ccontrary to or inconsistent with\xe2\x80\x9d the terms of the UCMJ, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(a), the jettisoning of so basic a right cannot lightly be excused as \xe2\x80\x9cpracticable.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Under the circumstances, then, the rules applicable in courts-martial must apply. Since it is undisputed that Commission Order No. 1 deviates in many significant respects from those rules, it necessarily violates Article 36(b).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government\xe2\x80\x99s objection that requiring compliance with the court-martial rules imposes an undue burden both ignores the plain meaning of Article 36(b) and misunderstands the purpose and the history of military commissions. The military commission was not born of a desire to dispense a more summary form of justice than is afforded by courts-martial; it developed, rather, as a tribunal of necessity to be employed when courts-martial lacked jurisdiction over either the accused or the subject matter. See Winthrop 831. Exigency lent the commission its legitimacy, but did not further justify the wholesale jettisoning of procedural protections. That history explains why the military commission\xe2\x80\x99s procedures typically have been the ones used by courts-martial. That the jurisdiction of the two tribunals today may sometimes overlap, see Madsen, 343 U. S., at 354, does not detract from the force of this history;[Footnote 53] Article 21 did not transform the military commission from a tribunal of true exigency into a more convenient adjudicatory tool. Article 36, confirming as much, strikes a careful balance between uniform procedure and the need to accommodate exigencies that may sometimes arise in a theater of war. That Article not having been complied with here, the rules specified for Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial are illegal.[Footnote 54]

\n

D

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The procedures adopted to try Hamdan also violate the Geneva Conventions. The Court of Appeals dismissed Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s Geneva Convention challenge on three independent grounds: (1) the Geneva Conventions are not judicially enforceable; (2) Hamdan in any event is not entitled to their protections; and (3) even if he is entitled to their protections, Councilman abstention is appropriate. Judge Williams, concurring, rejected the second ground but agreed with the majority respecting the first and the last. As we explained in Part III, supra, the abstention rule applied in Councilman, 420 U. S. 738, is not applicable here.[Footnote 55] And for the reasons that follow, we hold that neither of the other grounds the Court of Appeals gave for its decision is persuasive.

\n

i

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court of Appeals relied on Johnson v. Eisentrager, 339 U. S. 763 (1950), to hold that Hamdan could not invoke the Geneva Conventions to challenge the Government\xe2\x80\x99s plan to prosecute him in accordance with Commission Order No. 1. Eisentrager involved a challenge by 21 German nationals to their 1945 convictions for war crimes by a military tribunal convened in Nanking, China, and to their subsequent imprisonment in occupied Germany. The petitioners argued, inter alia, that the 1929 Geneva Convention rendered illegal some of the procedures employed during their trials, which they said deviated impermissibly from the procedures used by courts-martial to try American soldiers. See id., at 789. We rejected that claim on the merits because the petitioners (unlike Hamdan here) had failed to identify any prejudicial disparity \xe2\x80\x9cbetween the Commission that tried [them] and those that would try an offending soldier of the American forces of like rank,\xe2\x80\x9d and in any event could claim no protection, under the 1929 Convention, during trials for crimes that occurred before their confinement as prisoners of war. Id., at 790.[Footnote 56]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Buried in a footnote of the opinion, however, is this curious statement suggesting that the Court lacked power even to consider the merits of the Geneva Convention argument:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cWe are not holding that these prisoners have no right which the military authorities are bound to respect. The United States, by the Geneva Convention of July 27, 1929, 47 Stat. 2021, concluded with forty-six other countries, including the German Reich, an agreement upon the treatment to be accorded captives. These prisoners claim to be and are entitled to its protection. It is, however, the obvious scheme of the Agreement that responsibility for observance and enforcement of these rights is upon political and military authorities. Rights of alien enemies are vindicated under it only through protests and intervention of protecting powers as the rights of our citizens against foreign governments are vindicated only by Presidential intervention.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 789, n.\xc2\xa014.

\n

\nThe Court of Appeals, on the strength of this footnote, held that \xe2\x80\x9cthe 1949 Geneva Convention does not confer upon Hamdan a right to enforce its provisions in court.\xe2\x80\x9d 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 40.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Whatever else might be said about the Eisentrager footnote, it does not control this case. We may assume that \xe2\x80\x9cthe obvious scheme\xe2\x80\x9d of the 1949 Conventions is identical in all relevant respects to that of the 1929 Convention,[Footnote 57] and even that that scheme would, absent some other provision of law, preclude Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s invocation of the Convention\xe2\x80\x99s provisions as an independent source of law binding the Government\xe2\x80\x99s actions and furnishing petitioner with any enforceable right.[Footnote 58] For, regardless of the nature of the rights conferred on Hamdan, cf. United States v. Rauscher, 119 U. S. 407 (1886), they are, as the Government does not dispute, part of the law of war. See Hamdi, 542 U. S., at 520\xe2\x80\x93521 (plurality opinion). And compliance with the law of war is the condition upon which the authority set forth in Article 21 is granted.

\n

ii

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the Court of Appeals, acknowledgment of that condition was no bar to Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial by commission. As an alternative to its holding that Hamdan could not invoke the Geneva Conventions at all, the Court of Appeals concluded that the Conventions did not in any event apply to the armed conflict during which Hamdan was captured. The court accepted the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s assertions that Hamdan was captured in connection with the United States\xe2\x80\x99 war with al Qaeda and that that war is distinct from the war with the Taliban in Afghanistan. It further reasoned that the war with al Qaeda evades the reach of the Geneva Conventions. See 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 41\xe2\x80\x9342. We, like Judge Williams, disagree with the latter conclusion.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The conflict with al Qaeda is not, according to the Government, a conflict to which the full protections afforded detainees under the 1949 Geneva Conventions apply because Article 2 of those Conventions (which appears in all four Conventions) renders the full protections applicable only to \xe2\x80\x9call cases of declared war or of any other armed conflict which may arise between two or more of the High Contracting Parties.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S. T., at 3318.[Footnote 59] Since Hamdan was captured and detained incident to the conflict with al Qaeda and not the conflict with the Taliban, and since al Qaeda, unlike Afghanistan, is not a \xe2\x80\x9cHigh Contracting Party\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94i.e., a signatory of the Conventions, the protections of those Conventions are not, it is argued, applicable to Hamdan.[Footnote 60]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We need not decide the merits of this argument because there is at least one provision of the Geneva Conventions that applies here even if the relevant conflict is not one between signatories.[Footnote 61] Article 3, often referred to as Common Article 3 because, like Article 2, it appears in all four Geneva Conventions, provides that in a \xe2\x80\x9cconflict not of an international character occurring in the territory of one of the High Contracting Parties, each Party[Footnote 62] to the conflict shall be bound to apply, as a minimum,\xe2\x80\x9d certain provisions protecting \xe2\x80\x9c[p]ersons taking no active part in the hostilities, including members of armed forces who have laid down their arms and those placed hors de combat by \xe2\x80\xa6 detention.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 3318. One such provision prohibits \xe2\x80\x9cthe passing of sentences and the carrying out of executions without previous judgment pronounced by a regularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court of Appeals thought, and the Government asserts, that Common Article 3 does not apply to Hamdan because the conflict with al Qaeda, being \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98international in scope,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d does not qualify as a \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98conflict not of an international character.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 41. That reasoning is erroneous. The term \xe2\x80\x9cconflict not of an international character\xe2\x80\x9d is used here in contradistinction to a conflict between nations. So much is demonstrated by the \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental logic [of] the Convention\xe2\x80\x99s provisions on its application.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 44 (Williams, J., concurring). Common Article 2 provides that \xe2\x80\x9cthe present Convention shall apply to all cases of declared war or of any other armed conflict which may arise between two or more of the High Contracting Parties.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S. T., at 3318 (Art. 2, \xc2\xb61). High Contracting Parties (signatories) also must abide by all terms of the Conventions vis-\xc3\x80-vis one another even if one party to the conflict is a nonsignatory \xe2\x80\x9cPower,\xe2\x80\x9d and must so abide vis-\xc3\x80-vis the nonsignatory if \xe2\x80\x9cthe latter accepts and applies\xe2\x80\x9d those terms. Ibid. (Art. 2, \xc2\xb63). Common Article 3, by contrast, affords some minimal protection, falling short of full protection under the Conventions, to individuals associated with neither a signatory nor even a nonsignatory \xe2\x80\x9cPower\xe2\x80\x9d who are involved in a conflict \xe2\x80\x9cin the territory of\xe2\x80\x9d a signatory. The latter kind of conflict is distinguishable from the conflict described in Common Article 2 chiefly because it does not involve a clash between nations (whether signatories or not). In context, then, the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cnot of an international character\xe2\x80\x9d bears its literal meaning. See, e.g., J. Bentham, Introduction to the Principles of Morals and Legislation 6, 296 (J. Burns & H. Hart eds. 1970) (using the term \xe2\x80\x9cinternational law\xe2\x80\x9d as a \xe2\x80\x9cnew though not inexpressive appellation\xe2\x80\x9d meaning \xe2\x80\x9cbetwixt nation and nation\xe2\x80\x9d; defining \xe2\x80\x9cinternational\xe2\x80\x9d to include \xe2\x80\x9cmutual transactions between sovereigns as such\xe2\x80\x9d); Commentary on the Additional Protocols to the Geneva Conventions of 12 August 1949, p. 1351 (1987) (\xe2\x80\x9c[A] non-international armed conflict is distinct from an international armed conflict because of the legal status of the entities opposing each other\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Although the official commentaries accompanying Common Article 3 indicate that an important purpose of the provision was to furnish minimal protection to rebels involved in one kind of \xe2\x80\x9cconflict not of an international character,\xe2\x80\x9d i.e., a civil war, see GCIII Commentary 36\xe2\x80\x9337, the commentaries also make clear \xe2\x80\x9cthat the scope of the Article must be as wide as possible,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 36.[Footnote 63] In fact, limiting language that would have rendered Common Article 3 applicable \xe2\x80\x9cespecially [to] cases of civil war, colonial conflicts, or wars of religion,\xe2\x80\x9d was omitted from the final version of the Article, which coupled broader scope of application with a narrower range of rights than did earlier proposed iterations. See GCIII Commentary 42\xe2\x80\x9343.

\n

iii

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Common Article 3, then, is applicable here and, as indicated above, requires that Hamdan be tried by a \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S. T., at 3320 (Art. 3, \xc2\xb61(d)). While the term \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x9d is not specifically defined in either Common Article 3 or its accompanying commentary, other sources disclose its core meaning. The commentary accompanying a provision of the Fourth Geneva Convention, for example, defines \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98regularly constituted\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d tribunals to include \xe2\x80\x9cordinary military courts\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cdefinitely exclud[e] all special tribunals.\xe2\x80\x9d GCIV Commentary 340 (defining the term \xe2\x80\x9cproperly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d in Article 66, which the commentary treats as identical to \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d);[Footnote 64] see also Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 44 (Rutledge, J., dissenting) (describing military commission as a court \xe2\x80\x9cspecially constituted for a particular trial\xe2\x80\x9d). And one of the Red Cross\xe2\x80\x99 own treatises defines \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x9d as used in Common Article 3 to mean \xe2\x80\x9cestablished and organized in accordance with the laws and procedures already in force in a country.\xe2\x80\x9d Int\xe2\x80\x99l Comm. of Red Cross, 1 Customary International Humanitarian Law 355 (2005); see also GCIV Commentary 340 (observing that \xe2\x80\x9cordinary military courts\xe2\x80\x9d will \xe2\x80\x9cbe set up in accordance with the recognized principles governing the administration of justice\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government offers only a cursory defense of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission in light of Common Article 3. See Brief for Respondents 49\xe2\x80\x9350. As Justice Kennedy explains, that defense fails because \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he regular military courts in our system are the courts-martial established by

\n

congressional statutes.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 8 (opinion concurring in part). At a minimum, a military commission \xe2\x80\x9ccan be \xe2\x80\x98regularly constituted\xe2\x80\x99 by the standards of our military justice system only if some practical need explains deviations from court-martial practice.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 10. As we have explained, see Part VI\xe2\x80\x93C, supra, no such need has been demonstrated here.[Footnote 65]

\n

iv

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Inextricably intertwined with the question of regular constitution is the evaluation of the procedures governing the tribunal and whether they afford \xe2\x80\x9call the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S. T., at 3320 (Art. 3, \xc2\xb61(d)). Like the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court,\xe2\x80\x9d this phrase is not defined in the text of the Geneva Conventions. But it must be understood to incorporate at least the barest of those trial protections that have been recognized by customary international law. Many of these are described in Article 75 of Protocol I to the Geneva Conventions of 1949, adopted in 1977 (Protocol I). Although the United States declined to ratify Protocol I, its objections were not to Article 75 thereof. Indeed, it appears that the Government \xe2\x80\x9cregard[s] the provisions of Article 75 as an articulation of safeguards to which all persons in the hands of an enemy are entitled.\xe2\x80\x9d Taft, The Law of Armed Conflict After 9/11: Some Salient Features, 28 Yale J. Int\xe2\x80\x99l L. 319, 322 (2003). Among the rights set forth in Article 75 is the \xe2\x80\x9cright to be tried in [one\xe2\x80\x99s] presence.\xe2\x80\x9d Protocol I, Art. 75(4)(e).[Footnote 66]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We agree with Justice Kennedy that the procedures adopted to try Hamdan deviate from those governing courts-martial in ways not justified by any \xe2\x80\x9cevident practical need,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 11, and for that reason, at least, fail to afford the requisite guarantees. See post, at 8, 11\xe2\x80\x9317. We add only that, as noted in Part VI\xe2\x80\x93A, supra, various provisions of Commission Order No. 1 dispense with the principles, articulated in Article 75 and indisputably part of the customary international law, that an accused must, absent disruptive conduct or consent, be present for his trial and must be privy to the evidence against him. See \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa76(B)(3), (D).[Footnote 67] That the Government has a compelling interest in denying Hamdan access to certain sensitive information is not doubted. Cf. post, at 47\xe2\x80\x9348 (Thomas, J., dissenting). But, at least absent express statutory provision to the contrary, information used to convict a person of a crime must be disclosed to him.

\n

v

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Common Article 3 obviously tolerates a great degree of flexibility in trying individuals captured during armed conflict; its requirements are general ones, crafted to accommodate a wide variety of legal systems. But requirements they are nonetheless. The commission that the President has convened to try Hamdan does not meet those requirements.

\n

VII

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We have assumed, as we must, that the allegations made in the Government\xe2\x80\x99s charge against Hamdan are true. We have assumed, moreover, the truth of the message implicit in that charge\xe2\x80\x94viz., that Hamdan is a dangerous individual whose beliefs, if acted upon, would cause great harm and even death to innocent civilians, and who would act upon those beliefs if given the opportunity. It bears emphasizing that Hamdan does not challenge, and we do not today address, the Government\xe2\x80\x99s power to detain him for the duration of active hostilities in order to prevent such harm. But in undertaking to try Hamdan and subject him to criminal punishment, the Executive is bound to comply with the Rule of Law that prevails in this jurisdiction.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The judgment of the Court of Appeals is reversed, and the case is remanded for further proceedings.

\n

It is so ordered.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice took no part in the consideration or decision of this case.

\nFootnote 7

\xc2\xa0In his insistence to the contrary, Justice Scalia reads too much into Bruner v. United States, 343 U. S. 112 (1952), Hallowell v. Commons, 239 U. S. 506 (1916), and Insurance Co. v. Ritchie, 5 Wall. 541 (1867). See post, at 2\xe2\x80\x934 (dissenting opinion). None of those cases says that the absence of an express provision reserving jurisdiction over pending cases trumps or renders irrelevant any other indications of congressional intent. Indeed, Bruner itself relied on such other indications\xe2\x80\x93including a negative inference drawn from the statutory text, cf. infra, at 13\xe2\x80\x93to support its conclusion that jurisdiction was not available. The Court observed that (1) Congress had been put on notice by prior lower court cases addressing the Tucker Act that it ought to specifically reserve jurisdiction over pending cases, see 343 U. S., at 115, and (2) in contrast to the congressional silence concerning reservation of jurisdiction, reservation had been made of \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98any rights or liabilities\xe2\x80\x99 existing at the effective date of the Act\xe2\x80\x9d repealed by another provision of the Act, ibid., n. 7.

\nFootnote 8

\xc2\xa0The question in Lindh was whether new limitations on the availability of habeas relief imposed by the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (AEDPA), 110 Stat. 1214, applied to habeas actions pending on the date of AEDPA\xe2\x80\x99s enactment. We held that they did not. At the outset, we rejected the State\xe2\x80\x99s argument that, in the absence of a clear congressional statement to the contrary, a \xe2\x80\x9cprocedural\xe2\x80\x9d rule must apply to pending cases. 521 U. S., at 326.

\nFootnote 9

\xc2\xa0That paragraph (1), along with paragraphs (2) and (3), is to \xe2\x80\x9ctake effect on the date of enactment,\xe2\x80\x9d DTA \xc2\xa71005(h)(1), 119 Stat. 2743, is not dispositive; \xe2\x80\x9ca \xe2\x80\x98statement that a statute will become effective on a certain date does not even arguably suggest that it has any application to conduct that occurred at an earlier date.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U. S. 289, 317 (2001) (quoting Landgraf v. USI Film Products, 511 U. S. 244, 257 (1994)). Certainly, the \xe2\x80\x9ceffective date\xe2\x80\x9d provision cannot bear the weight Justice Scalia would place on it. See post, at 5, and n.\xc2\xa01. Congress deemed that provision insufficient, standing alone, to render subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) applicable to pending cases; hence its adoption of subsection (h)(2). Justice Scalia seeks to avoid reducing subsection (h)(2) to a mere redundancy\xe2\x80\x94a consequence he seems to acknowledge must otherwise follow from his interpretation\xe2\x80\x94by speculating that Congress had special reasons, not also relevant to subsection (e)(1), to worry that subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) would be ruled inapplicable to pending cases. As we explain infra, at 17, and n. 12, that attempt fails.

\nFootnote 10

Cf. post, at 14 (Scalia, J., dissenting).)

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0While statements attributed to the final bill\xe2\x80\x99s two other sponsors, Senators Graham and Kyl, arguably contradict Senator Levin\xe2\x80\x99s contention that the final version of the Act preserved jurisdiction over pending habeas cases, see 151 Cong. \nFootnote 12

\xc2\xa0This assertion is itself highly questionable. The cases that Justice Scalia cites to support his distinction are Republic of Austria v. Altmann, 541 U. S. 677 (2004), and Hughes Aircraft Co. v. United States ex rel. Schumer, 520 U. S. 939 (1997). See post, at 8. While the Court in both of those cases recognized that statutes \xe2\x80\x9ccreating\xe2\x80\x9d jurisdiction may have retroactive effect if they affect \xe2\x80\x9csubstantive\xe2\x80\x9d rights, see Altmann, 541 U. S. 320, 327\xe2\x80\x93328 (1997); id., at 342\xe2\x80\x93343 (Rehnquist, C. J., dissenting) (construing AEDPA\xe2\x80\x99s amendments as \xe2\x80\x9cousting jurisdiction\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 13

\xc2\xa0See Landgraf, 511 U. We express no view about whether the DTA would require transfer of such an action to the District of Columbia Circuit.

\nFootnote 15

\xc2\xa0Because we conclude that \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) does not strip federal courts\xe2\x80\x99 jurisdiction over cases pending on the date of the DTA\xe2\x80\x99s enactment, we do not decide whether, if it were otherwise, this Court would nonetheless retain jurisdiction to hear Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s appeal. Cf. supra, at 10. Nor do we decide the manner in which the canon of constitutional avoidance should affect subsequent interpretation of the DTA. See, e.g., St. Cyr, 533 U. \nFootnote 16

\xc2\xa0Councilman distinguished service personnel from civilians, whose challenges to ongoing military proceedings are cognizable in federal court. See, e.g., United States ex rel. Toth v. Quarles, 350 U. S. 11 (1955). As we explained in Councilman, abstention is not appropriate in cases in which individuals raise \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98substantial arguments denying the right of the military to try them at all,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and in which the legal challenge \xe2\x80\x9cturn[s] on the status of the persons as to whom the military asserted its power.\xe2\x80\x9d 420 U. S., at 759 (quoting Noyd v. Bond, 395 U. S. 683, 696, n.\xc2\xa08 (1969)). In other words, we do not apply Councilman abstention when there is a substantial question whether a military tribunal has personal jurisdiction over the defendant. Because we conclude that abstention is inappropriate for a more basic reason, we need not consider whether the jurisdictional exception recognized in Councilman applies here.

\nFootnote 17

\xc2\xa0See also Noyd, 395 U. S., at 694\xe2\x80\x93696 (noting that the Court of Military Appeals consisted of \xe2\x80\x9cdisinterested civilian judges,\xe2\x80\x9d and concluding that there was no reason for the Court to address an Air Force Captain\xe2\x80\x99s argument that he was entitled to remain free from confinement pending appeal of his conviction by court-martial \xe2\x80\x9cwhen the highest military court stands ready to consider petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s arguments\xe2\x80\x9d). Cf. Parisi v. Davidson, 405 U. S. 34, 41\xe2\x80\x9343 (1972) (\xe2\x80\x9cUnder accepted principles of comity, the court should stay its hand only if the relief the petitioner seeks \xe2\x80\xa6 would also be available to him with reasonable promptness and certainty through the machinery of the military judicial system in its processing of the court-martial charge\xe2\x80\x9d).

\xc2\xa0If he chooses, the President may delegate this ultimate decisionmaking authority to the Secretary of Defense. See \xc2\xa76(H)(6).

\nFootnote 19

\xc2\xa0Justice Scalia chides us for failing to include the District of Columbia Circuit\xe2\x80\x99s review powers under the DTA in our description of the review mechanism erected by Commission Order No. 1. See post, at 22. Whether or not the limited review permitted under the DTA may be treated as akin to the plenary review exercised by the Court of Appeals for the Armed Forces, petitioner here is not afforded a right to such review. See infra, at 52; \xc2\xa71005(e)(3), 119 Stat. 2743.

\nFootnote 20

\xc2\xa0Having correctly declined to abstain from addressing Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s challenge to the lawfulness of the military commission convened to try him, the Court of Appeals suggested that Councilman abstention nonetheless applied to bar its consideration of one of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s arguments\xe2\x80\x94namely, that his commission violated Article 3 of the Third Geneva Convention, 6 U. S. T. 3316, 3318. See Part VI, infra. Although the Court of Appeals rejected the Article 3 argument on the merits, it also stated that, because the challenge was not \xe2\x80\x9cjurisdictional,\xe2\x80\x9d it did not fall within the exception that Schlesinger v. Councilman, 420 U. S. 738 (1975), recognized for defendants who raise substantial arguments that a military tribunal lacks personal jurisdiction over them. See 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 42.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In reaching this conclusion, the Court of Appeals conflated two distinct inquiries: (1) whether Hamdan has raised a substantial argument that the military commission lacks authority to try him; and, more fundamentally, (2) whether the comity considerations underlying Councilman apply to trigger the abstention principle in the first place. As the Court of Appeals acknowledged at the beginning of its opinion, the first question warrants consideration only if the answer to the second is yes. See 415 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 36\xe2\x80\x9337. Since, as the Court of Appeals properly concluded, the answer to the second question is in fact no, there is no need to consider any exception.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At any rate, it appears that the exception would apply here. As discussed in Part VI, infra, Hamdan raises a substantial argument that, because the military commission that has been convened to try him is not a \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98regularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d under the Geneva Conventions, it is ultra vires and thus lacks jurisdiction over him. Brief for Petitioner 5.
