\xc2\xa0See also Winthrop 831 (\xe2\x80\x9c[I]n general, it is those provisions of the Constitution which empower Congress to \xe2\x80\x98declare war\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98raise armies,\xe2\x80\x99 and which, in authorizing the initiation of war, authorize the employment of all necessary and proper agencies for its due prosecution, from which this tribunal derives its original sanction\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis in original)).

\nFootnote 22

\xc2\xa0Article 15 was first adopted as part of the Articles of War in 1916. See Act of Aug. 29, 1916, ch. 418, \xc2\xa73, Art. 15, 39 Stat. 652. When the Articles of War were codified and re-enacted as the UCMJ in 1950, Congress determined to retain Article 15 because it had been \xe2\x80\x9cconstrued by the Supreme Court (Ex Parte Quirin, 317 U. S. 1 (1942)).\xe2\x80\x9d S.\xc2\xa0Rep. No. 486, 81st Cong., 1st Sess., 13 (1949).

\nFootnote 23

\xc2\xa0Whether or not the President has independent power, absent congressional authorization, to convene military commissions, he may not disregard limitations that Congress has, in proper exercise of its own war powers, placed on his powers. See Youngstown Sheet & Tube Co. v. Sawyer, 343 U. S. 579, 637 (1952) (Jackson, J., concurring). The Government does not argue otherwise.

\nFootnote 24

\xc2\xa0On this point, it is noteworthy that the Court in Ex parte Quirin, 317 U. S. 1 (1942), looked beyond Congress\xe2\x80\x99 declaration of war and accompanying authorization for use of force during World War II, and relied instead on Article of War 15 to find that Congress had authorized the use of military commissions in some circumstances. See id., at 26\xe2\x80\x9329. Justice Thomas\xe2\x80\x99 assertion that we commit \xe2\x80\x9cerror\xe2\x80\x9d in reading Article 21 of the UCMJ to place limitations upon the President\xe2\x80\x99s use of military commissions, see post, at 5 (dissenting opinion), ignores the reasoning in Quirin.

\nFootnote 25

\xc2\xa0The justification for, and limitations on, these commissions were summarized in Milligan:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cIf, in foreign invasion or civil war, the courts are actually closed, and it is impossible to administer criminal justice according to law, then, on the theatre of active military operations, where war really prevails, there is a necessity to furnish a substitute for the civil authority, thus overthrown, to preserve the safety of the army and society; and as no power is left but the military, it is allowed to govern by martial rule until the laws can have their free course. As necessity creates the rule, so it limits its duration; for, if this government is continued after the courts are reinstated, it is a gross usurpation of power. Martial rule can never exist where the courts are open, and in the proper and unobstructed exercise of their jurisdiction. It is also confined to the locality of actual war.\xe2\x80\x9d 4 Wall., at 127 (emphases in original).

\nFootnote 26

\xc2\xa0The limitations on these occupied territory or military government commissions are tailored to the tribunals\xe2\x80\x99 purpose and the exigencies that necessitate their use. They may be employed \xe2\x80\x9cpending the establishment of civil government,\xe2\x80\x9d Madsen, 343 U. S., at 354\xe2\x80\x93355, which may in some cases extend beyond the \xe2\x80\x9ccessation of hostilities,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 348.

\nFootnote 27

\xc2\xa0So much may not be evident on cold review of the Civil War trials often cited as precedent for this kind of tribunal because the commissions established during that conflict operated as both martial law or military government tribunals and law-of-war commissions. Hence, \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary commanders began the practice [during the Civil War] of using the same name, the same rules, and often the same tribunals\xe2\x80\x9d to try both ordinary crimes and war crimes. Bickers, 34 Tex. Tech. L.\xc2\xa0Rev., at 908. \xe2\x80\x9cFor the first time, accused horse thieves and alleged saboteurs found themselves subject to trial by the same military commission.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 909. The Civil War precedents must therefore be considered with caution; as we recognized in Quirin, 317 U. S., at 29, and as further discussed below, commissions convened during time of war but under neither martial law nor military government may try only offenses against the law of war.

\nFootnote 28

\xc2\xa0If the commission is established pursuant to martial law or military government, its jurisdiction extends to offenses committed within \xe2\x80\x9cthe exercise of military government or martial law.\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 837.

\nFootnote 29

\xc2\xa0Winthrop adds as a fifth, albeit not-always-complied-with, criterion that \xe2\x80\x9cthe trial must be had within the theatre of war . . . ; that, if held elsewhere, and where the civil courts are open and available, the proceedings and sentence will be coram non judice.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 836. The Government does not assert that Guantanamo Bay is a theater of war, but instead suggests that neither Washington, D.\xc2\xa0C., in 1942 nor the Philippines in 1945 qualified as a \xe2\x80\x9cwar zone\xe2\x80\x9d either. Brief for Respondents 27; cf. Quirin, 317 U. S. 1; In re Yamashita, 327 U. S. 1 (1946).

\nFootnote 30

\xc2\xa0The elements of this conspiracy charge have been defined not by Congress but by the President. See Military Commission Instruction No. 2, 32 CFR \xc2\xa711.6 (2005).

\nFootnote 31

\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas would treat Osama bin Laden\xe2\x80\x99s 1996 declaration of jihad against Americans as the inception of the war. See post, at 7\xe2\x80\x9310 (dissenting opinion). But even the Government does not go so far; although the United States had for some time prior to the attacks of September 11, 2001, been aggressively pursuing al Qaeda, neither in the charging document nor in submissions before this Court has the Government asserted that the President\xe2\x80\x99s war powers were activated prior to September 11, 2001. Cf. Brief for Respondents 25 (describing the events of September 11, 2001, as \xe2\x80\x9can act of war\xe2\x80\x9d that \xe2\x80\x9ctriggered a right to deploy military forces abroad to defend the United States by combating al Qaeda\xe2\x80\x9d). Justice Thomas\xe2\x80\x99 further argument that the AUMF is \xe2\x80\x9cbackward looking\xe2\x80\x9d and therefore authorizes trial by military commission of crimes that occurred prior to the inception of war is insupportable. See post, at 8, n.\xc2\xa03. If nothing else, Article 21 of the UCMJ requires that the President comply with the law of war in his use of military commissions. As explained in the text, the law of war permits trial only of offenses \xe2\x80\x9ccommitted within the period of the war.\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 837; see also Quirin, 317 U. S., at 28\xe2\x80\x9329 (observing that law-of-war military commissions may be used to try \xe2\x80\x9cthose enemies who in their attempt to thwart or impede our military effort have violated the law of war\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)). The sources that Justice Thomas relies on to suggest otherwise simply do not support his position. Colonel Green\xe2\x80\x99s short exegesis on military commissions cites Howland for the proposition that \xe2\x80\x9c[o]ffenses committed before a formal declaration of war or before the declaration of martial law may be tried by military commission.\xe2\x80\x9d The Military Commission, 42 Am. J. Int\xe2\x80\x99l L. 832, 848 (1948) (emphases added) (cited post, at 9\xe2\x80\x9310). Assuming that to be true, nothing in our analysis turns on the admitted absence of either a formal declaration of war or a declaration of martial law. Our focus instead is on the September 11, 2001 attacks that the Government characterizes as the relevant \xe2\x80\x9cact[s] of war,\xe2\x80\x9d and on the measure that authorized the President\xe2\x80\x99s deployment of military force\xe2\x80\x94the AUMF. Because we do not question the Government\xe2\x80\x99s position that the war commenced with the events of September 11, 2001, the Prize Cases, 2 Black 635 (1863) (cited post, at 2, 7, 8, and 10 (Thomas, J., dissenting)), are not germane to the analysis.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, Justice Thomas\xe2\x80\x99 assertion that Julius Otto Kuehn\xe2\x80\x99s trial by military commission \xe2\x80\x9cfor conspiring with Japanese officials to betray the United States fleet to the Imperial Japanese Government prior to its attack on Pearl Harbor\xe2\x80\x9d stands as authoritative precedent for Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial by commission, post, at 9, misses the mark in three critical respects. First, Kuehn was tried for the federal espionage crimes under what were then 50 U. S.C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa731, 32, and 34, not with common-law violations of the law of war. See Hearings before the Joint Committee on the Investigation of the Pearl Harbor Attack, 79th Cong., 1st Sess., pt. 30, pp. 3067\xe2\x80\x933069 (1946). Second, he was tried by martial law commission (a kind of commission Justice Thomas acknowledges is not relevant to the analysis here, and whose jurisdiction extends to offenses committed within \xe2\x80\x9cthe exercise of . . . martial law,\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 837, see supra, n. 28), not a commission established exclusively to try violations of the law of war. See ibid. Third, the martial law commissions established to try crimes in Hawaii were ultimately declared illegal by this Court. See Duncan v. Kahanamoku, 327 U. S. 304, 324 (1946) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe phrase \xe2\x80\x98martial law\xe2\x80\x99 as employed in [the Hawaiian Organic Act], while intended to authorize the military to act vigorously for the maintenance of an orderly civil government and for the defense of the Islands against actual or threatened rebellion or invasion, was not intended to authorize the supplanting of courts by military tribunals\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 32

\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas adopts the remarkable view, not advocated by the Government, that the charging document in this case actually includes more than one charge: Conspiracy and several other ill-defined crimes, like \xe2\x80\x9cjoining an organization\xe2\x80\x9d that has a criminal purpose, \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[b]eing a guerilla,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and aiding the enemy. See post, at 16\xe2\x80\x9321, and n. 9. There are innumerable problems with this approach.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, the crimes Justice Thomas identifies were not actually charged. It is one thing to observe that charges before a military commission \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98need not be stated with the precision of a common law indictment,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 15, n.\xc2\xa07 (citation omitted); it is quite another to say that a crime not charged may nonetheless be read into an indictment. Second, the Government plainly had available to it the tools and the time it needed to charge petitioner with the various crimes Justice Thomas refers to, if it believed they were supported by the allegations. As Justice Thomas himself observes, see post, at 21, the crime of aiding the enemy may, in circumstances where the accused owes allegiance to the party whose enemy he is alleged to have aided, be triable by military commission pursuant to Article 104 of the UCMJ, 10 U. S. C. \xc2\xa7904. Indeed, the Government has charged detainees under this provision when it has seen fit to do so. See Brief for David Hicks as Amicus Curiae 7.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Third, the cases Justice Thomas relies on to show that Hamdan may be guilty of violations of the law of war not actually charged do not support his argument. Justice Thomas begins by blurring the distinction between those categories of \xe2\x80\x9coffender\xe2\x80\x9d who may be tried by military commission (e.g., jayhawkers and the like) with the \xe2\x80\x9coffenses\xe2\x80\x9d that may be so tried. Even when it comes to \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98being a guerilla,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d cf. post, at 18, n.\xc2\xa09 (citation omitted), a label alone does not render a person susceptible to execution or other criminal punishment; the charge of \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98being a guerilla\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d invariably is accompanied by the allegation that the defendant \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98took up arms\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d as such. This is because, as explained by Judge Advocate General Holt in a decision upholding the charge of \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98being a guerilla\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d as one recognized by \xe2\x80\x9cthe universal usage of the times,\xe2\x80\x9d the charge is simply shorthand (akin to \xe2\x80\x9cbeing a spy\xe2\x80\x9d) for \xe2\x80\x9cthe perpetration of a succession of similar acts\xe2\x80\x9d of violence. Record Books of the Judge Advocate General Office, R. 3, 590. The sources cited by Justice Thomas confirm as much. See cases cited post, at 18, n.\xc2\xa09.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Likewise, the suggestion that the Nuremberg precedents support Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s conviction for the (uncharged) crime of joining a criminal organization must fail. Cf. post, at 19\xe2\x80\x9321. The convictions of certain high-level Nazi officials for \xe2\x80\x9cmembership in a criminal organization\xe2\x80\x9d were secured pursuant to specific provisions of the Charter of the International Military Tribunal that permitted indictment of individual organization members following convictions of the organizations themselves. See Arts. 9 and 10, in 1 Trial of the Major War Criminals Before the International Military Tribunal 12 (1947). The initial plan to use organizations\xe2\x80\x99 convictions as predicates for mass individual trials ultimately was abandoned. See T. Taylor, Anatomy of the Nuremberg Trials: A Personal Memoir 584\xe2\x80\x93585, 638 (1992).

\nFootnote 33

\xc2\xa0Cf. 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7904 (making triable by military commission the crime of aiding the enemy); \xc2\xa7906 (same for spying); War Crimes Act of 1996, 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa72441 (2000 ed. and Supp. III) (listing war crimes); Foreign Operations, Export Financing, and Related Appropriations Act, 1998, \xc2\xa7583, 111 Stat. 2436 (same).

\nFootnote 34

\xc2\xa0While the common law necessarily is \xe2\x80\x9cevolutionary in nature,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 13 (Thomas, J., dissenting), even in jurisdictions where common law crimes are still part of the penal framework, an act does not become a crime without its foundations having been firmly established in precedent. See, e.g., R. v. Rimmington, [2006] 2 All E. R. 257, 275\xe2\x80\x93279 (House of Lords); id., at 279 (while \xe2\x80\x9csome degree of vagueness is inevitable and development of the law is a recognised feature of common law courts, \xe2\x80\xa6 the law-making function of the courts must remain within reasonable limits\xe2\x80\x9d); see also Rogers v. Tennessee, 532 U. S. 451, 472\xe2\x80\x93478 (2001) (Scalia, J., dissenting). The caution that must be exercised in the incremental development of common-law crimes by the judiciary is, for the reasons explained in the text, all the more critical when reviewing developments that stem from military action.

\nFootnote 35

\xc2\xa0The 19th-century trial of the \xe2\x80\x9cLincoln conspirators,\xe2\x80\x9d even if properly classified as a trial by law-of-war commission, cf. W. Rehnquist, All the Laws But One: Civil Liberties in Wartime 165\xe2\x80\x93167 (1998) (analyzing the conspiracy charges in light of ordinary criminal law principles at the time), is at best an equivocal exception. Although the charge against the defendants in that case accused them of \xe2\x80\x9ccombining, confederating, and conspiring together\xe2\x80\x9d to murder the President, they were also charged (as we read the indictment, cf. post, at 23, n. 14 (Thomas, J., dissenting)) with \xe2\x80\x9cmaliciously, unlawfully, and traitorously murdering the said Abraham Lincoln.\xe2\x80\x9d H.\xc2\xa0R. Doc. No. 314, 55th Cong., 1st Sess., 696 (1899). Moreover, the Attorney General who wrote the opinion defending the trial by military commission treated the charge as if it alleged the substantive offense of assassination. See 11 Op. Atty. Gen. 297 (1865) (analyzing the propriety of trying by military commission \xe2\x80\x9cthe offence of having assassinated the President\xe2\x80\x9d); see also Mudd v. Caldera, 134 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 138, 140 (DC 2001).

\nFootnote 36

\xc2\xa0By contrast, the Geneva Conventions do extend liability for substantive war crimes to those who \xe2\x80\x9corde[r]\xe2\x80\x9d their commission, see Third Geneva Convention, Art. 129, 6 U. \nFootnote 37

\xc2\xa0The other examples Justice Thomas offers are no more availing. The Civil War indictment against Robert Louden, cited post, at 25, alleged a conspiracy, but not one in violation of the law of war. See War Dept., General Court Martial Order No. 41, p.\xc2\xa020 (1864). A separate charge of \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[t]ransgression of the laws and customs of war\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d made no mention of conspiracy. Id., at 17. \nFootnote 38

\xc2\xa0The Court in Quirin \xe2\x80\x9cassume[d] that there are acts regarded in other countries, or by some writers on international law, as offenses against the law of war which would not be triable by military tribunal here, either because they are not recognized by our courts as violations of the law of war or because they are of that class of offenses constitutionally triable only by a jury.\xe2\x80\x9d 317 U. S., at 29. We need not test the validity of that assumption here because the international sources only corroborate the domestic ones.

\nFootnote 40

\xc2\xa0 See also 15 United Nations War Crimes Commissions, Law Reports of Trials of War Criminals 90\xe2\x80\x9391 (1949) (observing that, although a few individuals were charged with conspiracy under European domestic criminal codes following World War II, \xe2\x80\x9cthe United States Military Tribunals\xe2\x80\x9d established at that time did not \xe2\x80\x9crecognis[e] as a separate offence conspiracy to commit war crimes or crimes against humanity\xe2\x80\x9d). \nFootnote 41

\nFootnote 50

\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas relies on the legislative history of the UCMJ to argue that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 adoption of Article 36(b) in the wake of World War II was \xe2\x80\x9cmotivated\xe2\x80\x9d solely by a desire for \xe2\x80\x9cuniformity across the separate branches of the armed services.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 35. But even if Congress was concerned with ensuring uniformity across service branches, that does not mean it did not also intend to codify the longstanding practice of procedural parity between courts-martial and other military tribunals. Indeed, the suggestion that Congress did not intend uniformity across tribunal types is belied by the textual proximity of subsection (a) (which requires that the rules governing criminal trials in federal district courts apply, absent the President\xe2\x80\x99s determination of impracticability, to courts-martial, provost courts, and military commissions alike) and subsection (b) (which imposes the uniformity requirement).

\xc2\xa0We may assume that such a determination would be entitled to a measure of deference. For the reasons given by Justice Kennedy, see post, at 5 (opinion concurring in part), however, the level of deference accorded to a determination made under subsection (b) presum-\nably would not be as high as that accorded to a determination under subsection (a).

\nFootnote 52

\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas looks not to the President\xe2\x80\x99s official Article 36(a) determination, but instead to press statements made by the Secretary of Defense and the Under Secretary of Defense for Policy. See post, at 36\xe2\x80\x9338 (dissenting opinion). We have not heretofore, in evaluating the legality of Executive action, deferred to comments made by such officials to the media. Moreover, the only additional reason the comments provide\xe2\x80\x94aside from the general danger posed by international terrorism\xe2\x80\x94for departures from court-martial procedures is the need to protect classified information. As we explain in the text, and as Justice Kennedy elaborates in his separate opinion, the structural and procedural defects of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission extend far beyond rules preventing access to classified information.

\nFootnote 53

\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas relies extensively on Madsen for the proposition that the President has free rein to set the procedures that govern military commissions. See post, at 30, 31, 33, n.\xc2\xa016, 34, and 45. That reliance is misplaced. Not only did Madsen not involve a law-of-war military commission, but (1) the petitioner there did not challenge the procedures used to try her, (2) the UCMJ, with its new Article 36(b), did not become effective until May 31, 1951, after the petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s trial, see 343 U. S., at 345, n.\xc2\xa06, and (3) the procedures used to try the petitioner actually afforded more protection than those used in courts-martial, see id., at 358\xe2\x80\x93360; see also id., at 358 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Military Government Courts for Germany . . . have had a less military character than that of courts-martial\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 54

\xc2\xa0Prior to the enactment of Article 36(b), it may well have been the case that a deviation from the rules governing courts-martial would not have rendered the military commission \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98illegal.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 30\xe2\x80\x9331, n.\xc2\xa016 (Thomas, J., dissenting) (quoting Winthrop 841). Article 36(b), however, imposes a statutory command that must be heeded.

\nFootnote 55

\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas makes the different argument that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s Geneva Convention challenge is not yet \xe2\x80\x9cripe\xe2\x80\x9d because he has yet to be sentenced. See post, at 43\xe2\x80\x9345. This is really just a species of the abstention argument we have already rejected. See Part III, supra. The text of the Geneva Conventions does not direct an accused to wait until sentence is imposed to challenge the legality of the tribunal that is to try him.

\nFootnote 56

\xc2\xa0As explained in Part VI\xe2\x80\x93C, supra, that is no longer true under the 1949 Conventions.

\nFootnote 57

\xc2\xa0But see, e.g., 4 Int\xe2\x80\x99l Comm. of Red Cross, Commentary: Geneva Convention Relative to the Protection of Civilian Persons in Time of War 21 (1958) (hereinafter GCIV Commentary) (the 1949 Geneva Conventions were written \xe2\x80\x9cfirst and foremost to protect individuals, and not to serve State interests\xe2\x80\x9d); GCIII Commentary 91 (\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not \xe2\x80\xa6 until the Conventions of 1949 \xe2\x80\xa6 that the existence of \xe2\x80\x98rights\xe2\x80\x99 conferred in prisoners of war was affirmed\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 58

\xc2\xa0But see generally Brief for Louis Henkin et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae; 1 Int\xe2\x80\x99l Comm. for the Red Cross, Commentary: Geneva Convention for the Amelioration of the Condition of the Wounded and Sick in Armed Forces in the Field 84 (1952) (\xe2\x80\x9cIt should be possible in States which are parties to the Convention \xe2\x80\xa6 for the rules of the Convention to be evoked before an appropriate national court by the protected person who has suffered a violation\xe2\x80\x9d); GCII Commentary 92; GCIV Commentary 79.

\nFootnote 59

\xc2\xa0For convenience\xe2\x80\x99s sake, we use citations to the Third Geneva Convention only.

\nFootnote 60

\xc2\xa0The President has stated that the conflict with the Taliban is a conflict to which the Geneva Conventions apply. See White House Memorandum, Humane Treatment of Taliban and al Qaeda Detainees 2\n(Feb. 7, 2002), available at http://www.justicescholars.org/pegc/archive/\nWhite_House/bush_memo_20020207_ed.pdf (hereinafter White House Memorandum).

\nFootnote 61

\xc2\xa0Hamdan observes that Article 5 of the Third Geneva Convention requires that if there be \xe2\x80\x9cany doubt\xe2\x80\x9d whether he is entitled to prisoner-of-war protections, he must be afforded those protections until his status is determined by a \xe2\x80\x9ccompetent tribunal.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S. T., at 3324. See also Headquarters Depts. of Army, Navy, Air Force, and Marine Corps, Army Regulation 190\xe2\x80\x938, Enemy Prisoners of War, Retained Personnel, Civilian Internees and Other Detainees (1997), App. 116. Because we hold that Hamdan may not, in any event, be tried by the military commission the President has convened pursuant to the November 13 Order and Commission Order No. 1, the question whether his potential status as a prisoner of war independently renders illegal his trial by military commission may be reserved.

\nFootnote 62

\xc2\xa0The term \xe2\x80\x9cParty\xe2\x80\x9d here has the broadest possible meaning; a Party need neither be a signatory of the Convention nor \xe2\x80\x9ceven represent a legal entity capable of undertaking international obligations.\xe2\x80\x9d GCIII Commentary 37.

\nFootnote 63

\xc2\xa0See also GCIII Commentary 35 (Common Article 3 \xe2\x80\x9chas the merit of being simple and clear\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Its observance does not depend upon preliminary discussions on the nature of the conflict\xe2\x80\x9d); GCIV Commentary 51 (\xe2\x80\x9c[N]obody in enemy hands can be outside the law\xe2\x80\x9d); U. S. Army Judge Advocate General\xe2\x80\x99s Legal Center and School, Dept. of the Army, Law of War Handbook 144 (2004) (Common Article 3 \xe2\x80\x9cserves as a \xe2\x80\x98minimum yardstick of protection in all conflicts, not just internal armed conflicts\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Nicaragua v. United States, 1986 I.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0J. 14, \xc2\xb6218, 25 I. L. M. 1023)); Prosecutor v. Tadi\xc3\xa6, Case No. IT\xe2\x80\x9394\xe2\x80\x931, Decision on the Defence Motion for Interlocutory Appeal on Jurisdiction, \xc2\xb6102 (ICTY App. Chamber, Oct. 2, 1995) (stating that \xe2\x80\x9cthe character of the conflict is irrelevant\xe2\x80\x9d in deciding whether Common Article 3 applies).

\nFootnote 64

\xc2\xa0The commentary\xe2\x80\x99s assumption that the terms \xe2\x80\x9cproperly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d are interchangeable is beyond reproach; the French version of Article 66, which is equally authoritative, uses the term \xe2\x80\x9cr\xc3\xa9guli\xc3\xa8rement constitu\xc3\xa9s\xe2\x80\x9d in place of \xe2\x80\x9cproperly constituted.\xe2\x80\x9d

\nFootnote 65

\xc2\xa0Further evidence of this tribunal\xe2\x80\x99s irregular constitution is the fact that its rules and procedures are subject to change midtrial, at the whim of the Executive. See Commission Order No. 1, \xc2\xa711 (providing that the Secretary of Defense may change the governing rules \xe2\x80\x9cfrom time to time\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 66

\xc2\xa0Other international instruments to which the United States is a signatory include the same basic protections set forth in Article 75. See, e.g., International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights, Art. 14, \xc2\xb63(d), Mar. 23, 1976, 999 U. N. T. S. 171 (setting forth the right of an accused \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o be tried in his presence, and to defend himself in person or through legal assistance of his own choosing\xe2\x80\x9d). Following World War II, several defendants were tried and convicted by military commission for violations of the law of war in their failure to afford captives fair trials before imposition and execution of sentence. In two such trials, the prosecutors argued that the defendants\xe2\x80\x99 failure to apprise accused individuals of all evidence against them constituted violations of the law of war. See 5 U. N. War Crimes Commission 30 (trial of Sergeant-Major Shigeru Ohashi), 75 (trial of General Tanaka Hisakasu).

\nFootnote 67

\xc2\xa0The Government offers no defense of these procedures other than to observe that the defendant may not be barred from access to evidence if such action would deprive him of a \xe2\x80\x9cfull and fair trial.\xe2\x80\x9d Commission Order No. 1, \xc2\xa76(D)(5)(b). But the Government suggests no circumstances in which it would be \xe2\x80\x9cfair\xe2\x80\x9d to convict the accused based on evidence he has not seen or heard. Cf. Crawford v. Washington, 541 U. S. 36, 49 (2004) (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98It is a rule of the common law, founded on natural justice, that no man shall be prejudiced by evidence which he had not the liberty to cross examine\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting State v. Webb, 2 N.\xc2\xa0C. 103, 104 (Super. L. & Eq. 1794) (per curiam)); Diaz v. United States, 223 U. S. 442, 455 (1912) (describing the right to be present as \xe2\x80\x9cscarcely less important to the accused than the right of trial itself\xe2\x80\x9d); Lewis v. United States, 146 U. S. 370, 372 (1892) (exclusion of defendant from part of proceedings is \xe2\x80\x9ccontrary to the dictates of humanity\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)); Joint Anti&nbhyph;Fascist Refugee Comm. v. McGrath, 341 U. S. 123, 170, n.\xc2\xa017, 171 (1951) (Frankfurter, J., concurring) (\xe2\x80\x9c[t]he plea that evidence of guilt must be secret is abhorrent to free men\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). More fundamentally, the legality of a tribunal under Common Article 3 cannot be established by bare assurances that, whatever the character of the court or the procedures it follows, individual adjudicators will act fairly.

\n\n
\n
\n
\n
\n\n
\nKENNEDY, J., CONCURRING IN PART
\nHAMDAN V. RUMSFELD
\n548 U. S. ____ (2006)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 05-184
\n
\n

SALIM AHMED HAMDAN, PETITIONER v. DONALD\nH. RUMSFELD, SECRETARY OF DEFENSE, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the district of columbia circuit

\n

[June 29, 2006]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy, with whom Justice Souter, Justice Ginsburg, and Justice Breyer join as to Parts I and II, concurring in part.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Military Commission Order No. 1, which governs the military commission established to try petitioner Salim Hamdan for war crimes, exceeds limits that certain statutes, duly enacted by Congress, have placed on the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority to convene military courts. This is not a case, then, where the Executive can assert some unilateral authority to fill a void left by congressional inaction. It is a case where Congress, in the proper exercise of its powers as an independent branch of government, and as part of a long tradition of legislative involvement in matters of military justice, has considered the subject of military tribunals and set limits on the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority. Where a statute provides the conditions for the exercise of governmental power, its requirements are the result of a deliberative and reflective process engaging both of the political branches. Respect for laws derived from the customary operation of the Executive and Legislative Branches gives some assurance of stability in time of crisis. The Constitution is best preserved by reliance on standards tested over time and insulated from the pressures of the moment.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These principles seem vindicated here, for a case that may be of extraordinary importance is resolved by ordinary rules. The rules of most relevance here are those pertaining to the authority of Congress and the interpretation of its enactments.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It seems appropriate to recite these rather fundamental points because the Court refers, as it should in its exposition of the case, to the requirement of the Geneva Conventions of 1949 that military tribunals be \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 69\xe2\x80\x94a requirement that controls here, if for no other reason, because Congress requires that military commissions like the ones at issue conform to the \xe2\x80\x9claw of war,\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821. Whatever the substance and content of the term \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d as interpreted in this and any later cases, there seems little doubt that it relies upon the importance of standards deliberated upon and chosen in advance of crisis, under a system where the single power of the Executive is checked by other constitutional mechanisms. All of which returns us to the point of beginning\xe2\x80\x94that domestic statutes control this case. If Congress, after due consideration, deems it appropriate to change the controlling statutes, in conformance with the Constitution and other laws, it has the power and prerogative to do so.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I join the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, save Parts V and VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93iv. To state my reasons for this reservation, and to show my agreement with the remainder of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s analysis by identifying particular deficiencies in the military commissions at issue, this separate opinion seems appropriate.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Trial by military commission raises separation-of-powers concerns of the highest order. Located within a single branch, these courts carry the risk that offenses will be defined, prosecuted, and adjudicated by executive officials without independent review. Cf. Loving v. United States, 517 U. S. 748, 756\xe2\x80\x93758, 760 (1996). Concentration of power puts personal liberty in peril of arbitrary action by officials, an incursion the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s three-part system is designed to avoid. It is imperative, then, that when military tribunals are established, full and proper authority exists for the Presidential directive.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The proper framework for assessing whether Executive actions are authorized is the three-part scheme used by Justice Jackson in his opinion in Youngstown Sheet & Tube Co. v. Sawyer, 343 U. S. 579 (1952). \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the President acts pursuant to an express or implied authorization of Congress, his authority is at its maximum, for it includes all that he possesses in his own right plus all that Congress can delegate.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 635. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the President acts in absence of either a congressional grant or denial of authority, he can only rely upon his own independent powers, but there is a zone of twilight in which he and Congress may have concurrent authority, or in which its distribution is uncertain.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 637. And \xe2\x80\x9c[w]hen the President takes measures incompatible with the expressed or implied will of Congress, his power is at its lowest ebb.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In this case, as the Court observes, the President has acted in a field with a history of congressional participation and regulation. Ante, at 28\xe2\x80\x9330, 55\xe2\x80\x9357. In the Uniform Code of Military Justice (UCMJ), 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7801 et seq., which Congress enacted, building on earlier statutes, in 1950, see Act of May 5, 1950, ch. 169, 64 Stat. 107, and later amended, see, e.g., Military Justice Act of 1968, 82 Stat. 1335, Congress has set forth governing principles for military courts. The UCMJ as a whole establishes an intricate system of military justice. It authorizes courts-martial in various forms, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7816\xe2\x80\x93820 (2000 ed. and Supp. III); it regulates the organization and procedure of those courts, e.g., \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7822\xe2\x80\x93835, 851\xe2\x80\x93854; it defines offenses, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7877\xe2\x80\x93934, and rights for the accused, e.g., \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7827(b)\xe2\x80\x93(c), 831, 844, 846, 855 (2000 ed.); and it provides mechanisms for appellate review, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7859\xe2\x80\x93876b (2000 ed. and Supp. III). As explained below, the statute further recognizes that special military commissions may be convened to try war crimes. See infra, at 5\xe2\x80\x936; \xc2\xa7821 (2000 ed.). While these laws provide authority for certain forms of military courts, they also impose limitations, at least two of which control this case. If the President has exceeded these limits, this becomes a case of conflict between Presidential and congressional action\xe2\x80\x94a case within Justice Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s third category, not the second or first.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0One limit on the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority is contained in \xc2\xa7836 of the UCMJ. That section provides:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c(a) Pretrial, trial, and post-trial procedures, including modes of proof, for cases arising under this chapter triable in courts-martial, military commissions and other military tribunals, and procedures for courts of inquiry, may be prescribed by the President by regulations which shall, so far as he considers practicable, apply the principles of law and the rules of evidence generally recognized in the trial of criminal cases in the United States district courts, but which may not be contrary to or inconsistent with this chapter.

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c(b) All rules and regulations made under this article shall be uniform insofar as practicable.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836 (2000 ed.).

\n

\nIn this provision the statute allows the President to implement and build on the UCMJ\xe2\x80\x99s framework by adopting procedural regulations, subject to three requirements: (1) Procedures for military courts must conform to district-court rules insofar as the President \xe2\x80\x9cconsiders practicable\xe2\x80\x9d; (2) the procedures may not be contrary to or inconsistent with the provisions of the UCMJ; and (3) \xe2\x80\x9cinsofar as practicable\xe2\x80\x9d all rules and regulations under \xc2\xa7836 must be uniform, a requirement, as the Court points out, that indicates the rules must be the same for military commissions as for courts-martial unless such uniformity is impracticable, ante, at 57, 59, and n. 50.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As the Court further instructs, even assuming the first and second requirements of \xc2\xa7836 are satisfied here\xe2\x80\x94a matter of some dispute, see ante, at 57\xe2\x80\x9359\xe2\x80\x94the third requires us to compare the military-commission procedures with those for courts-martial and determine, to the extent there are deviations, whether greater uniformity would be practicable. Ante, at 59\xe2\x80\x9362. Although we can assume the President\xe2\x80\x99s practicability judgments are entitled to some deference, the Court observes that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 choice of language in the uniformity provision of 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(b) contrasts with the language of \xc2\xa7836(a). This difference suggests, at the least, a lower degree of deference for \xc2\xa7836(b) determinations. Ante, at 59\xe2\x80\x9360. The rules for military courts may depart from federal-court rules whenever the President \xe2\x80\x9cconsiders\xe2\x80\x9d conformity impracticable, \xc2\xa7836(a); but the statute requires procedural uniformity across different military courts \xe2\x80\x9cinsofar as [uniformity is] practicable,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7836(b), not insofar as the President considers it to be so. The Court is right to conclude this is of relevance to our decision. Further, as the Court is also correct to conclude, ante, at 60, the term \xe2\x80\x9cpracticable\xe2\x80\x9d cannot be construed to permit deviations based on mere convenience or expedience. \xe2\x80\x9cPracticable\xe2\x80\x9d means \xe2\x80\x9cfeasible,\xe2\x80\x9d that is, \xe2\x80\x9cpossible to practice or perform\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9ccapable of being put into practice, done, or accomplished.\xe2\x80\x9d Webster\xe2\x80\x99s Third New International Dictionary 1780 (1961). Congress\xe2\x80\x99 chosen language, then, is best understood to allow the selection of procedures based on logistical constraints, the accommodation of witnesses, the security of the proceedings, and the like. Insofar as the \xe2\x80\x9c[p]retrial, trial, and post-trial procedures\xe2\x80\x9d for the military commissions at issue deviate from court-martial practice, the deviations must be explained by some such practical need.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition to \xc2\xa7836, a second UCMJ provision, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821, requires us to compare the commissions at issue to courts-martial. This provision states:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cThe provisions of this chapter conferring jurisdiction upon courts-martial do not deprive military commissions, provost courts, or other military tribunals of concurrent jurisdiction with respect to offenders or offenses that by statute or by the law of war may be tried by military commissions, provost courts, or other military tribunals.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In \xc2\xa7821 Congress has addressed the possibility that special military commissions\xe2\x80\x94criminal courts other than courts-martial\xe2\x80\x94may at times be convened. At the same time, however, the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority to convene military commissions is limited: It extends only to \xe2\x80\x9coffenders or offenses\xe2\x80\x9d that \xe2\x80\x9cby statute or by the law of war may be tried by\xe2\x80\x9d such military commissions. Ibid.; see also ante, at 28\xe2\x80\x9329. The Government does not claim to base the charges against Hamdan on a statute; instead it invokes the law of war. That law, as the Court explained in Ex parte Quirin, 317 U. S. 1 (1942), derives from \xe2\x80\x9crules and precepts of the law of nations\xe2\x80\x9d; it is the body of international law governing armed conflict. Id., at 28. If the military commission at issue is illegal under the law of war, then an offender cannot be tried \xe2\x80\x9cby the law of war\xe2\x80\x9d before that commission.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court is correct to concentrate on one provision of the law of war that is applicable to our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s armed conflict with al Qaeda in Afghanistan and, as a result, to the use of a military commission to try Hamdan. Ante, at 65\xe2\x80\x9370; see also 415 F.\xc2\xa03d 33, 44 (CADC 2005) (Williams, J., concurring). That provision is Common Article 3 of the four Geneva Conventions of 1949. It prohibits, as relevant here, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he passing of sentences and the carrying out of executions without previous judgment pronounced by a regularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d See, e.g., Article 3 of the Geneva Convention (III) Relative to the Treatment of Prisoners of War, Aug. 12, 1949, [1955] 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T. 3316, 3318, T.\xc2\xa0I.\xc2\xa0A.\xc2\xa0S. No. 3364. The provision is part of a treaty the United States has ratified and thus accepted as binding law. See id., at 3316. By Act of Congress, moreover, violations of Common Article 3 are considered \xe2\x80\x9cwar crimes,\xe2\x80\x9d punishable as federal offenses, when committed by or against United States nationals and military personnel. See 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa72441. There should be no doubt, then, that Common Article 3 is part of the law of war as that term is used in \xc2\xa7821.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The dissent by Justice Thomas argues that Common Article 3 nonetheless is irrelevant to this case because in Johnson v. Eisentrager, 339 U. S. 763 (1950), it was said to be the \xe2\x80\x9cobvious scheme\xe2\x80\x9d of the 1929 Geneva Convention that \xe2\x80\x9c[r]ights of alien enemies are vindicated under it only through protests and intervention of protecting powers,\xe2\x80\x9d i.e., signatory states, id., at 789, n.\xc2\xa014. As the Court explains, ante, at 63\xe2\x80\x9365, this language from Eisentrager is not controlling here. Even assuming the Eisentrager analysis has some bearing upon the analysis of the broader 1949 Conventions and that, in consequence, rights are vindicated \xe2\x80\x9cunder [those Conventions]\xe2\x80\x9d only through protests and intervention, 339 U. S., at 789, n.\xc2\xa014, Common Article 3 is nonetheless relevant to the question of authorization under \xc2\xa7821. Common Article 3 is part of the law of war that Congress has directed the President to follow in establishing military commissions. Ante, at 66\xe2\x80\x9367. Consistent with that view, the Eisentrager Court itself considered on the merits claims that \xe2\x80\x9cprocedural irregularities\xe2\x80\x9d under the 1929 Convention \xe2\x80\x9cdeprive[d] the Military Commission of jurisdiction.\xe2\x80\x9d 339 U. S., at 789, 790.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In another military commission case, In re Yamashita, 327 U. S. 1 (1946), the Court likewise considered on the merits\xe2\x80\x94without any caveat about remedies under the Convention\xe2\x80\x94a claim that an alleged violation of the 1929 Convention \xe2\x80\x9cestablish[ed] want of authority in the commission to proceed with the trial.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 23, 24. That is the precise inquiry we are asked to perform here.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Assuming the President has authority to establish a special military commission to try Hamdan, the commission must satisfy Common Article 3\xe2\x80\x99s requirement of a \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples,\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T., at 3318. The terms of this general standard are yet to be elaborated and further defined, but Congress has required compliance with it by referring to the \xe2\x80\x9claw of war\xe2\x80\x9d in \xc2\xa7821. The Court correctly concludes that the military commission here does not comply with this provision.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Common Article 3\xe2\x80\x99s standard of a \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., supports, at the least, a uniformity principle similar to that codified in \xc2\xa7836(b). The concept of a \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x9d providing \xe2\x80\x9cindispensable\xe2\x80\x9d judicial guarantees requires consideration of the system of justice under which the commission is established, though no doubt certain minimum standards are applicable. See ante, at 69\xe2\x80\x9370; 1 Int\xe2\x80\x99l Committee of the Red Cross, Customary International Humanitarian Law 355 (2005) (explaining that courts are \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d under Common Article 3 if they are \xe2\x80\x9cestablished and organised in accordance with the laws and procedures already in force in a country\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The regular military courts in our system are the courts-martial established by congressional statutes. Acts of Congress confer on those courts the jurisdiction to try \xe2\x80\x9cany person\xe2\x80\x9d subject to war crimes prosecution. 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7818. As the Court explains, moreover, while special military commissions have been convened in previous armed conflicts\xe2\x80\x94a practice recognized in \xc2\xa7821\xe2\x80\x94those military commissions generally have adopted the structure and procedure of courts-martial. See, e.g., 1 The War of the Rebellion: A Compilation of the Official Records of the Union and Confederate Armies 248 (2d series 1894) (Civil War general order requiring that military commissions \xe2\x80\x9cbe constituted in a similar manner and their proceedings be conducted according to the same general rules as courts-martial in order to prevent abuses which might otherwise arise\xe2\x80\x9d); W. Winthrop, Military Law and Precedents 835, n.\xc2\xa081 (rev. 2d ed. 1920) (\xe2\x80\x9c[M]ilitary commissions are constituted and composed, and their proceedings are conducted, similarly to general courts-martial\xe2\x80\x9d); 1 United Nations War Crimes Commission, Law Reports of Trials of War Criminals 116\xe2\x80\x93117 (1947) (reprint 1997) (hereinafter Law Reports) (discussing post-World War II regulations requiring that military commissions \xe2\x80\x9chav[e] regard for\xe2\x80\x9d rules of procedure and evidence applicable in general courts-martial); see also ante, at 53\xe2\x80\x9357; post, at 31, n.\xc2\xa015 (Thomas, J., dissenting). Today, moreover, \xc2\xa7836(b)\xe2\x80\x94which took effect after the military trials in the World War II cases invoked by the dissent, see Madsen v. Kinsella, 343 U. S. 341, 344\xe2\x80\x93345, and n.\xc2\xa06 (1952); Yamashita, supra, at 5; Quirin, 317 U. S., at 23\xe2\x80\x94codifies this presumption of uniformity at least as to \xe2\x80\x9c[p]retrial, trial, and post-trial procedures.\xe2\x80\x9d Absent more concrete statutory guidance, this historical and statutory background\xe2\x80\x94which suggests that some practical need must justify deviations from the court-martial model\xe2\x80\x94informs the understanding of which military courts are \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d under United States law.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition, whether or not the possibility, contemplated by the regulations here, of midtrial procedural changes could by itself render a military commission impermissibly irregular, ante, at 70, n. 65; see also Military Commission Order No. 1, \xc2\xa711 (Aug. 31, 2005), App. to Brief for Petitioner 46a\xe2\x80\x9372a (hereinafter MCO), an acceptable degree of independence from the Executive is necessary to render a commission \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d by the standards of our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s system of justice. And any suggestion of Executive power to interfere with an ongoing judicial process raises concerns about the proceedings\xe2\x80\x99 fairness. Again, however, courts-martial provide the relevant benchmark. Subject to constitutional limitations, see Ex parte Milligan, 4 Wall. 2 (1866), Congress has the power and responsibility to determine the necessity for military courts, and to provide the jurisdiction and procedures applicable to them. The guidance Congress has provided with respect to courts-martial indicates the level of independence and procedural rigor that Congress has deemed necessary, at least as a general matter, in the military context.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At a minimum a military commission like the one at issue\xe2\x80\x94a commission specially convened by the President to try specific persons without express congressional authorization\xe2\x80\x94can be \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d by the standards of our military justice system only if some practical need explains deviations from court-martial practice. In this regard the standard of Common Article 3, applied here in conformity with \xc2\xa7821, parallels the practicability standard of \xc2\xa7836(b). Section 836, however, is limited by its terms to matters properly characterized as procedural\xe2\x80\x94that is, \xe2\x80\x9c[p]retrial, trial, and post-trial procedures\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94while Common Article 3 permits broader consideration of matters of structure, organization, and mechanisms to promote the tribunal\xe2\x80\x99s insulation from command influence. Thus the combined effect of the two statutes discussed here\xe2\x80\x94\xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7836 and 821\xe2\x80\x94is that considerations of practicability must support departures from court-martial practice. Relevant concerns, as noted earlier, relate to logistical constraints, accommodation of witnesses, security of the proceedings, and the like, not mere expedience or convenience. This determination, of course, must be made with due regard for the constitutional principle that congressional statutes can be controlling, including the congressional direction that the law of war has a bearing on the determination.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These principles provide the framework for an analysis of the specific military commission at issue here.

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In assessing the validity of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission the precise circumstances of this case bear emphasis. The allegations against Hamdan are undoubtedly serious. Captured in Afghanistan during our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s armed conflict with the Taliban and al Qaeda\xe2\x80\x94a conflict that continues as we speak\xe2\x80\x94Hamdan stands accused of overt acts in furtherance of a conspiracy to commit terrorism: delivering weapons and ammunition to al Qaeda, acquiring trucks for use by Osama bin Laden\xe2\x80\x99s bodyguards, providing security services to bin Laden, and receiving weapons training at a terrorist camp. App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a\xe2\x80\x9367a. Nevertheless, the circumstances of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial present no exigency requiring special speed or precluding careful consideration of evidence. For roughly four years, Hamdan has been detained at a permanent United States military base in Guantanamo Bay, Cuba. And regardless of the outcome of the criminal proceedings at issue, the Government claims authority to continue to detain him based on his status as an enemy combatant.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Against this background, the Court is correct to conclude that the military commission the President has convened to try Hamdan is unauthorized. Ante, at 62, 69\xe2\x80\x9370, 72. The following analysis, which expands on the Court\xe2\x80\x99s discussion, explains my reasons for reaching this conclusion.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To begin with, the structure and composition of the military commission deviate from conventional court-martial standards. Although these deviations raise questions about the fairness of the trial, no evident practical need explains them.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Under the UCMJ, courts-martial are organized by a \xe2\x80\x9cconvening authority\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94either a commanding officer, the Secretary of Defense, the Secretary concerned, or the President. 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7822\xe2\x80\x93824 (2000 ed. and Supp. III). The convening authority refers charges for trial, Manual for Courts-Martial, United States, Rule for Courts-Martial 401 (2005 ed.) (hereinafter R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.), and selects the court-martial members who vote on the guilt or innocence of the accused and determine the sentence, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7825(d)(2), 851\xe2\x80\x93852 (2000 ed.); R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 503(a). Paralleling this structure, under Military Commission Order No. 1 an \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98Appointing Authority\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94either the Secretary of Defense or the Secretary\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cdesignee\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94establishes commissions subject to the order, MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa72, approves and refers charges to be tried by those commissions, \xc2\xa74(B)(2)(a), and appoints commission members who vote on the conviction and sentence, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa74(A)(1\xe2\x80\x933). In addition the Appointing Authority determines the number of commission members (at least three), oversees the chief prosecutor, provides \xe2\x80\x9cinvestigative or other resources\xe2\x80\x9d to the defense insofar as he or she \xe2\x80\x9cdeems necessary for a full and fair trial,\xe2\x80\x9d approves or rejects plea agreements, approves or disapproves communications with news media by prosecution or defense counsel (a function shared by the General Counsel of the Department of Defense), and issues supplementary commission regulations (subject to approval by the General Counsel of the Department of Defense, unless the Appointing Authority is the Secretary of Defense). See MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa74(A)(2), 5(H), 6(A)(4), 7(A); Military Commission Instruction No. 3, \xc2\xa75(C) (July 15, 2005) (hereinafter MCI), available at www.\ndefenselink.mil/news/Aug2005/d20050811MC13.pdf; MCI No. 4, \xc2\xa75(C) (Sept. 16, 2005), available at www.\ndefenselink.mil/news/Oct2005/d20051003MCI4.pdf MCI No. 6, \xc2\xa73(B)(3) (April 15, 2004), available at www.\ndefenselink.mil/news/Apr2004/d20040420ins6.pdf (all Internet materials as visited June 27, 2006, and available in Clerk of Court\xe2\x80\x99s case file).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Against the background of these significant powers for the Appointing Authority, which in certain respects at least conform to ordinary court-martial standards, the regulations governing the commissions at issue make several noteworthy departures. At a general court-martial\xe2\x80\x94the only type authorized to impose penalties of more than one year\xe2\x80\x99s incarceration or to adjudicate offenses against the law of war, R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 201(f); 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7818\xe2\x80\x93820 (2000 ed. and Supp. III)\xe2\x80\x94the presiding officer who rules on legal issues must be a military judge. R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 501(a)(1), 801(a)(4)\xe2\x80\x93(5); 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7816(1) (2000 ed., Supp. III); see also R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 201(f)(2)(B)(ii) (likewise requiring a military judge for certain other courts-martial); 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7819 (2000 ed. and Supp. III) (same). A military judge is an officer who is a member of a state or federal bar and has been specially certified for judicial duties by the Judge Advocate General for the officer\xe2\x80\x99s Armed Service. R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 502(c); 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7826(b). To protect their independence, military judges at general courts-martial are \xe2\x80\x9cassigned and directly responsible to the Judge Advocate General or the Judge Advocate General\xe2\x80\x99s designee.\xe2\x80\x9d R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 502(c). They must be detailed to the court, in accordance with applicable regulations, \xe2\x80\x9cby a person assigned as a military judge and directly responsible to the Judge Advocate General or the Judge Advocate General\xe2\x80\x99s designee.\xe2\x80\x9d R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 503(b); see also 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7826(c); see generally Weiss v. United States, 510 U. S. 163, 179\xe2\x80\x93181 (1994) (discussing provisions that \xe2\x80\x9cinsulat[e] military judges from the effects of command influence\xe2\x80\x9d and thus \xe2\x80\x9cpreserve judicial impartiality\xe2\x80\x9d). Here, by contrast, the Appointing Authority selects the presiding officer, MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa74(A)(1), (A)(4); and that officer need only be a judge advocate, that is, a military lawyer, \xc2\xa74(A)(4).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Appointing Authority, moreover, exercises supervisory powers that continue during trial. Any interlocutory question \xe2\x80\x9cthe disposition of which would effect a termination of proceedings with respect to a charge\xe2\x80\x9d is subject to decision not by the presiding officer, but by the Appointing Authority. \xc2\xa74(A)(5)(e) (stating that the presiding officer \xe2\x80\x9cshall certify\xe2\x80\x9d such questions to the Appointing Authority). Other interlocutory questions may be certified to the Appointing Authority as the presiding officer \xe2\x80\x9cdeems appropriate.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. While in some circumstances the Government may appeal certain rulings at a court-martial\xe2\x80\x94including \xe2\x80\x9can order or ruling that terminates the proceedings with respect to a charge or specification,\xe2\x80\x9d R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 908(a); see also 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7862(a)\xe2\x80\x94the appeals go to a body called the Court of Criminal Appeals, not to the convening authority. R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 908; 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7862(b); see also R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 1107 (requiring the convening authority to approve or disapprove the findings and sentence of a court-martial but providing for such action only after entry of sentence and restricting actions that increase penalties); 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7860 (same); cf. \xc2\xa7837(a) (barring command influence on court-martial actions). The Court of Criminal Appeals functions as the military\xe2\x80\x99s intermediate appeals court; it is established by the Judge Advocate General for each Armed Service and composed of appellate military judges. R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 1203; 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7866. This is another means in which, by structure and tradition, the court-martial process is insulated from those who have an interest in the outcome of the proceedings.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, in addition to these powers with respect to the presiding officer, the Appointing Authority has greater flexibility in appointing commission members. While a general court-martial requires, absent a contrary election by the accused, at least five members, R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 501(a)(1); 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7816(1) (2000 ed. and Supp. III), the Appointing Authority here is free, as noted earlier, to select as few as three. MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa74(A)(2). This difference may affect the deliberative process and the prosecution\xe2\x80\x99s burden of persuasion.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As compared to the role of the convening authority in a court-martial, the greater powers of the Appointing Authority here\xe2\x80\x94including even the resolution of dispositive issues in the middle of the trial\xe2\x80\x94raise concerns that the commission\xe2\x80\x99s decisionmaking may not be neutral. If the differences are supported by some practical need beyond the goal of constant and ongoing supervision, that need is neither apparent from the record nor established by the Government\xe2\x80\x99s submissions.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is no answer that, at the end of the day, the Detainee Treatment Act of 2005 (DTA), 119 Stat. 2739, affords military-commission defendants the opportunity for judicial review in federal court. As the Court is correct to observe, the scope of that review is limited, DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(D), id., at 2743; see also ante, at 8\xe2\x80\x939, and the review is not automatic if the defendant\xe2\x80\x99s sentence is under 10 years, \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(B), ibid. Also, provisions for review of legal issues after trial cannot correct for structural defects, such as the role of the Appointing Authority, that can cast doubt on the factfinding process and the presiding judge\xe2\x80\x99s exercise of discretion during trial. Before military-commission defendants may obtain judicial review, furthermore, they must navigate a military review process that again raises fairness concerns. At the outset, the Appointing Authority (unless the Appointing Authority is the Secretary of Defense) performs an \xe2\x80\x9cadministrative review\xe2\x80\x9d of undefined scope, ordering any \xe2\x80\x9csupplementary proceedings\xe2\x80\x9d deemed necessary. MCO No. 1 \xc2\xa76(H)(3). After that the case is referred to a three-member\nReview Panel composed of officers selected by the\nSecretary of Defense. \xc2\xa76(H)(4); MCI No. 9, \xc2\xa74(B) (Oct. 11, 2005), available at www.defenselink.mil/news/Oct2005/\nd20051014MCI9.pdf. Though the Review Panel may return the case for further proceedings only if a majority \xe2\x80\x9cform[s] a definite and firm conviction that a material error of law occurred,\xe2\x80\x9d MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa76(H)(4); MCI No. 9, \xc2\xa74(C)(1)(a), only one member must have \xe2\x80\x9cexperience as a judge,\xe2\x80\x9d MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa76(H)(4); nothing in the regulations requires that other panel members have legal training. By comparison to the review of court-martial judgments performed by such independent bodies as the Judge Advocate General, the Court of Criminal Appeals, and the Court of Appeals for the Armed Forces, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7862, 864, 866, 867, 869, the review process here lacks structural protections designed to help ensure impartiality.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These structural differences between the military commissions and courts-martial\xe2\x80\x94the concentration of functions, including legal decisionmaking, in a single executive official; the less rigorous standards for composition of the tribunal; and the creation of special review procedures in place of institutions created and regulated by Congress\xe2\x80\x94remove safeguards that are important to the fairness of the proceedings and the independence of the court. Congress has prescribed these guarantees for courts-martial; and no evident practical need explains the departures here. For these reasons the commission cannot be considered regularly constituted under United States law and thus does not satisfy Congress\xe2\x80\x99 requirement that military commissions conform to the law of war.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Apart from these structural issues, moreover, the basic procedures for the commissions deviate from procedures for courts-martial, in violation of \xc2\xa7836(b). As the Court explains, ante, at 51, 61, the Military Commission Order abandons the detailed Military Rules of Evidence, which are modeled on the Federal Rules of Evidence in conformity with \xc2\xa7836(a)\xe2\x80\x99s requirement of presumptive compliance with district-court rules.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Instead, the order imposes just one evidentiary rule: \xe2\x80\x9cEvidence shall be admitted if \xe2\x80\xa6 the evidence would have probative value to a reasonable person,\xe2\x80\x9d MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa76(D)(1). Although it is true some military commissions applied an amorphous evidence standard in the past, see, e.g., 1 Law Reports 117\xe2\x80\x93118 (discussing World War II military commission orders); Exec. Order No. 9185, 7 Fed. Reg. 5103 (1942) (order convening military commission to try Nazi saboteurs), the evidentiary rules for those commissions were adopted before Congress enacted the uniformity requirement of 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(b) as part of the UCMJ, see Act of May 5, 1950, ch. 169, 64 Stat. 107, 120, 149. And while some flexibility may be necessary to permit trial of battlefield captives like Hamdan, military statutes and rules already provide for introduction of deposition testimony for absent witnesses, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7849(d); R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 702, and use of classified information, Military Rule Evid. 505. Indeed, the deposition-testimony provision specifically mentions military commissions and thus is one of the provisions the Government concedes must be followed by the commission at issue. See ante, at 58. That provision authorizes admission of deposition testimony only if the witness is absent for specified reasons, \xc2\xa7849(d)\xe2\x80\x94a requirement that makes no sense if military commissions may consider all probative evidence. Whether or not this conflict renders the rules at issue \xe2\x80\x9ccontrary to or inconsistent with\xe2\x80\x9d the UCMJ under \xc2\xa7836(a), it creates a uniformity problem under \xc2\xa7836(b).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The rule here could permit admission of multiple hearsay and other forms of evidence generally prohibited on grounds of unreliability. Indeed, the commission regulations specifically contemplate admission of unsworn written statements, MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa76(D)(3); and they make no provision for exclusion of coerced declarations save those \xe2\x80\x9cestablished to have been made as a result of torture,\xe2\x80\x9d MCI No. 10, \xc2\xa73(A) (Mar. 24, 2006), available at www.\ndefenselink.mil/news/Mar2006/d20060327MCI10.pdf; cf. Military Rule Evid. 304(c)(3) (generally barring use of statements obtained \xe2\x80\x9cthrough the use of coercion, unlawful influence, or unlawful inducement\xe2\x80\x9d); 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7831(d) (same). Besides, even if evidence is deemed nonprobative by the presiding officer at Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial, the military-commission members still may view it. In another departure from court-martial practice the military commission members may object to the presiding officer\xe2\x80\x99s evidence rulings and determine themselves, by majority vote, whether to admit the evidence. MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa76(D)(1); cf. R.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M. 801(a)(4), (e)(1) (providing that the military judge at a court-martial determines all questions of law).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As the Court explains, the Government has made no demonstration of practical need for these special rules and procedures, either in this particular case or as to the military commissions in general, ante, at 59\xe2\x80\x9361; nor is any such need self-evident. For all the Government\xe2\x80\x99s regulations and submissions reveal, it would be feasible for most, if not all, of the conventional military evidence rules and procedures to be followed.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, as presently structured, Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission exceeds the bounds Congress has placed on the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority in \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7836 and 821 of the UCMJ. Because Congress has prescribed these limits, Congress can change them, requiring a new analysis consistent with the Constitution and other governing laws. At this time, however, we must apply the standards Congress has provided. By those standards the military commission is deficient.

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In light of the conclusion that the military commission here is unauthorized under the UCMJ, I see no need to consider several further issues addressed in the plurality opinion by Justice Stevens and the dissent by Justice Thomas.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, I would not decide whether Common Article 3\xe2\x80\x99s standard\xe2\x80\x94a \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples,\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T., at 3320 (\xc2\xb6(1)(d))\xe2\x80\x94necessarily requires that the accused have the right to be present at all stages of a criminal trial. As Justice Stevens explains, Military Commission Order No. 1 authorizes exclusion of the accused from the proceedings if the presiding officer determines that, among other things, protection of classified information so requires. See \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa76(B)(3), (D)(5); ante, at 50. Justice Stevens observes that these regulations create the possibility of a conviction and sentence based on evidence Hamdan has not seen or heard\xe2\x80\x94a possibility the plurality is correct to consider troubling. Ante, at 71\xe2\x80\x9372, n. 67 (collecting cases); see also In re Oliver, 333 U. S. 257, 277 (1948) (finding \xe2\x80\x9cno support for sustaining petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s conviction of contempt of court upon testimony given in petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s absence\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As the dissent by Justice Thomas points out, however, the regulations bar the presiding officer from admitting secret evidence if doing so would deprive the accused of a \xe2\x80\x9cfull and fair trial.\xe2\x80\x9d MCO No. 1, \xc2\xa76(D)(5)(b); see also post, at 47. This fairness determination, moreover, is unambiguously subject to judicial review under the DTA. See \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(D)(i), 119 Stat. 2743 (allowing review of compliance with the \xe2\x80\x9cstandards and procedures\xe2\x80\x9d in Military Commission Order No. 1). The evidentiary proceedings at Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s trial have yet to commence, and it remains to be seen whether he will suffer any prejudicial exclusion.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There should be reluctance, furthermore, to reach unnecessarily the question whether, as the plurality seems to conclude, ante, at 70, Article 75 of Protocol I to the Geneva Conventions is binding law notwithstanding the earlier decision by our Government not to accede to the Protocol. For all these reasons, and without detracting from the importance of the right of presence, I would rely on other deficiencies noted here and in the opinion by the Court\xe2\x80\x94deficiencies that relate to the structure and procedure of the commission and that inevitably will affect the proceedings\xe2\x80\x94as the basis for finding the military commissions lack authorization under 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836 and fail to be regularly constituted under Common Article 3 and \xc2\xa7821.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I likewise see no need to address the validity of the conspiracy charge against Hamdan\xe2\x80\x94an issue addressed at length in Part V of Justice Stevens\xe2\x80\x99 opinion and in Part II\xe2\x80\x93C of Justice Thomas\xe2\x80\x99 dissent. See ante, at 36\xe2\x80\x9349; post, at 12\xe2\x80\x9328. In light of the conclusion that the military commissions at issue are unauthorized Congress may choose to provide further guidance in this area. Congress, not the Court, is the branch in the better position to undertake the \xe2\x80\x9csensitive task of establishing a principle not inconsistent with the national interest or international justice.\xe2\x80\x9d Banco Nacional de Cuba v. Sabbatino, 376 U. S. 398, 428 (1964).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, for the same reason, I express no view on the merits of other limitations on military commissions described as elements of the common law of war in Part V of Justice Stevens\xe2\x80\x99 opinion. See ante, at 31\xe2\x80\x9336, 48\xe2\x80\x9349; post, at 6\xe2\x80\x9312.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0With these observations I join the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion with the exception of Parts V and VI\xe2\x80\x93D\xe2\x80\x93iv.

\n
\n
\n
\n
\n\n
\n548 U. S. ____ (2006)
\n548 U. S. ____ (2006)
\n548 U. S. ____ (2006)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 05-184
\n
\n

SALIM AHMED HAMDAN, PETITIONER v. DONALD\nH. RUMSFELD, SECRETARY OF DEFENSE, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the district of columbia circuit

\n

[June 29, 2006]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer, with whom Justice Kennedy, Justice Souter, and Justice Ginsburg join, concurring.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The dissenters say that today\xe2\x80\x99s decision would \xe2\x80\x9csorely hamper the President\xe2\x80\x99s ability to confront and defeat a new and deadly enemy.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 29 (opinion of Thomas, J.). They suggest that it undermines our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s ability to \xe2\x80\x9cpreven[t] future attacks\xe2\x80\x9d of the grievous sort that we have already suffered. Post, at 48. That claim leads me to state briefly what I believe the majority sets forth both explicitly and implicitly at greater length. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion ultimately rests upon a single ground: Congress has not issued the Executive a \xe2\x80\x9cblank check.\xe2\x80\x9d Cf. Hamdi v. Rumsfeld, 542 U. S. 507, 536 (2004) (plurality opinion). Indeed, Congress has denied the President the legisla-\ntive authority to create military commissions of the kind at issue here. Nothing prevents the President from returning to Congress to seek the authority he believes necessary.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Where, as here, no emergency prevents consultation with Congress, judicial insistence upon that consultation does not weaken our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s ability to deal with danger. To the contrary, that insistence strengthens the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s ability to determine\xe2\x80\x94through democratic means\xe2\x80\x94how best to do so. The Constitution places its faith in those democratic means. Our Court today simply does the same.

\n
\n
\n
\n
\n\n
\nALITO, J., DISSENTING
\nHAMDAN V. RUMSFELD
\n548 U. S. ____ (2006)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 05-184
\n
\n

SALIM AHMED HAMDAN, PETITIONER v. DONALD\nH. RUMSFELD, SECRETARY OF DEFENSE, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the district of columbia circuit

\n

[June 29, 2006]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Alito, with whom Justices Scalia and Thomas join in Parts I\xe2\x80\x93III, dissenting.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the reasons set out in Justice Scalia\xe2\x80\x99s dissent, which I join, I would hold that we lack jurisdiction. On the merits, I join Justice Thomas\xe2\x80\x99 dissent with the exception of Parts I, II\xe2\x80\x93C\xe2\x80\x931, and III\xe2\x80\x93B\xe2\x80\x932, which concern matters that I find unnecessary to reach. I add the following comments to provide a further explanation of my reasons for disagreeing with the holding of the Court.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The holding of the Court, as I understand it, rests on the following reasoning. A military commission is lawful only if it is authorized by 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821; this provision permits the use of a commission to try \xe2\x80\x9coffenders or offenses\xe2\x80\x9d that \xe2\x80\x9cby statute or by the law of war may be tried by\xe2\x80\x9d such a commission; because no statute provides that an offender such as petitioner or an offense such as the one with which he is charged may be tried by a military commission, he may be tried by military commission only if the trial is authorized by \xe2\x80\x9cthe law of war\xe2\x80\x9d; the Geneva Conventions are part of the law of war; and Common Article 3 of the Conventions prohibits petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s trial because the commission before which he would be tried is not \xe2\x80\x9ca regularly constituted court,\xe2\x80\x9d Third Geneva Convention, Art. 3, \xc2\xb61(d), Relative to the Treatment of Prisoners of War, Aug. 12, 1949, [1955] 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T. 3316, 3320, T.\xc2\xa0I.\xc2\xa0A.\xc2\xa0S. No. 3364. I disagree with this holding because petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s commission is \xe2\x80\x9ca regularly constituted court.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Common Article 3 provides as follows:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cIn the case of armed conflict not of an international character occurring in the territory of one of the High Contracting Parties, each Party to the conflict shall\nbe bound to apply, as a minimum, the following\nprovisions:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(1)\xe2\x80\x82\xe2\x80\xa6 [T]he following acts are and shall remain prohibited \xe2\x80\xa6 :

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(d)\xe2\x80\x82[T]he passing of sentences and the carrying out of executions without previous judgment pronounced by a regularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 3318\xe2\x80\x933320 (emphasis added).

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Common Article 3 thus imposes three requirements. Sentences may be imposed only by (1) a \xe2\x80\x9ccourt\xe2\x80\x9d (2) that is \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d and (3) that affords \xe2\x80\x9call the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 3320.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I see no need here to comment extensively on the meaning of the first and third requirements. The first requirement is largely self-explanatory, and, with respect to the third, I note only that on its face it imposes a uniform international standard that does not vary from signatory to signatory.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The second element (\xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d) is the one on which the Court relies, and I interpret this element to require that the court be appointed or established in accordance with the appointing country\xe2\x80\x99s domestic law.
