I agree with the Court, see ante, at 69, n. 64, that, as used in Common Article 3, the term \xe2\x80\x9cregularly\xe2\x80\x9d is synonymous with \xe2\x80\x9cproperly.\xe2\x80\x9d The term \xe2\x80\x9cconstitute\xe2\x80\x9d means \xe2\x80\x9cappoint,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cset up,\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cestablish,\xe2\x80\x9d Webster\xe2\x80\x99s Third New International Dictionary 486 (1961), and therefore \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d means properly appointed, set up, or established. Our cases repeatedly use the phrases \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cproperly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d in this sense. See, e.g., Hamdi v. Rumsfeld, 542 U. S. 507, 538 (2004) (plurality opinion of O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, J.); Nguyen v. United States, 539 U. S. 69, 83 (2003); Ryder v. United States, 515 U. S. 177, 187 (1995); Williams v. Bruffy, 96 U. S. 176, 185 (1878).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In order to determine whether a court has been properly appointed, set up, or established, it is necessary to refer to a body of law that governs such matters. I interpret Common Article 3 as looking to the domestic law of the appointing country because I am not aware of any international law standard regarding the way in which such a court must be appointed, set up, or established, and because different countries with different government structures handle this matter differently. Accordingly, \xe2\x80\x9ca regularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x9d is a court that has been appointed, set up, or established in accordance with the domestic law of the appointing country.

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In contrast to this interpretation, the opinions supporting the judgment today hold that the military commission before which petitioner would be tried is not \xe2\x80\x9ca regularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x9d (a) because \xe2\x80\x9cno evident practical need explains\xe2\x80\x9d why its \xe2\x80\x9cstructure and composition \xe2\x80\xa6 deviate from conventional court-martial standards,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 11 (Kennedy, J., concurring in part); see also ante, at 69\xe2\x80\x9370 (Opinion of the Court); and (b) because, contrary to 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(b), the procedures specified for use in the proceeding before the military commission impermissibly differ from those provided under the Uniform Code of Military Justice (UCMJ) for use by courts-martial, ante, at 52\xe2\x80\x9362 (Opinion of the Court); ante, at 16\xe2\x80\x9318 (Kennedy, J., concurring in part). I do not believe that either of these grounds is sound.

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I see no basis for the Court\xe2\x80\x99s holding that a military commission cannot be regarded as \xe2\x80\x9ca regularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x9d unless it is similar in structure and composition to a regular military court or unless there is an \xe2\x80\x9cevident practical need\xe2\x80\x9d for the divergence. There is no reason why a court that differs in structure or composition from an ordinary military court must be viewed as having been improperly constituted. Tribunals that vary significantly in structure, composition, and procedures may all be \xe2\x80\x9cregularly\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cproperly\xe2\x80\x9d constituted. Consider, for example, a municipal court, a state trial court of general jurisdiction, an Article I federal trial court, a federal district court, and an international court, such as the International Criminal Tribunal for the Former Yugoslavia. Although these courts are \xe2\x80\x9cdifferently constituted\xe2\x80\x9d and differ substantially in many other respects, they are all \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If Common Article 3 had been meant to require trial before a country\xe2\x80\x99s military courts or courts that are similar in structure and composition, the drafters almost certainly would have used language that expresses that thought more directly. Other provisions of the Convention Relative to the Treatment of Prisoners of War refer expressly to the ordinary military courts and expressly prescribe the \xe2\x80\x9cuniformity principle\xe2\x80\x9d that Justice Kennedy sees in Common Article 3, see ante, at 8\xe2\x80\x939. Article 84 provides that \xe2\x80\x9c[a] prisoner of war shall be tried only by a military court, unless the existing laws of the Detaining Power expressly permit the civil courts to try a member of the armed forces of the Detaining Power in respect of the particular offence alleged to have been committed by the prisoner of war.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S. T., at 3382. Article 87 states that \xe2\x80\x9c[p]risoners of war may not be sentenced by the military authorities and courts of the Detaining Power to any penalties except those provided for in respect of members of the armed forces of the said Power who have committed the same acts.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 3384. Similarly, Article 66 of the Geneva Convention Relative to the Treatment of Civilian Persons in Time of War\xe2\x80\x94a provision to which the Court looks for guidance in interpreting Common Article 3, see ante at 69\xe2\x80\x94expressly provides that civilians charged with committing crimes in occupied territory may be handed over by the occupying power \xe2\x80\x9cto its properly constituted, non-political military courts, on condition that the said courts sit in the occupied country.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S. T. 3516, 3558\xe2\x80\x933560, T.\xc2\xa0I.\xc2\xa0A.\xc2\xa0S. No. 3365. If Common Article 3 had been meant to incorporate a \xe2\x80\x9cuniformity principle,\xe2\x80\x9d it presumably would have used language like that employed in the provisions noted above. For these reasons, I cannot agree with the Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that the military commission at issue here is not a \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court\xe2\x80\x9d because its structure and composition differ from those of a court-martial.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Contrary to the suggestion of the Court, see ante, at 69, the commentary on Article 66 of Fourth Geneva Convention does not undermine this conclusion. As noted, Article 66 permits an occupying power to try civilians in its \xe2\x80\x9cproperly constituted, non-political military courts,\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T., at 3558. The commentary on this provision states:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cThe courts are to be \xe2\x80\x98regularly constituted\xe2\x80\x99. This wording definitely excludes all special tribunals. It is the ordinary military courts of the Occupying Power which will be competent.\xe2\x80\x9d 4 Int\xe2\x80\x99l Comm. of Red Cross, Commentary: Geneva Convention Relative to the Protection of Civilian Persons in Time of War 340 (1958) (hereinafter GCIV Commentary).

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court states that this commentary \xe2\x80\x9cdefines \xe2\x80\x98\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x99 tribunals to include \xe2\x80\x98ordinary military courts\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98definitely exclud[e] all special tribunals.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 69 (alteration in original). This much is clear from the commentary itself. Yet the mere statement that a military court is a regularly constituted tribunal is of no help in addressing petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claim that his commission is not such a tribunal. As for the commentary\xe2\x80\x99s mention of \xe2\x80\x9cspecial tribunals,\xe2\x80\x9d it is doubtful whether we should take this gloss on Article 66\xe2\x80\x94which forbids an occupying power from trying civilians in courts set up specially for that purpose\xe2\x80\x94to tell us much about the very different context addressed by Common Article 3.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0But even if Common Article 3 recognizes this prohibition on \xe2\x80\x9cspecial tribunals,\xe2\x80\x9d that prohibition does not cover petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s tribunal. If \xe2\x80\x9cspecial\xe2\x80\x9d means anything in contradistinction to \xe2\x80\x9cregular,\xe2\x80\x9d it would be in the sense of \xe2\x80\x9cspecial\xe2\x80\x9d as \xe2\x80\x9crelating to a single thing,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cregular\xe2\x80\x9d as \xe2\x80\x9cuniform in course, practice, or occurrence.\xe2\x80\x9d Webster\xe2\x80\x99s Third New International Dictionary 2186, 1913. Insofar as respondents propose to conduct the tribunals according to the procedures of Military Commission Order No. 1 and orders promulgated thereunder\xe2\x80\x94and nobody has suggested respondents intend otherwise\xe2\x80\x94then it seems that petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s tribunal, like the hundreds of others respondents propose to conduct, is very much regular and not at all special.

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I also disagree with the Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s military commission is \xe2\x80\x9cillegal,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 62, because its procedures allegedly do not comply with 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836. Even if \xc2\xa7836(b), unlike Common Article 3, does impose at least a limited uniformity requirement amongst the tribunals contemplated by the UCMJ, but see ante, at 35 (Thomas, J., dissenting), and even if it is assumed for the sake of argument that some of the procedures specified in Military Commission Order No. 1 impermissibly deviate from court-martial procedures, it does not follow that the military commissions created by that order are not \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d or that trying petitioner before such a commission would be inconsistent with the law of war. If Congress enacted a statute requiring the federal district courts to follow a procedure that is unconstitutional, the statute would be invalid, but the district courts would not. Likewise, if some of the procedures that may be used in military commission proceedings are improper, the appropriate remedy is to proscribe the use of those particular procedures, not to outlaw the commissions. I see no justification for striking down the entire commission structure simply because it is possible that petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s trial might involve the use of some procedure that is improper.

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Returning to the three elements of Common Article 3\xe2\x80\x94(1) a court, (2) that is appointed, set up, and established in compliance with domestic law, and (3) that respects universally recognized fundamental rights\xe2\x80\x94I conclude that all of these elements are satisfied in this case.

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, the commissions qualify as courts.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, the commissions were appointed, set up, and established pursuant to an order of the President, just like the commission in Ex parte Quirin, 317 U. S. 1 (1942), and the Court acknowledges that Quirin recognized that the statutory predecessor of 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821 \xe2\x80\x9cpreserved\xe2\x80\x9d the President\xe2\x80\x99s power \xe2\x80\x9cto convene military commissions,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 29. Although Justice Kennedy concludes that \xe2\x80\x9can acceptable degree of independence from the Executive is necessary to render a commission \xe2\x80\x98regularly constituted\xe2\x80\x99 by the standards of our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s system of justice,\xe2\x80\x9d ante at 9\xe2\x80\x9310, he offers no support for this proposition (which in any event seems to be more about fairness or integrity than regularity). The commission in Quirin was certainly no more independent from the Executive than the commissions at issue here, and 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7821 and 836 do not speak to this issue.[Footnote 1]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, the commission procedures, taken as a whole, and including the availability of review by a United States Court of Appeals and by this Court, do not provide a basis for deeming the commissions to be illegitimate. The Court questions the following two procedural rules: the rule allowing the Secretary of Defense to change the governing rules \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98from time to time\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (which does not rule out mid-trial changes), see ante, at 70, n. 65 (Opinion of the Court); ante, at 9\xe2\x80\x9310 (Kennedy, J., concurring in part), and the rule that permits the admission of any evidence that would have \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98probative value to a reasonable person\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (which departs from our legal system\xe2\x80\x99s usual rules of evidence), see ante, at 51, 60 (Opinion of the Court); ante, at 16\xe2\x80\x9318 (Kennedy, J., concurring in part).[Footnote 2] Neither\nof these two rules undermines the legitimacy of the\ncommissions.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Surely the entire commission structure cannot be stricken merely because it is possible that the governing rules might be changed during the course of one or more proceedings. If a change is made and applied during the course of an ongoing proceeding and if the accused is found guilty, the validity of that procedure can be considered in the review proceeding for that case. After all, not every midtrial change will be prejudicial. A midtrial change might amend the governing rules in a way that is inconsequential or actually favorable to the accused.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As for the standard for the admission of evidence at commission proceedings, the Court does not suggest that this rule violates the international standard incorporated into Common Article 3 (\xe2\x80\x9cthe judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples,\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T., at 3320). Rules of evidence differ from country to country, and much of the world does not follow aspects of our evidence rules, such as the general prohibition against the admission of hearsay. See, e.g., Blumenthal, Shedding Some Light on Calls for Hearsay Reform: Civil Law Hearsay Rules in Historical and Modern Perspective, 13 Pace Int\xe2\x80\x99l L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 93, 96\xe2\x80\x93101 (2001). If a particular accused claims to have been unfairly prejudiced by the admission of particular evidence, that claim can be reviewed in the review proceeding for that case. It makes no sense to strike down the entire commission structure based on speculation that some evidence might be improperly admitted in some future case.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, I believe that Common Article 3 is satisfied here because the military commissions (1) qualify as courts, (2) that were appointed and established in accordance with domestic law, and (3) any procedural improprieties that might occur in particular cases can be reviewed in those cases.

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The commentary on Common Article 3 supports this interpretation. The commentary on Common Article 3, \xc2\xb61(d), in its entirety states:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c[A]lthough [sentences and executions without a proper trial] were common practice until quite recently, they are nevertheless shocking to the civilized mind\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Sentences and executions without previous trial are too open to error. \xe2\x80\x98Summary justice\xe2\x80\x99 may be effective on account of the fear it arouses \xe2\x80\xa6 , but it adds too many further innocent victims to all the other innocent victims of the conflict. All civilized nations surround the administration of justice with safeguards aimed at eliminating the possibility of judicial errors. The Convention has rightly proclaimed that it is essential to do this even in time of war. We must be very clear about one point: it is only \xe2\x80\x98summary\xe2\x80\x99 justice which it is intended to prohibit. No sort of immunity is given to anyone under this provision. There is nothing in it to prevent a person presumed to be guilty from being arrested and so placed in a position where he can do no further harm; and it leaves intact the right of the State to prosecute, sentence and punish according to the law.\xe2\x80\x9d GCIV Commentary 39 (emphasis added).

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It seems clear that the commissions at issue here meet this standard. Whatever else may be said about the system that was created by Military Commission Order No. 1 and augmented by the Detainee Treatment Act, \xc2\xa71005(e)(1), 119 Stat. 2742, this system\xe2\x80\x94which features formal trial procedures, multiple levels of administrative review, and the opportunity for review by a United States Court of Appeals and by this Court\xe2\x80\x94does not dispense \xe2\x80\x9csummary justice.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For these reasons, I respectfully dissent.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0Section 821 looks to the \xe2\x80\x9claw of war,\xe2\x80\x9d not separation of powers issues. And \xc2\xa7836, as Justice Kennedy notes, concerns procedures, not structure, see ante, at 10.

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0The plurality, but not Justice Kennedy, suggests that the commission rules are improper insofar as they allow a defendant to be denied access to evidence under some circumstances. See, ante, at 70\xe2\x80\x9372. But here too, if this procedure is used in a particular case and the accused is convicted, the validity of this procedure can be challenged in the review proceeding in that case. In that context, both the asserted need for the procedure and its impact on the accused can be analyzed in concrete terms.

\n\n
\n
\n
\n
\n\n
\nTHOMAS, J., DISSENTING
\nHAMDAN V. RUMSFELD
\n548 U. S. ____ (2006)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 05-184
\n
\n

SALIM AHMED HAMDAN, PETITIONER v. DONALD\nH. RUMSFELD, SECRETARY OF DEFENSE, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the district of columbia circuit

\n

[June 29, 2006]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas, with whom Justice Scalia joins, and with whom Justice Alito joins in all but Parts I, II\xe2\x80\x93C\xe2\x80\x931, and III\xe2\x80\x93B\xe2\x80\x932, dissenting.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the reasons set forth in Justice Scalia\xe2\x80\x99s dissent, it is clear that this Court lacks jurisdiction to entertain petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claims, see ante, at 1\xe2\x80\x9311. The Court having concluded otherwise, it is appropriate to respond to the Court\xe2\x80\x99s resolution of the merits of petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claims because its opinion openly flouts our well-established duty to respect the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s judgment in matters of military operations and foreign affairs. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s evident belief that it is qualified to pass on the \xe2\x80\x9c[m]ilitary necessity,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 48, of the Commander in Chief\xe2\x80\x99s decision to employ a particular form of force against our enemies is so antithetical to our constitutional structure that it simply cannot go unanswered. I respectfully dissent.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Our review of petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claims arises in the context of the President\xe2\x80\x99s wartime exercise of his commander-in-chief authority in conjunction with the complete support of Congress. Accordingly, it is important to take measure of the respective roles the Constitution assigns to the three branches of our Government in the conduct of war.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As I explained in Hamdi v. Rumsfeld, 542 U. S. 507 (2004), the structural advantages attendant to the Executive Branch\xe2\x80\x94namely, the decisiveness, \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98activity, secrecy, and dispatch\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d that flow from the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98unity,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 581 (dissenting opinion) (quoting The Federalist No. 70, p. 472 (J. Cooke ed. 1961) (A. Hamilton))\xe2\x80\x94led the Founders to conclude that the \xe2\x80\x9cPresident ha[s] primary responsibility\xe2\x80\x94along with the necessary power\xe2\x80\x94to protect the national security and to conduct the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s foreign relations.\xe2\x80\x9d 542 U. S., at 580. Consistent with this conclusion, the Constitution vests in the President \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he executive Power,\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0II, \xc2\xa71, provides that he \xe2\x80\x9cshall be Commander in Chief\xe2\x80\x9d of the Armed Forces, \xc2\xa72, and places in him the power to recognize foreign governments, \xc2\xa73. This Court has observed that these provisions confer upon the President broad constitutional authority to protect the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s security in the manner he deems fit. See, e.g., Prize Cases, 2 Black 635, 668 (1863) (\xe2\x80\x9cIf a war be made by invasion of a foreign nation, the President is not only authorized but bound to resist force by force \xe2\x80\xa6 without waiting for any special legislative authority\xe2\x80\x9d); Fleming v. Page, 9 How. 603, 615 (1850) (acknowledging that the President has the authority to \xe2\x80\x9cemploy [the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s Armed Forces] in the manner he may deem most effectual to harass and conquer and subdue the enemy\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Congress, to be sure, has a substantial and essential role in both foreign affairs and national security. But \xe2\x80\x9cCongress cannot anticipate and legislate with regard to every possible action the President may find it necessary to take or every possible situation in which he might act,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c[s]uch failure of Congress \xe2\x80\xa6 does not, \xe2\x80\x98especially \xe2\x80\xa6 in the areas of foreign policy and national security,\xe2\x80\x99 imply \xe2\x80\x98congressional disapproval\xe2\x80\x99 of action taken by the Executive.\xe2\x80\x9d Dames & Moore v. Regan, 453 U. S. 654, 678 (1981) (quoting Haig v. Agee, 453 U. S. 280, 291 (1981)). Rather, in these domains, the fact that Congress has provided the President with broad authorities does not imply\xe2\x80\x94and the Judicial Branch should not infer\xe2\x80\x94that Congress intended to deprive him of particular powers not specifically enumerated. See Dames & Moore, 453 U. S., at 678 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he enactment of legislation closely related to the question of the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority in a particular case which evinces legislative intent to accord the President broad discretion may be considered to invite measures on independent presidential responsibility\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0When \xe2\x80\x9cthe President acts pursuant to an express or implied authorization from Congress,\xe2\x80\x9d his actions are \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98supported by the strongest of presumptions and the widest latitude of judicial interpretation, and the burden of persuasion \xe2\x80\xa6 rest[s] heavily upon any who might attack it.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 668 (quoting Youngstown Sheet & Tube Co. v. Sawyer, 343 U. S. 579, 637 (1952) (Jackson, J., concurring)). Accordingly, in the very context that we address today, this Court has concluded that \xe2\x80\x9cthe detention and trial of petitioners\xe2\x80\x94ordered by the President in the declared exercise of his powers as Commander in Chief of the Army in time of war and of grave public danger\xe2\x80\x94are not to be set aside by the courts without the clear conviction that they are in conflict with the Constitution or laws of Congress constitutionally enacted.\xe2\x80\x9d Ex parte Quirin, 317 U. S. 1, 25 (1942).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Under this framework, the President\xe2\x80\x99s decision to try Hamdan before a military commission for his involvement with al Qaeda is entitled to a heavy measure of deference. In the present conflict, Congress has authorized the President \xe2\x80\x9cto use all necessary and appropriate force against those nations, organizations, or persons he determines planned, authorized, committed, or aided the terrorist attacks that occurred on September 11, 2001 \xe2\x80\xa6 in order to prevent any future acts of international terrorism against the United States by such nations, organizations or persons.\xe2\x80\x9d Authorization for Use of Military Force (AUMF) 115 Stat. 224, note following 50 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71541 (2000 ed., Supp. III) (emphasis added). As a plurality of the Court observed in Hamdi, the \xe2\x80\x9ccapture, detention, and trial of unlawful combatants, by \xe2\x80\x98universal agreement and practice,\xe2\x80\x99 are \xe2\x80\x98important incident[s] of war,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Hamdi, 542 U. S., at 518 (quoting Quirin, supra, at 28, 30; emphasis added), and are therefore \xe2\x80\x9can exercise of the \xe2\x80\x98necessary and appropriate force\xe2\x80\x99 Congress has authorized the President to use.\xe2\x80\x9d Hamdi, 542 U. S., at 518; id., at 587 (Thomas, J., dissenting). Hamdi\xe2\x80\x99s observation that military commissions are included within the AUMF\xe2\x80\x99s authorization is supported by this Court\xe2\x80\x99s previous recognition that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]n important incident to the conduct of war is the adoption of measures by the military commander, not only to repel and defeat the enemy, but to seize and subject to disciplinary measures those enemies who, in their attempt to thwart or impede our military effort, have violated the law of war.\xe2\x80\x9d In re Yamashita, 327 U. S. 1, 11 (1946); see also Quirin, supra, at 28\xe2\x80\x9329; Madsen v. Kinsella, 343 U. S. 341, 354, n. 20 (1952) (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[T]he military commission \xe2\x80\xa6 is an institution of the greatest importance in the period of war and should be preserved\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting S.\xc2\xa0Rep. No. 229, 63d Cong., 2d Sess., 53 (1914) (testimony of Gen. Crowder))).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Although the Court concedes the legitimacy of the President\xe2\x80\x99s use of military commissions in certain circumstances, ante, at 28, it suggests that the AUMF has no bearing on the scope of the President\xe2\x80\x99s power to utilize military commissions in the present conflict, ante, at 29\xe2\x80\x9330. Instead, the Court determines the scope of this power based exclusively on Article 21 of the Uniform Code of Military Justice (UCMJ), 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821, the successor to Article 15 of the Articles of War, which Quirin held \xe2\x80\x9cauthorized trial of offenses against the law of war before [military] commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d 317 U. S., at 29. As I shall discuss below, Article 21 alone supports the use of commissions here. Nothing in the language of Article 21, however, suggests that it outlines the entire reach of congressional authorization of military commissions in all conflicts\xe2\x80\x94quite the contrary, the language of Article 21 presupposes the existence of military commissions under an independent basis of authorization.[Footnote 1] Indeed, consistent with Hamdi\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that the AUMF itself authorizes the trial of unlawful combatants, the original sanction for military commissions historically derived from congressional authorization of \xe2\x80\x9cthe initiation of war\xe2\x80\x9d with its attendant authorization of \xe2\x80\x9cthe employment of all necessary and proper agencies for its due prosecution.\xe2\x80\x9d W. Winthrop, Military Law and Precedents 831 (2d ed. 1920) (hereinafter Winthrop). Accordingly, congressional authorization for military commissions pertaining to the instant conflict derives not only from Article 21 of the UCMJ, but also from the more recent, and broader, authorization contained in the AUMF.[Footnote 2]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I note the Court\xe2\x80\x99s error respecting the AUMF not because it is necessary to my resolution of this case\xe2\x80\x94Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission can plainly be sustained solely under Article 21\xe2\x80\x94but to emphasize the complete congressional sanction of the President\xe2\x80\x99s exercise of his commander-in-chief authority to conduct the present war. In such circumstances, as previously noted, our duty to defer to the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s military and foreign policy judgment is at its zenith; it does not countenance the kind of second-guessing the Court repeatedly engages in today. Military and foreign policy judgments

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98are and should be undertaken only by those directly responsible to the people whose welfare they advance or imperil. They are decisions of a kind for which the Judiciary has neither aptitude, facilities nor responsibility and which has long been held to belong in the domain of political power not subject to judicial intrusion or inquiry.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Hamdi, supra, at 582\xe2\x80\x93583 (Thomas, J., dissenting) (quoting Chicago & Southern Air Lines, Inc. v. Waterman S. S. Corp., 333 U. S. 103, 111 (1948)).

\n

\nIt is within this framework that the lawfulness of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission should be examined.

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The plurality accurately describes some aspects of the history of military commissions and the prerequisites for their use. Thus, I do not dispute that military commissions have historically been \xe2\x80\x9cused in three [different] situations,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 31\xe2\x80\x9332, and that the only situation relevant to the instant case is the use of military commissions \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98to seize and subject to disciplinary measures those enemies who \xe2\x80\xa6 have violated the law of war,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 32 (quoting Quirin, supra, at 28\xe2\x80\x9329). Similarly, I agree with the plurality that Winthrop\xe2\x80\x99s treatise sets forth the four relevant considerations for determining the scope of a military commission\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction, considerations relating to the (1) time and (2) place of the offense, (3) the status of the offender, and (4) the nature of the offense charged. Winthrop 836\xe2\x80\x93840. The Executive has easily satisfied these considerations here. The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s contrary conclusion rests upon an incomplete accounting and an unfaithful application of those considerations.

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The first two considerations are that a law-of-war military commission may only assume jurisdiction of \xe2\x80\x9coffences committed within the field of the command of the convening commander,\xe2\x80\x9d and that such offenses \xe2\x80\x9cmust have been committed within the period of the war.\xe2\x80\x9d See id., at 836, 837; ante, at 33. Here, as evidenced by Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s charging document, the Executive has determined that the theater of the present conflict includes \xe2\x80\x9cAfghanistan, Pakistan and other countries\xe2\x80\x9d where al Qaeda has established training camps, App. to Pet. for Cert. 64a, and that the duration of that conflict dates back (at least) to Usama bin Laden\xe2\x80\x99s August 1996 \xe2\x80\x9cDeclaration of Jihad Against the Americans,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid. Under the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s description of the conflict, then, every aspect of the charge, which alleges overt acts in \xe2\x80\x9cAfghanistan, Pakistan, Yemen and other countries\xe2\x80\x9d taking place from 1996 to 2001, satisfies the temporal and geographic prerequisites for the exercise of law-of-war military commission jurisdiction. Id., at 65a\xe2\x80\x9367a. And these judgments pertaining to the scope of the theater and duration of the present conflict are committed solely to the President in the exercise of his commander-in-chief authority. See Prize Cases, 2 Black, at 670 (concluding that the President\xe2\x80\x99s commander-in-chief judgment about the nature of a particular conflict was \xe2\x80\x9ca question to be decided by him, and this Court must be governed by the decisions and acts of the political department of the Government to which this power was entrusted\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nevertheless, the plurality concludes that the legality of the charge against Hamdan is doubtful because \xe2\x80\x9cHamdan is charged not with an overt act for which he was caught redhanded in a theater of war \xe2\x80\xa6 but with an agreement the inception of which long predated \xe2\x80\xa6 the [relevant armed conflict].\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 48 (emphasis in original). The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s willingness to second-guess the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s judgments in this context, based upon little more than its unsupported assertions, constitutes an unprecedented departure from the traditionally limited role of the courts with respect to war and an unwarranted intrusion on executive authority. And even if such second-guessing were appropriate, the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s attempt to do so is unpersuasive.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As an initial matter, the plurality relies upon the date of the AUMF\xe2\x80\x99s enactment to determine the beginning point for the \xe2\x80\x9cperiod of the war,\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 836, thereby suggesting that petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s commission does not have jurisdiction to try him for offenses committed prior to the AUMF\xe2\x80\x99s enactment. Ante, at 34\xe2\x80\x9336, 48. But this suggestion betrays the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s unfamiliarity with the realities of warfare and its willful blindness to our precedents. The starting point of the present conflict (or indeed any conflict) is not determined by congressional enactment, but rather by the initiation of hostilities. See Prize Cases, supra, at 668 (recognizing that war may be initiated by \xe2\x80\x9cinvasion of a foreign nation,\xe2\x80\x9d and that such initiation, and the President\xe2\x80\x99s response, usually precedes congressional action). Thus, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 enactment of the AUMF did not mark the beginning of this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s conflict with al Qaeda, but instead authorized the President to use force in the midst of an ongoing conflict. Moreover, while the President\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cwar powers\xe2\x80\x9d may not have been activated until the AUMF was passed, ante, 35, n. 31, the date of such activation has never been used to determine the scope of a military commission\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction.[Footnote 3] Instead, the traditional rule is that \xe2\x80\x9c[o]ffenses committed before a formal declaration of war or before the declaration of martial law may be tried by military commission.\xe2\x80\x9d Green, The Military Commission, 42 Am. J. Int\xe2\x80\x99l L. 832, 848 (1948) (hereinafter Green); see also C. Howland, Digest of Opinions of the Judge-Advocates General of the Army 1067 (1912) (hereinafter Howland) (\xe2\x80\x9cA military commission \xe2\x80\xa6 exercising \xe2\x80\xa6 jurisdiction \xe2\x80\xa6 under the laws of war \xe2\x80\xa6 may take cognizance of offenses committed, during the war, before the initiation of the military government or martial law\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis in original));[Footnote 4] cf. Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 13 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe extent to which the power to prosecute violations of the law of war shall be exercised before peace is declared rests, not with the courts, but with the political branch of the Government\xe2\x80\x9d). Consistent with this principle, on facts virtually identical to those here, a military commission tried Julius Otto Kuehn for conspiring with Japanese officials to betray the United States Fleet to the Imperial Japanese Government prior to its attack on Pearl Harbor. Green 848.[Footnote 5]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Moreover, the President\xe2\x80\x99s determination that the present conflict dates at least to 1996 is supported by overwhelming evidence. According to the State Department, al Qaeda declared war on the United States as early as August 1996. See Dept. of State Fact Sheet: Usama bin Ladin (Aug. 21, 1998); Dept. of State Fact Sheet: The Charges against International Terrorist Usama Bin Laden (Dec. 20, 2000); cf. Prize Cases, 2 Black, at 668 (recognizing that a state of war exists even if \xe2\x80\x9cthe declaration of it be unilateral\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis in original)). In February 1998, al Qaeda leadership issued another statement ordering the indiscriminate\xe2\x80\x94and, even under the laws of war as applied to legitimate nation-states, plainly illegal\xe2\x80\x94killing of American civilians and military personnel alike. See Jihad Against Jews and Crusaders: World Islamic Front Statement 2 (Feb. 23, 1998), in Y. Alexander & M. Swetnam, Usama bin Laden\xe2\x80\x99s al-Qaida: Profile of a Terrorist Network, App. 1B (2001) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe ruling to kill the Americans and their allies\xe2\x80\x94civilians and military\xe2\x80\x94is an individual duty for every Muslim who can do it in any country in which it is possible to do it\xe2\x80\x9d). This was not mere rhetoric; even before September 11, 2001, al Qaeda was involved in the bombing of the World Trade Center in New York City in 1993, the bombing of the Khobar Towers in Saudi Arabia in 1996, the bombing of the U. S. Embassies in Kenya and Tanzania in 1998, and the attack on the U. S.\xc2\xa0S. Cole in Yemen in 2000. See id., at 1. In response to these incidents, the United States \xe2\x80\x9cattack[ed] facilities belonging to Usama bin Ladin\xe2\x80\x99s network\xe2\x80\x9d as early as 1998. Dept. of State Fact Sheet: Usama bin Ladin (Aug. 21, 1998). Based on the foregoing, the President\xe2\x80\x99s judgment\xe2\x80\x94that the present conflict substantially predates the AUMF, extending at least as far back as al Qaeda\xe2\x80\x99s 1996 declaration of war on our Nation, and that the theater of war extends at least as far as the localities of al Qaeda\xe2\x80\x99s principal bases of operations\xe2\x80\x94is beyond judicial reproach. And the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s unsupportable contrary determination merely confirms that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the Judiciary has neither aptitude, facilities nor responsibility\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d for making military or foreign affairs judgments. Hamdi, 542 U. S., at 585 (Thomas, J., dissenting) (quoting Chicago & Southern Air Lines, 333 U. S., at 111).

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The third consideration identified by Winthrop\xe2\x80\x99s treatise for the exercise of military commission jurisdiction pertains to the persons triable before such a commission, see ante, at 33; Winthrop 838. Law-of-war military commissions have jurisdiction over \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98individuals of the enemy\xe2\x80\x99s army who have been guilty of illegitimate warfare or other offences in violation of the laws of war,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 33-34 (quoting Winthrop 838). They also have jurisdiction over \xe2\x80\x9c[i]rregular armed bodies or persons not forming part of the organized forces of a belligerent\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cwho would not be likely to respect the laws of war.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 783, 784. Indeed, according to Winthrop, such persons are not \xe2\x80\x9cwithin the protection of the laws of war\xe2\x80\x9d and were \xe2\x80\x9cliable to be shot, imprisoned, or banished, either summarily where their guilt was clear or upon trial and conviction by military commission.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 784. This consideration is easily satisfied here, as Hamdan is an unlawful combatant charged with joining and conspiring with a terrorist network dedicated to flouting the laws of war. 344 F. Supp. 2d 152, 161 (DC 2004); App. to Pet. for Cert. 63a\xe2\x80\x9367a.

\n

C

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The fourth consideration relevant to the jurisdiction of law-of-war military commissions relates to the nature of the offense charged. As relevant here, such commissions have jurisdiction to try \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[v]iolations of the laws and usages of war cognizable by military tribunals only,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 34 (quoting Winthrop 839). In contrast to the preceding considerations, this Court\xe2\x80\x99s precedents establish that judicial review of \xe2\x80\x9cwhether any of the acts charged is an offense against the law of war cognizable before a military tribunal\xe2\x80\x9d is appropriate. Quirin, 317 U. S., at 29. However, \xe2\x80\x9ccharges of violations of the law of war triable before a military tribunal need not be stated with the precision of a common law indictment.\xe2\x80\x9d Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 17. And whether an offense is a violation of the law of war cognizable before a military commission must be determined pursuant to \xe2\x80\x9cthe system of common law applied by military tribunals.\xe2\x80\x9d Quirin, supra, at 30; Yamashita, supra, at 8.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The common law of war as it pertains to offenses triable by military commission is derived from the \xe2\x80\x9cexperience of our wars\xe2\x80\x9d and our wartime tribunals, Winthrop 839, and \xe2\x80\x9cthe laws and usages of war as understood and practiced by the civilized nations of the world,\xe2\x80\x9d 11 Op. Atty. Gen. 297, 310 (1865). Moreover, the common law of war is marked by two important features. First, as with the common law generally, it is flexible and evolutionary in nature, building upon the experience of the past and taking account of the exigencies of the present. Thus, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he law of war, like every other code of laws, declares what shall not be done, and does not say what may be done. The legitimate use of the great power of war, or rather the prohibitions upon the use of that power, increase or diminish as the necessity of the case demands.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 300. Accordingly, this Court has recognized that the \xe2\x80\x9cjurisdiction\xe2\x80\x9d of \xe2\x80\x9cour common-law war courts\xe2\x80\x9d has not been \xe2\x80\x9cprescribed by statute,\xe2\x80\x9d but rather \xe2\x80\x9chas been adapted in each instance to the need that called it forth.\xe2\x80\x9d Madsen, 343 U. S., at 346\xe2\x80\x93348. Second, the common law of war affords a measure of respect for the judgment of military commanders. Thus, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he commander of an army in time of war has the same power to organize military tribunals and execute their judgments that he has to set his squadrons in the field and fight battles. His authority in each case is from the law and usage of war.\xe2\x80\x9d 11 Op. Atty. Gen., at 305. In recognition of these principles, Congress has generally \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98left it to the President, and the military commanders representing him, to employ the commission, as occasion may require, for the investigation and punishment of violations of the law of war.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Madsen, supra, at 347, n. 9 (quoting Winthrop 831; emphasis added).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In one key respect, the plurality departs from the proper framework for evaluating the adequacy of the charge against Hamdan under the laws of war. The plurality holds that where, as here, \xe2\x80\x9cneither the elements of the offense nor the range of permissible punishments is defined by statute or treaty, the precedent [establishing whether an offense is triable by military commission] must be plain and unambiguous.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 38. This is a pure contrivance, and a bad one at that. It is contrary to the presumption we acknowledged in Quirin, namely, that the actions of military commissions are \xe2\x80\x9cnot to be set aside by the courts without the clear conviction that they are\xe2\x80\x9d unlawful, 317 U. S., at 25 (emphasis added). It is also contrary to Yamashita, which recognized the legitimacy of that military commission notwithstanding a substantial disagreement pertaining to whether Yamashita had been charged with a violation of the law of war. Compare 327 U. S., at 17 (noting that the allegations were \xe2\x80\x9cadequat[e]\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cneed not be stated with \xe2\x80\xa6 precision\xe2\x80\x9d), with id., at 35 (Murphy, J., dissenting) (arguing that the charge was inadequate). Nor does it find support from the separation of powers authority cited by the plurality. Indeed, Madison\xe2\x80\x99s praise of the separation of powers in The Federalist No. 47, quoted ante, at 38-39, if it has any relevance at all, merely highlights the illegitimacy of today\xe2\x80\x99s judicial intrusion onto core executive prerogatives in the waging of war, where executive competence is at its zenith and judicial competence at its nadir.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s newly minted clear-statement rule is also fundamentally inconsistent with the nature of the common law which, by definition, evolves and develops over time and does not, in all cases, \xe2\x80\x9csay what may be done.\xe2\x80\x9d 11 Op. Atty. Gen., at 300. Similarly, it is inconsistent with the nature of warfare, which also evolves and changes over time, and for which a flexible, evolutionary common-law system is uniquely appropriate.[Footnote 6] Though the charge against Hamdan easily satisfies even the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s manufactured rule, see supra, at 16\xe2\x80\x9328, the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s inflexible approach has dangerous implications for the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s ability to discharge his duties as Commander in Chief in future cases. We should undertake to determine whether an unlawful combatant has been charged with an offense against the law of war with an understanding that the common law of war is flexible, responsive to the exigencies of the present conflict, and deferential to the judgment of military commanders.

\n

\n\n1

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Under either the correct, flexible approach to evaluating the adequacy of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s charge, or under the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s new, clear-statement approach, Hamdan has been charged with conduct constituting two distinct violations of the law of war cognizable before a military commission: membership in a war-criminal enterprise and conspiracy to commit war crimes. The charging section of the indictment alleges both that Hamdan \xe2\x80\x9cwillfully and knowingly joined an enterprise of persons who shared a common criminal purpose,\xe2\x80\x9d App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a, and that he \xe2\x80\x9cconspired and agreed with [al Qaeda] to commit \xe2\x80\xa6 offenses triable by military commission,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid.[Footnote 7]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The common law of war establishes that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s willful and knowing membership in al Qaeda is a war crime chargeable before a military commission. Hamdan, a confirmed enemy combatant and member or affiliate of al Qaeda, has been charged with willfully and knowingly joining a group (al Qaeda) whose purpose is \xe2\x80\x9cto support violent attacks against property and nationals (both military and civilian) of the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 64a; 344 F. Supp. 2d, at 161. Moreover, the allegations specify that Hamdan joined and maintained his relationship with al Qaeda even though he \xe2\x80\x9cbelieved that Usama bin Laden and his associates were involved in the attacks on the U. S. Embassies in Kenya and Tazania in August 1998, the attack on the USS COLE in October 2000, and the attacks on the United States on September 11, 2001.\xe2\x80\x9d App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a. These allegations, against a confirmed unlawful combatant, are alone sufficient to sustain the jurisdiction of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For well over a century it has been established that \xe2\x80\x9cto unite with banditti, jayhawkers, guerillas, or any other unauthorized marauders is a high offence against the laws of war; the offence is complete when the band is organized or joined. The atrocities committed by such a band do not constitute the offence, but make the reasons, and sufficient reasons they are, why such banditti are denounced by the laws of war.\xe2\x80\x9d 11 Op. Atty. Gen., at 312 (emphasis added).[Footnote 8] In other words, unlawful combatants, such as Hamdan, violate the law of war merely by joining an organization, such as al Qaeda, whose principal purpose is the \xe2\x80\x9ckilling [and] disabling \xe2\x80\xa6 of peaceable citizens or soldiers.\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 784; see also 11 Op. Atty. Gen., at 314 (\xe2\x80\x9cA bushwhacker, a jayhawker, a bandit, a war rebel, an assassin, being public enemies, may be tried, condemned, and executed as offenders against the laws of war\xe2\x80\x9d). This conclusion is unsurprising, as it is a \xe2\x80\x9ccardinal principle of the law of war \xe2\x80\xa6 that the civilian population must enjoy complete immunity.\xe2\x80\x9d 4 International Committee of Red Cross, Commentary: Geneva Convention Relative to the Protection of Civilian Persons in Time of War 3 (J. Pictet ed. 1958). \xe2\x80\x9cNumerous instances of trials, for \xe2\x80\x98Violation of the laws of war,\xe2\x80\x99 of offenders of this description, are published in the General Orders of the years 1862 to 1866.\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 784, and n. 57.[Footnote 9] Accordingly, on this basis alone, \xe2\x80\x9cthe allegations of [Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s] charge, tested by any reasonable standard, adequately allege a violation of the law of war.\xe2\x80\x9d Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 17.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The conclusion that membership in an organization whose purpose is to violate the laws of war is an offense triable by military commission is confirmed by the experience of the military tribunals convened by the United States at Nuremberg. Pursuant to Article 10 of the Charter of the International Military Tribunal (IMT), the United States convened military tribunals \xe2\x80\x9cto bring individuals to trial for membership\xe2\x80\x9d in \xe2\x80\x9ca group or organization \xe2\x80\xa6 declared criminal by the [IMT].\xe2\x80\x9d 1 Trials of War Criminals Before the Nuernberg Military Tribunals, p.\xc2\xa0XII (hereinafter Trials). The IMT designated various components of four Nazi groups\xe2\x80\x94the Leadership Corps, Gestapo, SD, and SS\xe2\x80\x94as criminal organizations. 22 IMT, Trial of the Major War Criminals 505, 511, 517 (1948); see also T. Taylor, The Anatomy of the Nuremberg Trials: A Personal Memoir 584\xe2\x80\x93585 (1992). \xe2\x80\x9c[A] member of [such] an organization [could] be \xe2\x80\xa6 convicted of the crime of membership and be punished for that crime by death.\xe2\x80\x9d 22 IMT, at 499. Under this authority, the United States Military Tribunal at Nuremberg convicted numerous individuals for the act of knowing and voluntary membership in these organizations. For example, in Military Tribunal Case No. 1, United States v. Brandt, Karl Brandt, Karl Gebhardt, Rudolf Brandt, Joachim Mrugowsky, Wolfram Sievers, Viktor Brack, and Waldemar Hoven, were convicted and sentenced to death for the crime of, inter alia, membership in an organization declared criminal by the IMT; Karl Genzken and Fritz Fischer were sentenced to life imprisonment for the same; and Helmut Poppendick was convicted of no other offense than membership in a criminal organization and sentenced to a 10-year term of imprisonment. 2 Trials 180\xe2\x80\x93300. This Court denied habeas relief, 333 U. S. 836 (1948), and the executions were carried out at Landsberg prison on June 2, 1948. 2 Trials 330.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Moreover, the Government has alleged that Hamdan was not only a member of al Qaeda while it was carrying out terrorist attacks on civilian targets in the United States and abroad, but also that Hamdan aided and assisted al Qaeda\xe2\x80\x99s top leadership by supplying weapons, transportation, and other services. App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a\xe2\x80\x9367a. These allegations further confirm that Hamdan is triable before a law-of-war military commission for his involvement with al Qaeda. See H.\xc2\xa0R. Doc. No. 65, 55th Cong., 3d Sess., 234 (1894) (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]here are numerous rebels \xe2\x80\xa6 that \xe2\x80\xa6 furnish the enemy with arms, provisions, clothing, horses and means of transportation; [such] insurgents are banding together in several of the interior counties for the purpose of assisting the enemy to rob, to maruad and to lay waste to the country. All such persons are by the laws of war in every civilized country liable to capital punishment\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)); Winthrop 840 (including in the list of offenses triable by law-of-war military commissions \xe2\x80\x9cdealing with \xe2\x80\xa6 enemies, or furnishing them with money, arms, provisions, medicines, &c\xe2\x80\x9d).[Footnote 10] Undoubtedly, the conclusion that such conduct violates the law of war led to the enactment of Article 104 of the UCMJ, which provides that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]ny person who \xe2\x80\xa6 aids, or attempts to aid, the enemy with arms, ammunition, supplies, money, or other things \xe2\x80\xa6 shall suffer death or such other punishment as a court-martial or military commission may direct.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7904.

\n

2

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Separate and apart from the offense of joining a contingent of \xe2\x80\x9cuncivilized combatants who [are] not \xe2\x80\xa6 likely to respect the laws of war,\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 784, Hamdan has been charged with \xe2\x80\x9cconspir[ing] and agree[ing] with \xe2\x80\xa6 the al Qaida organization \xe2\x80\xa6 to commit \xe2\x80\xa6 offenses triable by military commission,\xe2\x80\x9d App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a. Those offenses include \xe2\x80\x9cattacking civilians; attacking civilian objects; murder by an unprivileged belligerent; and terrorism.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. This, too, alleges a violation of the law of war triable by military commission.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he experience of our wars,\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 839, is rife with evidence that establishes beyond any doubt that conspiracy to violate the laws of war is itself an offense cognizable before a law-of-war military commission. World War II provides the most recent examples of the use of American military commissions to try offenses pertaining to violations of the laws of war. In that conflict, the orders establishing the jurisdiction of military commissions in various theaters of operation provided that conspiracy to violate the laws of war was a cognizable offense. See Letter, General Headquarters, United States Army Forces, Pacific (Sept. 24, 1945), Record in Yamashita v. Styer, O.\xc2\xa0T. 1945, No. 672, pp.\xc2\xa014, 16 (Exh. F) (Order respecting the \xe2\x80\x9cRegulations Governing the Trial of War Criminals\xe2\x80\x9d provided that \xe2\x80\x9cparticipation in a common plan or conspiracy to accomplish\xe2\x80\x9d various offenses against the law of war was cognizable before military commissions); 1 United Nations War Crimes Commission, Law Reports of Trials of War Criminals 114\xe2\x80\x93115 (1997) (hereinafter U.\xc2\xa0N. Commission) (recounting that the orders establishing World War II military commissions in the Pacific and China included \xe2\x80\x9cparticipation in a common plan or conspiracy\xe2\x80\x9d pertaining to certain violations of the laws of war as an offense triable by military commission). Indeed, those orders authorized trial by military commission of participation in a conspiracy to commit \xe2\x80\x9cmurder \xe2\x80\xa6 or other inhumane acts \xe2\x80\xa6 against any civilian population,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 114, which is precisely the offense Hamdan has been charged with here. And conspiracy to violate the laws of war was charged in the highest profile case tried before a World War II military commission, see Quirin, 317 U. S., at 23, and on numerous other occasions. See, e.g., Colepaugh v. Looney, 235 F.\xc2\xa02d 429, 431 (CA10 1956); Green 848 (describing the conspiracy trial of Julius Otto Kuehn).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To support its contrary conclusion, ante, at 35\xe2\x80\x9336, the plurality attempts to evade the import of Quirin (and the other World War II authorities) by resting upon this Court\xe2\x80\x99s failure to address the sufficiency of the conspiracy charge in the Quirin case, ante, at 41\xe2\x80\x9343. But the common law of war cannot be ascertained from this Court\xe2\x80\x99s failure to pass upon an issue, or indeed to even mention the issue in its opinion;[Footnote 11] rather, it is ascertained by the practice and usage of war. Winthrop 839; supra, at 11\xe2\x80\x9312.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Civil War experience provides further support for the President\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that conspiracy to violate the laws of war is an offense cognizable before law-of-war military commissions. Indeed, in the highest profile case to be tried before a military commission relating to that war, namely, the trial of the men involved in the assassination of President Lincoln, the charge provided that those men had \xe2\x80\x9ccombin[ed], confederat[ed], and conspir[ed] \xe2\x80\xa6 to kill and murder\xe2\x80\x9d President Lincoln. G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No. 356 (1865), reprinted in H.\xc2\xa0R. Doc. No. 314, 55th Cong., 3d Sess., 696 (1899) (hereinafter G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No. 356).[Footnote 12]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition to the foregoing high-profile example, Winthrop\xe2\x80\x99s treatise enumerates numerous Civil War military commission trials for conspiracy to violate the law of war. Winthrop 839, n. 5. The plurality attempts to explain these examples away by suggesting that the conspiracies listed by Winthrop are best understood as \xe2\x80\x9ca species of compound offense,\xe2\x80\x9d namely, violations both of the law of war and ordinary criminal laws, rather than \xe2\x80\x9cstand-alone offense[s] against the law of war.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 44\xe2\x80\x9345 (citing, as an example, murder in violation of the laws of war). But the fact that, for example, conspiracy to commit murder can at the same time violate ordinary criminal laws and the law of war, so that it is \xe2\x80\x9ca combination of the two species of offenses,\xe2\x80\x9d Howland 1071, does not establish that a military commission would not have jurisdiction to try that crime solely on the basis that it was a violation of the law of war. Rather, if anything, and consistent with the principle that the common law of war is flexible and affords some level of deference to the judgments of military commanders, it establishes that military commissions would have the discretion to try the offense as (1) one against the law of war, or (2) one against the ordinary criminal laws, or (3) both.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In any event, the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s effort to avoid the import of Winthrop\xe2\x80\x99s footnote through the smokescreen of its \xe2\x80\x9ccompound offense\xe2\x80\x9d theory, ante, at 44\xe2\x80\x9345, cannot be reconciled with the particular charges that sustained military commission jurisdiction in the cases that Winthrop cites. For example, in the military commission trial of Henry Wirtz, Charge I provided that he had been

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c[m]aliciously, willfully, and traitorously . . . combining, confederating, and conspiring, together [with various other named and unnamed co-conspirators], to injure the health and destroy the lives of soldiers in the military service of the United States, then held and being prisoners of war within the lines of the so-called Confederate States, and in the military prisons thereof, to the end that the armies of the United States might be weakened and impaired, in violation of the laws and customs of war.\xe2\x80\x9d G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No. 607 (1865), reprinted in H.\xc2\xa0R. Doc. No. 314, at 785 (emphasis added).

\n

\nLikewise, in the military commission trial of Lenger Grenfel, Charge I accused Grenfel of \xe2\x80\x9c[c]onspiring, in violation of the laws of war, to release rebel prisoners of war confined by authority of the United States at Camp Douglas, near Chicago, Ill.\xe2\x80\x9d G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No. 452 (1865), reprinted in H.\xc2\xa0R. Doc. No. 314, at 724 (emphasis added)[Footnote 13]; see also G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No. 41, at 20 (1864) (indictment in the military commission trial of Robert Louden charged \xe2\x80\x9c[c]onspiring with the rebel enemies of the United States to embarrass and impede the military authorities in the suppression of the existing rebellion, by the burning and destruction of steamboats and means of transportation on the Mississippi river\xe2\x80\x9d). These examples provide incontrovertible support for the President\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that the common law of war permits military commission trials for conspiracy to violate the law of war. And they specifically contradict the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion to the contrary, thereby easily satisfying its requirement that the Government \xe2\x80\x9cmake a substantial showing that the crime for which it seeks to try a defendant by military commission is acknowledged to be an offense against the law of war.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 39-40.[Footnote 14]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The plurality further contends, in reliance upon Winthrop, that conspiracy is not an offense cognizable before a law-of-war military commission because \xe2\x80\x9cit is not enough to intend to violate the law of war and commit overt acts in furtherance of that intention unless the overt acts either are themselves offenses against the law of war or constitute steps sufficiently substantial to qualify as an attempt.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. But Winthrop does not support the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion. The passage in Winthrop cited by the plurality states only that \xe2\x80\x9cthe jurisdiction of the military commission should be restricted to cases of offence consisting in overt acts, i.e. in unlawful commissions or actual attempts to commit, and not in intentions merely.\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop 841 (emphasis in original). This passage would be helpful to the plurality if its subject were \xe2\x80\x9cconspiracy,\xe2\x80\x9d rather than the \xe2\x80\x9cjurisdiction of the military commission.\xe2\x80\x9d Winthrop is not speaking here of the requirements for a conspiracy charge, but of the requirements for all charges. Intentions do not suffice. An unlawful act\xe2\x80\x94such as committing the crime of conspiracy\xe2\x80\x94is necessary. Winthrop says nothing to exclude either conspiracy or membership in a criminal enterprise, both of which go beyond \xe2\x80\x9cintentions merely\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cconsis[t] of overt acts, i.e. \xe2\x80\xa6 unlawful commissions or actual attempts to commit,\xe2\x80\x9d and both of which are expressly recognized by Winthrop as crimes against the law of war triable by military commissions. Id., at 784; id., at 839, and n.\xc2\xa05, 840. Indeed, the commission of an \xe2\x80\x9covert ac[t]\xe2\x80\x9d is the traditional requirement for the completion of the crime of conspiracy, and the charge against Hamdan alleges numerous such overt acts. App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a. The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s approach, unsupported by Winthrop, requires that any overt act to further a conspiracy must itself be a completed war crime distinct from conspiracy\xe2\x80\x94which merely begs the question the plurality sets out to answer, namely, whether conspiracy itself may constitute a violation of the law of war. And, even the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s unsupported standard is satisfied here. Hamdan has been charged with the overt acts of providing protection, transportation, weapons, and other services to the enemy, id., at 65a\xe2\x80\x9367a, acts which in and of themselves are violations of the laws of war. See supra, at 20\xe2\x80\x9321; Winthrop 839\xe2\x80\x93840.

\n

3

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Ultimately, the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s determination that Hamdan has not been charged with an offense triable before a military commission rests not upon any historical example or authority, but upon the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s raw judgment of the \xe2\x80\x9cinability on the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s part here to satisfy the most basic precondition \xe2\x80\xa6 for establishment of military commissions: military necessity.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 48. This judgment starkly confirms that the plurality has appointed itself the ultimate arbiter of what is quintessentially a policy and military judgment, namely, the appropriate military measures to take against those who \xe2\x80\x9caided the terrorist attacks that occurred on September 11, 2001.\xe2\x80\x9d AUMF \xc2\xa72(a), 115 Stat. 224. The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission is illegitimate because it is not dispensing swift justice on the battlefield is unsupportable. Ante, at 43. Even a cursory review of the authorities confirms that law-of-war military commissions have wide-ranging jurisdiction to try offenses against the law of war in exigent and nonexigent circumstances alike. See, e.g., Winthrop 839\xe2\x80\x93840; see also Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 5 (military commission trial after the cessation of hostilities in the Philippines); Quirin, 317 U. S. 1 (military commission trial in Washington, D.\xc2\xa0C.). Traditionally, retributive justice for heinous war crimes is as much a \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary necessity\xe2\x80\x9d as the \xe2\x80\x9cdemands\xe2\x80\x9d of \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary efficiency\xe2\x80\x9d touted by the plurality, and swift military retribution is precisely what Congress authorized the President to impose on the September 11 attackers in the AUMF.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Today a plurality of this Court would hold that conspiracy to massacre innocent civilians does not violate the laws of war. This determination is unsustainable. The judgment of the political branches that Hamdan, and others like him, must be held accountable before military commissions for their involvement with and membership in an unlawful organization dedicated to inflicting massive civilian casualties is supported by virtually every relevant authority, including all of the authorities invoked by the plurality today. It is also supported by the nature of the present conflict. We are not engaged in a traditional battle with a nation-state, but with a worldwide, hydra-headed enemy, who lurks in the shadows conspiring to reproduce the atrocities of September 11, 2001, and who has boasted of sending suicide bombers into civilian gatherings, has proudly distributed videotapes of beheadings of civilian workers, and has tortured and dismembered captured American soldiers. But according to the plurality, when our Armed Forces capture those who are plotting terrorist atrocities like the bombing of the Khobar Towers, the bombing of the U. S.\xc2\xa0S. Cole, and the attacks of September 11\xe2\x80\x94even if their plots are advanced to the very brink of fulfillment\xe2\x80\x94our military cannot charge those criminals with any offense against the laws of war. Instead, our troops must catch the terrorists \xe2\x80\x9credhanded,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 48, in the midst of the attack itself, in order to bring them to justice. Not only is this conclusion fundamentally inconsistent with the cardinal principal of the law of war, namely protecting non-combatants, but it would sorely hamper the President\xe2\x80\x99s ability to confront and defeat a new and deadly enemy.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0After seeing the plurality overturn longstanding precedents in order to seize jurisdiction over this case, ante, at 2\xe2\x80\x934 (Scalia, J., dissenting), and after seeing them disregard the clear prudential counsel that they abstain in these circumstances from using equitable powers, ante, at 19\xe2\x80\x9324, it is no surprise to see them go on to overrule one after another of the President\xe2\x80\x99s judgments pertaining to the conduct of an ongoing war. Those Justices who today disregard the commander-in-chief\xe2\x80\x99s wartime decisions, only 10 days ago deferred to the judgment of the Corps of Engineers with regard to a matter much more within the competence of lawyers, upholding that agency\xe2\x80\x99s wildly implausible conclusion that a storm drain is a tributary of the waters of the United States. See Rapanos v. United States, 547 U. S. ___(2006). It goes without saying that there is much more at stake here than storm drains. The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s willingness to second-guess the determination of the political branches that these conspirators must be brought to justice is both unprecedented and dangerous.

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court holds that even if \xe2\x80\x9cthe Government has charged Hamdan with an offense against the law of war cognizable by military commission, the commission lacks power to proceed\xe2\x80\x9d because of its failure to comply with the terms of the UCMJ and the four Geneva Conventions signed in 1949. Ante, at 49. This position is untenable.

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As with the jurisdiction of military commissions, the procedure of such commissions \xe2\x80\x9chas [not] been prescribed by statute,\xe2\x80\x9d but \xe2\x80\x9chas been adapted in each instance to the need that called it forth.\xe2\x80\x9d Madsen, 343 U. S., at 347\xe2\x80\x93348. Indeed, this Court has concluded that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]n the absence of attempts by Congress to limit the President\xe2\x80\x99s power, it appears that, as Commander in Chief of the Army and Navy of the United States, he may, in time of war, establish and prescribe the jurisdiction and procedure of military commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 348. This conclusion is consistent with this Court\xe2\x80\x99s understanding that military commissions are \xe2\x80\x9cour common-law war courts.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 346\xe2\x80\x93347.[Footnote 15] As such, \xe2\x80\x9c[s]hould the conduct of those who compose martial-law tribunals become [a] matter of judicial determination subsequently before the civil courts, those courts will give great weight to the opinions of the officers as to what the customs of war in any case justify and render necessary.\xe2\x80\x9d Birkhimer 534.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court nevertheless concludes that at least one provision of the UCMJ amounts to an attempt by Congress to limit the President\xe2\x80\x99s power. This conclusion is not only contrary to the text and structure of the UCMJ, but it is also inconsistent with precedent of this Court. Consistent with Madsen\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion pertaining to the common-law nature of military commissions and the President\xe2\x80\x99s discretion to prescribe their procedures, Article 36 of the UCMJ authorizes the President to establish procedures for military commissions \xe2\x80\x9cwhich shall, so far as he considers practicable, apply the principles of law and the rules of evidence generally recognized in the trial of criminal cases in the United States district courts, but which may not be contrary to or inconsistent with this chapter.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(a) (emphasis added). Far from constraining the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority, Article 36 recognizes the President\xe2\x80\x99s prerogative to depart from the procedures applicable in criminal cases whenever he alone does not deem such procedures \xe2\x80\x9cpracticable.\xe2\x80\x9d While the procedural regulations promulgated by the Executive must not be \xe2\x80\x9ccontrary to\xe2\x80\x9d the UCMJ, only a few provisions of the UCMJ mention \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary commissions,\xe2\x80\x9d see ante, at 58, n.\xc2\xa049, and there is no suggestion that the procedures to be employed by Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission implicate any of those provisions.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Notwithstanding the foregoing, the Court concludes that Article 36(b) of the UCMJ, 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(b), which provides that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[a]ll rules and regulations made under this article shall be uniform insofar as practicable,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 57, requires the President to employ the same rules and procedures in military commissions as are employed by courts-martial \xe2\x80\x9cinsofar as practicable.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 59. The Court further concludes that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission is unlawful because the President has not explained why it is not practicable to apply the same rules and procedures to Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission as would be applied in a trial by court martial. Ante, at 60.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This interpretation of \xc2\xa7836(b) is unconvincing. As an initial matter, the Court fails to account for our cases interpreting the predecessor to Article 21 of the UCMJ\xe2\x80\x94Article 15 of the Articles of War\xe2\x80\x94which provides crucial context that bears directly on the proper interpretation of Article 36(b). Article 15 of the Articles of War provided that:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cThe provisions of these articles conferring jurisdiction upon courts-martial shall not be construed as depriving military commissions, provost courts, or other military tribunals of concurrent jurisdiction in respect of offenders or offences that by statute or by the law of war may be triable by such military commissions, provost courts, or other military tribunals.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\nIn Yamashita, this Court concluded that Article 15 of the Articles of War preserved the President\xe2\x80\x99s unfettered authority to prescribe military commission procedure. The Court explained, \xe2\x80\x9c[b]y thus recognizing military commissions in order to preserve their traditional jurisdiction over enemy combatants unimpaired by the Articles, Congress gave sanction \xe2\x80\xa6 to any use of the military commission contemplated by the common law of war.\xe2\x80\x9d 327 U. S., at 20 (emphasis added)[Footnote 16]; see also Quirin, 317 U. S., at 28; Madsen, 343 U. S., at 355. In reaching this conclusion, this Court treated as authoritative the congressional testimony of Judge Advocate General Crowder, who testified that Article 15 of the Articles of War was enacted to preserve the military commission as \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98our common-law war court.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Yamashita, supra, at 19, n.\xc2\xa07. And this Court recognized that Article 15\xe2\x80\x99s preservation of military commissions as common-law war courts preserved the President\xe2\x80\x99s commander-in-chief authority to both \xe2\x80\x9cestablish\xe2\x80\x9d military commissions and to \xe2\x80\x9cprescribe [their] procedure[s].\xe2\x80\x9d Madsen, 343 U. S., at 348; id., at 348\xe2\x80\x93349 (explaining that Congress had \xe2\x80\x9crefrain[ed] from legislating\xe2\x80\x9d in the area of military commission procedures, in \xe2\x80\x9ccontras[t] with its traditional readiness to \xe2\x80\xa6 prescrib[e], with particularity, the jurisdiction and procedure of United States courts-martial\xe2\x80\x9d); cf. Green 834 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe military commission exercising jurisdiction under common law authority is usually appointed by a superior military commander and is limited in its procedure only by the will of that commander. Like any other common law court, in the absence of directive of superior authority to the contrary, the military commission is free to formulate its own rules of procedure\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Given these precedents, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that Article 36(b) requires the President to apply the same rules and procedures to military commissions as are applicable to courts-martial is unsustainable. When Congress codified Article 15 of the Articles of War in Article 21 of the UCMJ it was \xe2\x80\x9cpresumed to be aware of \xe2\x80\xa6 and to adopt\xe2\x80\x9d this Court\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation of that provision as preserving the common-law status of military commissions, inclusive of the President\xe2\x80\x99s unfettered authority to prescribe their procedures. Lorillard v. Pons, 434 U. S. 575, 580 (1978). The Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that Article 36(b) repudiates this settled meaning of Article 21 is not based upon a specific textual reference to military commissions, but rather on a one-sentence subsection providing that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]ll rules and regulations made under this article shall be uniform insofar as practicable.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7836(b). This is little more than an impermissible repeal by implication. See Branch v. Smith, 538 U. S. 254, 273 (2003). (\xe2\x80\x9cWe have repeatedly stated \xe2\x80\xa6 that absent a clearly expressed congressional intention, repeals by implication are not favored\xe2\x80\x9d (citation and internal quotation marks omitted)). Moreover, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion is flatly contrary to its duty not to set aside Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission \xe2\x80\x9cwithout the clear conviction that [it is] in conflict with the \xe2\x80\xa6 laws of Congress constitutionally enacted.\xe2\x80\x9d Quirin, supra, at 25 (emphasis added).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nothing in the text of Article 36(b) supports the Court\xe2\x80\x99s sweeping conclusion that it represents an unprecedented congressional effort to change the nature of military commissions from common-law war courts to tribunals that must presumptively function like courts-martial. And such an interpretation would be strange indeed. The vision of uniformity that motivated the adoption of the UCMJ, embodied specifically in Article 36(b), is nothing more than uniformity across the separate branches of the armed services. See ch. 169, 64 Stat. 107 (preamble to the UCMJ explaining that the UCMJ is an act \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o unify, consolidate, revise, and codify the Articles of War, the Articles for the Government of the Navy, and the disciplinary laws of the Coast Guard\xe2\x80\x9d). There is no indication that the UCMJ was intended to require uniformity in procedure between courts-martial and military commissions, tribunals that the UCMJ itself recognizes are different. To the contrary, the UCMJ expressly recognizes that different tribunals will be constituted in different manners and employ different procedures. See 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7866 (providing for three different types of courts-martial\xe2\x80\x94 general, special, and summary\xe2\x80\x94constituted in different manners and employing different procedures). Thus, Article 36(b) is best understood as establishing that, so far as practicable, the rules and regulations governing tribunals convened by the Navy must be uniform with the rules and regulations governing tribunals convened by the Army. But, consistent with this Court\xe2\x80\x99s prior interpretations of Article 21 and over a century of historical practice, it cannot be understood to require the President to conform the procedures employed by military commissions to those employed by courts-martial.[Footnote 17]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even if Article 36(b) could be construed to require procedural uniformity among the various tribunals contemplated by the UCMJ, Hamdan would not be entitled to relief. Under the Court\xe2\x80\x99s reading, the President is entitled to prescribe different rules for military commissions than for courts-martial when he determines that it is not \xe2\x80\x9cpracticable\xe2\x80\x9d to prescribe uniform rules. The Court does not resolve the level of deference such determinations would be owed, however, because, in its view, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he President has not . . . [determined] that it is impracticable to apply the rules for courts-martial.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 60. This is simply not the case.
