On the same day that the President issued Military Commission Order No. 1, the Secretary of Defense explained that \xe2\x80\x9cthe president decided to establish military commissions because he wanted the option of a process that is different from those processes which we already have, namely the federal court system . . . and the military court system,\xe2\x80\x9d Dept. of Defense News Briefing on Military Commissions (Mar. 21, 2002) (remarks of Donald Rumsfeld), available at http://www.dod.gov/transcrips/\n2002/t03212002_t0321sd.html (as visited June 26, 2006, and available in Clerk of Court\xe2\x80\x99s case file) (hereinafter News Briefing), and that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he commissions are intended to be different . . . because the [P]resident recognized that there had to be differences to deal with the unusual situation we face and that a different approach was needed.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. The President reached this conclusion because

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99re in the middle of a war, and . . . had to design a procedure that would allow us to pursue justice for these individuals while at the same time prosecuting the war most effectively. And that means setting rules that would allow us to preserve our intelligence secrets, develop more information about terrorist activities that might be planned for the future so that we can take action to prevent terrorist attacks against the United States. . . . [T]here was a constant balancing of the requirements of our war policy and the importance of providing justice for individuals . . . and each deviation from the standard kinds of rules that we have in our criminal courts was motivated by the desire to strike the balance between individual justice and the broader war policy.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (remarks of Douglas J. Feith, Under Secretary of Defense for Policy (emphasis added)).

\n

\nThe Court provides no explanation why the President\xe2\x80\x99s determination that employing court-martial procedures in the military commissions established pursuant to Military Commission Order No. 1 would hamper our war effort is in any way inadequate to satisfy its newly minted \xe2\x80\x9cpracticability\xe2\x80\x9d requirement. On the contrary, this determination is precisely the kind for which the \xe2\x80\x9cJudiciary has neither aptitude, facilities nor responsibility and which has long been held to belong in the domain of political power not subject to judicial intrusion or inquiry.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Chicago & Southern Air Lines, Inc. v. Waterman S. S. Corp., 333 U. S. 103, 111 (1948). And, in the context of the present conflict, it is exactly the kind of determination Congress countenanced when it authorized the President to use all necessary and appropriate force against our enemies. Accordingly, the President\xe2\x80\x99s determination is sufficient to satisfy any practicability requirement imposed by Article 36(b).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The plurality further contends that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission is unlawful because it fails to provide him the right to be present at his trial, as recognized in 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7839(c) (Supp. 2006). Ante, at 61. But \xc2\xa7839(c) applies to courts-martial, not military commissions. It provides:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cWhen the members of a court-martial deliberate or vote, only the members may be present. All other proceedings, including any other consultation of the members of the court with counsel or the military judge, shall be made a part of the record and shall be in the presence of the accused, the defense counsel, the trial counsel, and, in cases in which a military judge has been detailed to the court, the military judge.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\nIn context, \xe2\x80\x9call other proceedings\xe2\x80\x9d plainly refers exclusively to \xe2\x80\x9cother proceedings\xe2\x80\x9d pertaining to a court-martial.[Footnote 18] This is confirmed by the provision\xe2\x80\x99s subsequent reference to \xe2\x80\x9cmembers of the court\xe2\x80\x9d and to \xe2\x80\x9ccases in which a military judge has been detailed to the court.\xe2\x80\x9d It is also confirmed by the other provisions of \xc2\xa7839, which refer only to courts-martial. See \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7839(a)(1)\xe2\x80\x93(4) (\xe2\x80\x9c[A]ny time after the service of charges which have been referred for trial to a court-martial composed of a military judge and members, the military judge may \xe2\x80\xa6 call the court into session without the presence of the members for the purpose of,\xe2\x80\x9d hearing motions, issuing rulings, holding arraignments, receiving pleas, and performing various procedural functions). See also \xc2\xa7839(b) (\xe2\x80\x9cProceedings under subsection (a) shall be conducted in the presence of the accused\xe2\x80\x9d). Section 839(c) simply does not address the procedural requirements of military commissions.

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court contends that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission is also unlawful because it violates Common Article 3 of the Geneva Conventions, see ante, at 65\xe2\x80\x9372. Furthermore, Hamdan contends that his commission is unlawful because it violates various provisions of the Third Geneva Convention. These contentions are untenable.

\n

1

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As an initial matter, and as the Court of Appeals concluded, both of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s Geneva Convention claims are foreclosed by Johnson v. Eisentrager, 339 U. S. 763 (1950). In that case the respondents claimed, inter alia, that their military commission lacked jurisdiction because it failed to provide them with certain procedural safeguards that they argued were required under the Geneva Conventions. Id., at 789\xe2\x80\x93790. While this Court rejected the underlying merits of the respondents\xe2\x80\x99 Geneva Convention claims, id., at 790, it also held, in the alternative, that the respondents could \xe2\x80\x9cnot assert \xe2\x80\xa6 that anything in the Geneva Convention makes them immune from prosecution or punishment for war crimes,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 789. The Court explained:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cWe are not holding that these prisoners have no right which the military authorities are bound to respect. The United States, by the Geneva Convention of July 27, 1929, 47 Stat. 2021, concluded with forty-six other countries, including the German Reich, an agreement upon the treatment to be accorded captives. These prisoners claim to be and are entitled to its protection. It is, however, the obvious scheme of the Agreement that responsibility for observance and enforcement of these rights is upon political and military authorities. Rights of alien enemies are vindicated under it only through protests and intervention of protecting powers as the rights of our citizens against foreign governments are vindicated only by Presidential intervention.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 789, n.\xc2\xa014.

\n

\nThis alternative holding is no less binding than if it were the exclusive basis for the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision. See Massachusetts v. United States, 333 U. S. 611, 623 (1948). While the Court attempts to cast Eisentrager\xe2\x80\x99s unqualified, alternative holding as footnote dictum, ante, at 63\xe2\x80\x9364, it does not dispute the correctness of its conclusion, namely, that the provisions of the 1929 Geneva Convention were not judicially enforceable because that Convention contemplated that diplomatic measures by political and military authorities were the exclusive mechanisms for such enforcement. Nor does the Court suggest that the 1949 Geneva Conventions departed from this framework. See ante, at 64 (\xe2\x80\x9cWe may assume that \xe2\x80\x98the obvious scheme\xe2\x80\x99 of the 1949 Conventions is identical in all relevant respects to that of the 1929 Convention\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Instead, the Court concludes that petitioner may seek judicial enforcement of the provisions of the Geneva Conventions because \xe2\x80\x9cthey are \xe2\x80\xa6 part of the law of war. And compliance with the law of war is the condition upon which the authority set forth in Article 21 is granted.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 65 (citation omitted). But Article 21 authorizes the use of military commissions; it does not purport to render judicially enforceable aspects of the law of war that are not so enforceable of their own accord. See Quirin, 317 U. S., at 28 (by enacting Article 21, \xe2\x80\x9cCongress has explicitly provided, so far as it may constitutionally do so, that military tribunals shall have jurisdiction to try offenders or offenses against the law of war\xe2\x80\x9d). The Court cannot escape Eisentrager\xe2\x80\x99s holding merely by observing that Article 21 mentions the law of war; indeed, though Eisentrager did not specifically consider the Court\xe2\x80\x99s novel interpretation of Article 21, Eisentrager involved a challenge to the legality of a World War II military commission, which, like all such commissions, found its authorization in Article 15 of the Articles of War, the predecessor to Article 21 of the UCMJ. Thus, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation of Article 21 is foreclosed by Eisentrager.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In any event, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s argument is too clever by half. The judicial nonenforceability of the Geneva Conventions derives from the fact that those Conventions have exclusive enforcement mechanisms, see Eisentrager, supra, at 789, n.\xc2\xa014, and this, too, is part of the law of war. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s position thus rests on the assumption that Article 21\xe2\x80\x99s reference to the \xe2\x80\x9claws of war\xe2\x80\x9d selectively incorporates only those aspects of the Geneva Conventions that the Court finds convenient, namely, the substantive requirements of Common Article 3, and not those aspects of the Conventions that the Court, for whatever reason, disfavors, namely the Conventions\xe2\x80\x99 exclusive diplomatic enforcement scheme. The Court provides no account of why the partial incorporation of the Geneva Conventions should extend only so far\xe2\x80\x94and no further\xe2\x80\x94because none is available beyond its evident preference to adjudicate those matters that the law of war, through the Geneva Conventions, consigns exclusively to the political branches.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even if the Court were correct that Article 21 of the UCMJ renders judicially enforceable aspects of the law of war that are not so enforceable by their own terms, Article 21 simply cannot be interpreted to render judicially enforceable the particular provision of the law of war at issue here, namely Common Article 3 of the Geneva Conventions. As relevant, Article 21 provides that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he provisions of this chapter conferring jurisdiction upon courts-martial do not deprive military commissions \xe2\x80\xa6 of concurrent jurisdiction with respect to offenders or offenses that by statute or by the law of war may be tried by military commissions.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821 (emphasis added). Thus, to the extent Article 21 can be interpreted as authorizing judicial enforcement of aspects of the law of war that are not otherwise judicially enforceable, that authorization only extends to provisions of the law of war that relate to whether a particular \xe2\x80\x9coffender\xe2\x80\x9d or a particular \xe2\x80\x9coffense\xe2\x80\x9d is triable by military commission. Common Article 3 of the Geneva Conventions, the sole provision of the Geneva Conventions relevant to the Court\xe2\x80\x99s holding, relates to neither. Rather, it relates exclusively to the particulars of the tribunal itself, namely, whether it is \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d and whether it \xe2\x80\x9cafford[s] all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d Third Geneva Convention, Art. 3, \xc2\xb61(d), Relative to the Treatment of Prisoners of War, Aug. 12, 1949, [1955] 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T. 3316, 3320, T. I. A. S No. 3364.

\n

2

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition to being foreclosed by Eisentrager, Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s claim under Common Article 3 of the Geneva Conventions is meritless. Common Article 3 applies to \xe2\x80\x9carmed conflict not of an international character occurring in the territory of one of the High Contracting Parties.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T., at 3318. \xe2\x80\x9cPursuant to [his] authority as Commander in Chief and Chief Executive of the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d the President has \xe2\x80\x9caccept[ed] the legal conclusion of the Department of Justice \xe2\x80\xa6 that common Article 3 of Geneva does not apply to \xe2\x80\xa6 al Qaeda \xe2\x80\xa6 detainees, because, among other reasons, the relevant conflicts are international in scope and common Article 3 applies only to \xe2\x80\x98armed conflict not of an international character.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d App. 35. Under this Court\xe2\x80\x99s precedents, \xe2\x80\x9cthe meaning attributed to treaty provisions by the Government agencies charged with their negotiation and enforcement is entitled to great weight.\xe2\x80\x9d Sumitomo Shoji America, Inc. v. Avagliano, 457 U. S. 176, 184\xe2\x80\x93185 (1982); United States v. Stuart, 489 U. S. 353, 369 (1989). Our duty to defer to the President\xe2\x80\x99s understanding of the provision at issue here is only heightened by the fact that he is acting pursuant to his constitutional authority as Commander in Chief and by the fact that the subject matter of Common Article 3 calls for a judgment about the nature and character of an armed conflict. See generally United States v. Curtiss-Wright Export Corp., 299 U. S. 304, 320 (1936).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The President\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation of Common Article 3 is reasonable and should be sustained. The conflict with al Qaeda is international in character in the sense that it is occurring in various nations around the globe. Thus, it is also \xe2\x80\x9coccurring in the territory of\xe2\x80\x9d more than \xe2\x80\x9cone of the High Contracting Parties.\xe2\x80\x9d The Court does not dispute the President\xe2\x80\x99s judgments respecting the nature of our conflict with al Qaeda, nor does it suggest that the President\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation of Common Article 3 is implausible or foreclosed by the text of the treaty. Indeed, the Court concedes that Common Article 3 is principally concerned with \xe2\x80\x9cfurnish[ing] minimal protection to rebels involved in \xe2\x80\xa6 a civil war,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 68, precisely the type of conflict the President\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation envisions to be subject to Common Article 3. Instead, the Court, without acknowledging its duty to defer to the President, adopts its own, admittedly plausible, reading of Common Article 3. But where, as here, an ambiguous treaty provision (\xe2\x80\x9cnot of an international character\xe2\x80\x9d) is susceptible of two plausible, and reasonable, interpretations, our precedents require us to defer to the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation.

\n

3

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0But even if Common Article 3 were judicially enforceable and applicable to the present conflict, petitioner would not be entitled to relief. As an initial matter, any claim petitioner has under Common Article 3 is not ripe. The only relevant \xe2\x80\x9cacts\xe2\x80\x9d that \xe2\x80\x9care and shall remain prohibited\xe2\x80\x9d under Common Article 3 are \xe2\x80\x9cthe passing of sentences and the carrying out of executions without previous judgment pronounced by a regularly constituted court affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d Art. 3, \xc2\xb61(d), 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T., at 1318, 1320 (emphases added). As its terms make clear, Common Article 3 is only violated, as relevant here, by the act of \xe2\x80\x9cpassing of sentenc[e],\xe2\x80\x9d and thus Hamdan will only have a claim if his military commission convicts him and imposes a sentence. Accordingly, as Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s claim is \xe2\x80\x9ccontingent [upon] future events that may not occur as anticipated, or indeed may not occur at all,\xe2\x80\x9d it is not ripe for adjudication. Texas v. United States, 523 U. S. 296, 300 (1998) (internal quotation marks omitted).[Footnote 19] Indeed, even if we assume he will be convicted and sentenced, whether his trial will be conducted in a manner so as to deprive him of \xe2\x80\x9cthe judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples\xe2\x80\x9d is entirely speculative. And premature adjudication of Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s claim is especially inappropriate here because \xe2\x80\x9creaching the merits of the dispute would force us to decide whether an action taken by one of the other two branches of the Federal Government was unconstitutional.\xe2\x80\x9d Raines v. Byrd, 521 U. S. 811, 819\xe2\x80\x93820 (1997).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In any event, Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s military commission complies with the requirements of Common Article 3. It is plainly \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted\xe2\x80\x9d because such commissions have been employed throughout our history to try unlawful combatants for crimes against the law of war. This Court has recounted that history as follows:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98By a practice dating from 1847 and renewed and firmly established during the Civil War, military commissions have become adopted as authorized tribunals in this country in time of war\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Their competency has been recognized not only in acts of Congress, but in executive proclamations, in rulings of the courts, and in the opinions of the Attorneys General.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Madsen, 343 U. S., at 346, n.\xc2\xa08.

\n

\nHamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission has been constituted in accordance with these historical precedents. As I have previously explained, the procedures to be employed by that commission, and the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s authority to alter those procedures, are consistent with the practice of previous American military commissions. See supra, at 30\xe2\x80\x9334 , and n. 15.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court concludes Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission fails to satisfy the requirements of Common Article 3 not because it differs from the practice of previous military commissions but because it \xe2\x80\x9cdeviate[s] from [the procedures] governing courts-martial.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 71. But there is neither a statutory nor historical requirement that military commissions conform to the structure and practice of courts-martial. A military commission is a different tribunal, serving a different function, and thus operates pursuant to different procedures. The 150-year pedigree of the military commission is itself sufficient to establish that such tribunals are \xe2\x80\x9cregularly constituted court[s].\xe2\x80\x9d Art. 3, \xc2\xb61(d), 6 U. S. T., at 3320.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Similarly, the procedures to be employed by Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission afford \xe2\x80\x9call the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d Neither the Court nor petitioner disputes the Government\xe2\x80\x99s description of those procedures.

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cPetitioner is entitled to appointed military legal counsel, 32 C.F.R. 9.4(c)(2), and may retain a civilian attorney (which he has done), 32 C.F.R. 9.4(c)(2)(iii)(B). Petitioner is entitled to the presumption of innocence, 32 C.F.R. 9.5(b), proof beyond a reasonable doubt, 32 C.F.R. 9.5(c), and the right to remain silent, 32 C.F.R. 9.5(f). He may confront witnesses against him, 32 C.F.R. 9.5(i), and may subpoena his own witnesses, if reasonably available, 32 C.F.R. 9.5(h). Petitioner may personally be present at every stage of the trial unless he engages in disruptive conduct or the prosecution introduces classified or otherwise protected information for which no adequate substitute is available and whose admission will not deprive him of a full and fair trial, 32 C.F.R. 9.5(k); Military Commission Order No. 1 (Dep\xe2\x80\x99t of Defense Aug. 31, 2005) \xc2\xa76(B)(3) and (D)(5)(b). If petitioner is found guilty, the judgment will be reviewed by a review panel, the Secretary of Defense, and the President, if he does not designate the Secretary as the final decisionmaker. 32 C.F.R. 9.6(h). The final judgment is subject to review in the Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit and ultimately in this Court. See DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(3), 119 Stat. 2743; 28 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. 1254(1).\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Respondents 4.

\n

\nNotwithstanding these provisions, which in my judgment easily satisfy the nebulous standards of Common Article 3,[Footnote 20] the plurality concludes that Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s commission is unlawful because of the possibility that Hamdan will be barred from proceedings and denied access to evidence that may be used to convict him. Ante, at 70\xe2\x80\x9372. But, under the commissions\xe2\x80\x99 rules, the Government may not impose such bar or denial on Hamdan if it would render his trial unfair, a question that is clearly within the scope of the appellate review contemplated by regulation and statute.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Moreover, while the Executive is surely not required to offer a particularized defense of these procedures prior to their application, the procedures themselves make clear that Hamdan would only be excluded (other than for disruption) if it were necessary to protect classified (or classifiable) intelligence, Dept. of Defense, Military Commission Order No. 1, \xc2\xa76(B)(3) (Aug. 31, 2005), including the sources and methods for gathering such intelligence. The Government has explained that \xe2\x80\x9cwe want to make sure that these proceedings, which are going on in the middle of the war, do not interfere with our war effort and \xe2\x80\xa6 because of the way we would be able to handle interrogations and intelligence information, may actually assist us in promoting our war aims.\xe2\x80\x9d News Briefing (remarks of Douglas J. Feith, Under Secretary of Defense for Policy). And this Court has concluded, in the very context of a threat to reveal our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s intelligence gathering sources and methods, that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]t is \xe2\x80\x98obvious and unarguable\xe2\x80\x99 that no governmental interest is more compelling than the security of the Nation,\xe2\x80\x9d Haig, 453 U. S., at 307 (quoting Aptheker v. Secretary of State, 378 U. S. 500, 509 (1964)), and that \xe2\x80\x9c[m]easures to protect the secrecy of our Government\xe2\x80\x99s foreign intelligence operations plainly serve these interests,\xe2\x80\x9d Haig, supra, at 307. See also Snepp v. United States, 444 U. S. 507, 509, n. 3 (1980) (per curiam) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe Government has a compelling interest in protecting both the secrecy of information important to our national security and the appearance of confidentiality so essential to the effective operation of our foreign intelligence service\xe2\x80\x9d); Curtiss-Wright, 299 U. S., at 320. This interest is surely compelling here. According to the Government, \xe2\x80\x9c[b]ecause al Qaeda operates as a clandestine force relying on sleeper agents to mount surprise attacks, one of the most critical fronts in the current war involves gathering intelligence about future terrorist attacks and how the terrorist network operates\xe2\x80\x94identifying where its operatives are, how it plans attacks, who directs operations, and how they communicate.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for United States in No. 03\xe2\x80\x934792, United States v. Moussaoui (CA4), p. 9. We should not rule out the possibility that this compelling interest can be protected, while at the same time affording Hamdan (and others like him) a fair trial.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In these circumstances, \xe2\x80\x9ccivilized peoples\xe2\x80\x9d would take into account the context of military commission trials against unlawful combatants in the war on terrorism, including the need to keep certain information secret in the interest of preventing future attacks on our Nation and its foreign installations so long as it did not deprive the accused of a fair trial. Accordingly, the President\xe2\x80\x99s understanding of the requirements of Common Article 3 is entitled to \xe2\x80\x9cgreat weight.\xe2\x80\x9d See supra, at 43.

\n

4

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition to Common Article 3, which applies to conflicts \xe2\x80\x9cnot of an international character,\xe2\x80\x9d Hamdan also claims that he is entitled to the protections of the Third Geneva Convention, which applies to conflicts between two or more High Contracting Parties. There is no merit to Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s claim.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Article 2 of the Convention provides that \xe2\x80\x9cthe present Convention shall apply to all cases of declared war or of any other armed conflict which may arise between two or more of the High Contracting Parties.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 U. S.\xc2\xa0T., at 1318. \xe2\x80\x9cPursuant to [his] authority as Commander in Chief and Chief Executive of the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d the President has determined that the Convention is inapplicable here, explaining that \xe2\x80\x9cnone of the provisions of Geneva apply to our conflict with al Qaeda in Afghanistan or elsewhere throughout the world, because, among other reasons, al Qaeda is not a High Contracting Party.\xe2\x80\x9d App. 35. The President\xe2\x80\x99s findings about the nature of the present conflict with respect to members of al Qaeda operating in Afghanistan represents a core exercise of his commander-in-chief authority that this Court is bound to respect. See Prize Cases, 2 Black, at 670.

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For these reasons, I would affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeals.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0As previously noted, Article 15 of the Articles of War was the predecessor of Article 21 of the UCMJ. Article 21 provides as follows: \xe2\x80\x9cThe provisions of this chapter conferring jurisdiction upon courts-martial do not deprive military commissions, provost courts, or other military tribunals of concurrent jurisdiction with respect to offenders or offenses that by statute or by the law of war may be tried by military commissions, provost courts, or other military tribunals.\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7821.

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0Although the President very well may have inherent authority to try unlawful combatants for violations of the law of war before military commissions, we need not decide that question because Congress has authorized the President to do so. Cf. Hamdi v. Rumsfeld, 542 U. S. 507, 587 (2004) (Thomas, J., dissenting) (same conclusion respecting detention of unlawful combatants).

\nFootnote 3

\xc2\xa0Even if the formal declaration of war were generally the determinative act in ascertaining the temporal reach of the jurisdiction of a military commission, the AUMF itself is inconsistent with the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion that such a rule is appropriate in this case. See ante, at 34\xe2\x80\x9336, 48. The text of the AUMF is backward looking, authorizing the use of \xe2\x80\x9call necessary and appropriate force against those nations, organizations, or persons he determines planned, authorized, committed, or aided the terrorist attacks that occurred on September 11, 2001.\xe2\x80\x9d Thus, the President\xe2\x80\x99s decision to try Hamdan by military commission\xe2\x80\x94a use of force authorized by the AUMF\xe2\x80\x94for Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s involvement with al Qaeda prior to September 11, 2001, fits comfortably within the framework of the AUMF. In fact, bringing the September 11 conspirators to justice is the primary point of the AUMF. By contrast, on the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s logic, the AUMF would not grant the President the authority to try Usama bin Laden himself for his involvement in the events of September 11, 2001.

\nFootnote 4

\xc2\xa0The plurality suggests these authorities are inapplicable because nothing in its \xe2\x80\x9canalysis turns on the admitted absence of either a formal declaration of war or a declaration of martial law. Our focus instead is on the \xe2\x80\xa6 AUMF.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 35, n.\xc2\xa031. The difference identified by the plurality is purely semantic. Both Green and Howland confirm that the date of the enactment that establishes a legal basis for forming military commissions\xe2\x80\x94whether it be a declaration of war, a declaration of martial law, or an authorization to use military force\xe2\x80\x94does not limit the jurisdiction of military commissions to offenses committed after that date.

\nFootnote 5

\xc2\xa0The plurality attempts to evade the import of this historical example by observing that Kuehn was tried before a martial law commission for a violation of federal espionage statutes. Ibid. As an initial matter, the fact that Kuehn was tried before a martial law commission for an offense committed prior to the establishment of martial law provides strong support for the President\xe2\x80\x99s contention that he may try Hamdan for offenses committed prior to the enactment of the AUMF. Here the AUMF serves the same function as the declaration of martial law in Hawaii in 1941, establishing legal authority for the constitution of military commissions. Moreover, Kuehn was not tried and punished \xe2\x80\x9cby statute, but by the laws and usages of war.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Bernard Julius Otto Kuehn, Board of Review 5 (Office of the Military Governor, Hawaii 1942). Indeed, in upholding the imposition of the death penalty, a sentence \xe2\x80\x9cnot authorized by the Espionage statutes,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., Kuehn\xe2\x80\x99s Board of Review explained that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he fact that persons may be tried and punished \xe2\x80\xa6 by a military commission for committing acts defined as offenses by \xe2\x80\xa6 federal statutes does not mean that such persons are being tried for violations of such \xe2\x80\xa6 statutes; they are, instead, being tried for acts made offenses only by orders of the \xe2\x80\xa6 commanding general.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 6. Lastly, the import of this example is not undermined by Duncan v. Kahanamoku, 327 U. S. 304 (1946). The question before the Court in that case involved only whether \xe2\x80\x9cloyal civilians in loyal territory should have their daily conduct governed by military orders,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 319; it did \xe2\x80\x9cnot involve the well-established power of the military to exercise jurisdiction over . . . enemy belligerents,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 313.

\nFootnote 6

\xc2\xa0Indeed, respecting the present conflict, the President has found that \xe2\x80\x9cthe war against terrorism ushers in a new paradigm, one in which groups with broad, international reach commit horrific acts against innocent civilians, sometimes with the direct support of states. Our Nation recognizes that this new paradigm\xe2\x80\x94ushered in not by us, but by terrorists\xe2\x80\x94requires new thinking in the law of war.\xe2\x80\x9d App. 34\xe2\x80\x9335. Under the Court\xe2\x80\x99s approach, the President\xe2\x80\x99s ability to address this \xe2\x80\x9cnew paradigm\xe2\x80\x9d of inflicting death and mayhem would be completely frozen by rules developed in the context of conventional warfare.

\nFootnote 7

\xc2\xa0It is true that both of these separate offenses are charged under a single heading entitled \xe2\x80\x9cCHARGE: CONSPIRACY,\xe2\x80\x9d App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a. But that does not mean that they must be treated as a single crime, when the law of war treats them as separate crimes. As we acknowledged in In\xc2\xa0re Yamashita, 327 U. S. 1 (1946), \xe2\x80\x9ccharges of violations of the law of war triable before a military tribunal need not be stated with the precision of a common law indictment.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 17; cf. W. Birkhimer, Military Government and Martial Law 536 (3d ed. 1914) (hereinafter Birkhimer) (\xe2\x80\x9c[I]t would be extremely absurd to expect the same precision in a charge brought before a court-martial as is required to support a conviction before a justice of the peace\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)).

\n

\n\nNevertheless, the plurality contends that Hamdan was \xe2\x80\x9cnot actually charged,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 37, n. 32 (emphasis deleted), with being a member in a war criminal organization. But that position is demonstrably wrong. Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s charging document expressly charges that he \xe2\x80\x9cwillfully and knowingly joined an enterprise of persons who shared a common criminal purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d App. to Pet. for Cert. 65a. Moreover, the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s contention that we may only look to the label affixed to the charge to determine if the charging document alleges an offense triable by military commission is flatly inconsistent with its treatment of the Civil War cases\xe2\x80\x94where it accepts as valid charges that did not appear in the heading or title of the charging document, or even the listed charge itself, but only in the supporting specification. See, e.g., ante, at 45\xe2\x80\x9346 (discussing the military commission trial of Wirz). For example, in the Wirz case, Wirz was charged with conspiring to violate the laws of war, and that charge was supported with allegations that he personally committed a number of atrocities. The plurality concludes that military commission jurisdiction was appropriate in that case not based upon the charge of conspiracy, but rather based upon the allegations of various atrocities in the specification which were not separately charged. Ante, at 45. Just as these atrocities, not separately charged, were independent violations of the law of war supporting Wirz\xe2\x80\x99s trial by military commission, so too here Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s membership in al Qaeda and his provision of various forms of assistance to al Qaeda\xe2\x80\x99s top leadership are independent violations of the law of war supporting his trial by military commission.

\nFootnote 8

\xc2\xa0These observations respecting the law of war were made by the Attorney General in defense of the military commission trial of the Lincoln conspirators\xe2\x80\x99. As the foregoing quoted portion of that opinion makes clear, the Attorney General did not, as the Court maintains, \xe2\x80\x9ctrea[t] the charge as if it alleged the substantive offense of assassination.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 40, n. 35. Rather, he explained that the conspirators \xe2\x80\x9chigh offence against the laws of war\xe2\x80\x9d was \xe2\x80\x9ccomplete\xe2\x80\x9d when their band was \xe2\x80\x9corganized or joined,\xe2\x80\x9d and did not depend upon \xe2\x80\x9catrocities committed by such a band.\xe2\x80\x9d 11 Op. Atty. Gen. 297, 312 (1865). Moreover, the Attorney General\xe2\x80\x99s conclusions specifically refute the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s unsupported suggestion that I have blurred the line between \xe2\x80\x9cthose categories of \xe2\x80\x98offender\xe2\x80\x99 who may be tried by military commission \xe2\x80\xa6 with the \xe2\x80\x98offenses\xe2\x80\x99 that may be so tried.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 37, n. 32.

\nFootnote 9

\xc2\xa0The General Orders establishing the jurisdiction for military commissions during the Civil War provided that such offenses were violations of the laws of war cognizable before military commissions. See H.\xc2\xa0R. Doc. No. 65, 55th Cong., 3d Sess., 164 (1894) (\xe2\x80\x9c[P]ersons charged with the violation of the laws of war as spies, bridge-burners, marauders, &c., will \xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\xa6 be held for trial under such charges\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 234 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]here are numerous rebels \xe2\x80\xa6 that \xe2\x80\xa6 furnish the enemy with arms, provisions, clothing, horses and means of transportation; [such] insurgents are banding together in several of the interior counties for the purpose of assisting the enemy to rob, to maraud and to lay waste to the country. All such persons are by the laws of war in every civilized country liable to capital punishment\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)). Numerous trials were held under this authority. See, e.g., U. S. War Dept., General Court-Martial Order No. 51, p.\xc2\xa01 (1866) (hereinafter G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O.). (indictment in the military commission trial of James Harvey Wells charged \xe2\x80\x9c[b]eing a guerrilla\xe2\x80\x9d and specified that he \xe2\x80\x9cwillfully \xe2\x80\xa6 [took] up arms as a guerrilla marauder, and did join, belong to, act and co-operate with guerrillas\xe2\x80\x9d); G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No. 108, Head-Quarters Dept. of Kentucky, p.\xc2\xa01 (1865) (indictment in the military commission trial of Henry C. Magruder charged \xe2\x80\x9c[b]eing a guerrilla\xe2\x80\x9d and specified that he \xe2\x80\x9cunlawfully, and of his own wrong, [took] up arms as a guerrilla marauder, and did join, belong to, act, and co-operate with a band of guerrillas\xe2\x80\x9d); G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No. 41, p.\xc2\xa01 (1864) (indictment in the military commission trial of John West Wilson charged that Wilson \xe2\x80\x9cdid take up arms as an insurgent and guerrilla against the laws and authorities of the United States, and did join and co-operate with an armed band of insurgents and guerrillas who were engaged in plundering the property of peaceable citizens \xe2\x80\xa6 in violation of the laws and customs of war\xe2\x80\x9d); G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No. 153, p.\xc2\xa01 (1864) (indictment in the military commission trial of Simeon B. Kight charged that defendant was \xe2\x80\x9ca guerrilla, and has been engaged in an unwarrantable and barbarous system of warfare against citizens and soldiers of the United States\xe2\x80\x9d); G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No.\xc2\xa093, pp.\xc2\xa03\xe2\x80\x934 (1864) (indictment in the military commission trial of Francis H. Norvel charged \xe2\x80\x9c[b]eing a guerrilla\xe2\x80\x9d and specified that he \xe2\x80\x9cunlawfully and by his own wrong, [took] up arms as an outlaw, guerrilla, and bushwhacker, against the lawfully constituted authorities of the United States government\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 9 (indictment in the military commission trial of James A. Powell charged \xe2\x80\x9c[t]ransgression of the laws and customs of war\xe2\x80\x9d and specified that he \xe2\x80\x9c[took] up arms in insurrection as a military insurgent, and did join himself to and, in arms, consort with \xe2\x80\xa6 a rebel enemy of the United States, and the leader of a band of insurgents and armed rebels\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 10\xe2\x80\x9311 (indictment in the military commission trial of Joseph Overstreet charged \xe2\x80\x9c[b]eing a guerrilla\xe2\x80\x9d and specified that he \xe2\x80\x9cdid join, belong to, consort and co-operate with a band of guerrillas, insurgents, outlaws, and public robbers\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 10

\xc2\xa0Even if the plurality were correct that a membership offense must be accompanied by allegations that the \xe2\x80\x9cdefendant \xe2\x80\x98took up arms,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 37, n.\xc2\xa032, that requirement has easily been satisfied here. Not only has Hamdan been charged with providing assistance to top al Qaeda leadership (itself an offense triable by military commission), he has also been charged with receiving weapons training at an al Qaeda camp. App. to Pet. for Cert. 66a\xe2\x80\x9367a.

\nFootnote 11

\xc2\xa0The plurality recounts the respective claims of the parties in Quirin pertaining to this issue and cites the United States Reports. Ante, at 41-42. But the claims of the parties are not included in the opinion of the Court, but rather in the sections of the Reports entitled \xe2\x80\x9cArgument for Petitioners,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cArgument for Respondent.\xe2\x80\x9d See 317 U. S., at \n6\xe2\x80\x9317.

\nFootnote 12

\xc2\xa0The plurality concludes that military commission jurisdiction was appropriate in the case of the Lincoln conspirators because they were charged with \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98maliciously, unlawfully, and traitorously murdering the said Abraham Lincoln,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 40, n.\xc2\xa035. But the sole charge filed in that case alleged conspiracy, and the allegations pertaining to \xe2\x80\x9cmaliciously, unlawfully, and traitorously murdering the said Abraham Lincoln\xe2\x80\x9d were not charged or labeled as separate offenses, but rather as overt acts \xe2\x80\x9cin pursuance of and in prosecuting said malicious, unlawful, and traitorous conspiracy. \xe2\x80\x9d G.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0M.\xc2\xa0O. No.\xc2\xa0356, at ___ (emphasis added). While the plurality contends the murder of President Lincoln was charged as a distinct separate offense, the foregoing quoted language of the charging document unequivocally establishes otherwise. Moreover, though I agree that the allegations pertaining to these overt acts provided an independent basis for the military commission\xe2\x80\x99s jurisdiction in that case, that merely confirms the propriety of examining all the acts alleged\xe2\x80\x94whether or not they are labeled as separate offenses\xe2\x80\x94to determine if a defendant has been charged with a violation of the law of war. As I have already explained, Hamdan has been charged with violating the law of war not only by participating in a conspiracy to violate the law of war, but also by joining a war criminal enterprise and by supplying provisions and assistance to that enterprise\xe2\x80\x99s top leadership.

\nFootnote 13

\xc2\xa0The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s attempt to undermine the significance of these cases is unpersuasive. The plurality suggests the Wirz case is not relevant because the specification supporting his conspiracy charge alleged that he \xe2\x80\x9cpersonally committed a number of atrocities.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 45. But this does not establish that conspiracy to violate the laws of war, the very crime with which Wirz was charged, is not itself a violation of the law of war. Rather, at best, it establishes that in addition to conspiracy Wirz violated the laws of war by committing various atrocities, just as Hamdan violated the laws of war not only by conspiring to do so, but also by joining al Qaeda and providing provisions and services to its top leadership. Moreover, the fact that Wirz was charged with overt acts that are more severe than the overt acts with which Hamdan has been charged does not establish that conspiracy is not an offense cognizable before military commission; rather it merely establishes that Wirz\xe2\x80\x99s offenses may have been comparably worse than Hamdan\xe2\x80\x99s offenses.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s claim that the charge against Lenger Grenfel supports its compound offense theory is similarly unsupportable. The plurality does not, and cannot, dispute that Grenfel was charged with conspiring to violate the laws of war by releasing rebel prisoners\xe2\x80\x94a charge that bears no relation to a crime \xe2\x80\x9cordinarily triable in civilian courts.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 46, n. 37. Tellingly, the plurality does not reference or discuss this charge, but instead refers to the conclusion of Judge Advocate Holt that Grenfel also \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98united himself with traitors and malefactors for the overthrow of our Republic in the interest of slavery.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (quoting H.\xc2\xa0R. Doc. No.\xc2\xa0314, at 689). But Judge Advocate Holt\xe2\x80\x99s observation provides no support for the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion, as it does not discuss the charges that sustained military commission jurisdiction, much less suggest that such charges were not violations of the law of war.

\nFootnote 14

\xc2\xa0The plurality contends that international practice\xe2\x80\x94including the practice of the IMT at Nuremberg\xe2\x80\x94supports its conclusion that conspiracy is not an offense triable by military commission because \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[t]he Anglo-American concept of conspiracy was not part of European legal systems and arguably not an element of the internationally recognized laws of war.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 47 (quoting T. Taylor, Anatomy of the Nuremberg Trials: A Personal Memoir 36 (1992)). But while the IMT did not criminalize all conspiracies to violate the law of war, it did criminalize \xe2\x80\x9cparticipation in a common plan or conspiracy\xe2\x80\x9d to wage aggressive war. See 1 Trials, pp. XI\xe2\x80\x93XII. Moreover, the World War II military tribunals of several European nations recognized conspiracy to violate the laws of war as an offense triable before military commissions. See 15 U.\xc2\xa0N. Commission 90\xe2\x80\x9391 (noting that the French Military Tribunal at Marseilles found Henri Georges Stadelhofer \xe2\x80\x9cguilty of the crime of association de malfaiteurs,\xe2\x80\x9d namely of \xe2\x80\x9chaving formed with various members of the German Gestapo an association with the aim of preparing or committing crimes against persons or property, without justification under the laws and usages of war\xe2\x80\x9d); 11 id., at 98 (noting that the Netherlands\xe2\x80\x99 military tribunals were authorized to try conspiracy to violate the laws of war). Thus, the European legal systems\xe2\x80\x99 approach to domestic conspiracy law has not prevented European nations from recognizing conspiracy offenses as violations of the law of war. This is unsurprising, as the law of war is derived not from domestic law but from the wartime practices of civilized nations, including the United States, which has consistently recognized that conspiracy to violate the laws of war is an offense triable by military commission.

\nFootnote 15

\xc2\xa0Though it does not constitute a basis for any holding of the Court, the Court maintains that, as a \xe2\x80\x9cgeneral rule,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cthe procedures governing trials by military commission historically have been the same as those governing courts-martial.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 54, 53. While it is undoubtedly true that military commissions have invariably employed most of the procedures employed by courts-martial, that is not a requirement. See Winthrop 841 (\xe2\x80\x9c[M]ilitary commissions \xe2\x80\xa6 are commonly conducted according to the rules and forms governing courts-martial. These war-courts are indeed more summary in their action than are the courts held under the Articles of war, and \xe2\x80\xa6 their proceedings \xe2\x80\xa6 will not be rendered illegal by the omission of details required upon trials by courts-martial\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis in original; footnotes omitted)); 1 U.\xc2\xa0N. Commission 116\xe2\x80\x93117 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe [World War II] Mediterranean Regulations (No. 8) provide that Military Commissions shall conduct their proceedings as may be deemed necessary for full and fair trial, having regard for, but not being bound by, the rules of procedure prescribed for General Courts Martial\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)); id., at 117 (\xe2\x80\x9cIn the [World War II] European directive it is stated \xe2\x80\xa6 that Military Commissions shall have power to make, as occasion requires, such rules for the conduct of their proceedings consistent with the powers of such Commissions, and with the rules of procedure \xe2\x80\xa6 as are deemed necessary for a full and fair trial of the accused, having regard for, without being bound by, the rules of procedure and evidence prescribed for General Courts Martial\xe2\x80\x9d). Moreover, such a requirement would conflict with the settled understanding of the flexible and responsive nature of military commissions and the President\xe2\x80\x99s wartime authority to employ such tribunals as he sees fit. See Birkhimer 537\xe2\x80\x93538 (\xe2\x80\x9c[M]ilitary commissions may so vary their procedure as to adapt it to any situation, and may extend their powers to any necessary degree\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. The military commander decides upon the character of the military tribunal which is suited to the occasion \xe2\x80\xa6 and his decision is final\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 16

\xc2\xa0The Court suggests that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 amendment to Article 2 of the UCMJ, providing that the UCMJ applies to \xe2\x80\x9cpersons within an area leased by or otherwise reserved or acquired for the use of the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7802(a)(12), deprives Yamashita\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion respecting the President\xe2\x80\x99s authority to promulgate military commission procedures of its \xe2\x80\x9cprecedential value.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 56. But this merely begs the question of the scope and content of the remaining provisions of the UCMJ. Nothing in the additions to Article 2, or any other provision of the UCMJ, suggests that Congress has disturbed this Court\xe2\x80\x99s unequivocal interpretation of Article 21 as preserving the common-law status of military commissions and the corresponding authority of the President to set their procedures pursuant to his commander-in-chief powers. See Quirin, 317 U. S., at 28; Yamashita, 327 U. S., at 20; Madsen v. Kinsella, 343 U. S. 341, 355 (1952).

\nFootnote 17

\xc2\xa0It bears noting that while the Court does not hesitate to cite legislative history that supports its view of certain statutory provisions, see ante, at 14\xe2\x80\x9315, and n.\xc2\xa010, it makes no citation of the legislative history pertaining to Article 36(b), which contradicts its interpretation of that provision. Indeed, if it were authoritative, the only legislative history relating to Article 36(b) would confirm the obvious\xe2\x80\x94Article 36(b)\xe2\x80\x99s uniformity requirement pertains to uniformity between the three branches of the Armed Forces, and no more. When that subsection was introduced as an amendment to Article 36, its author explained that it would leave the three branches \xe2\x80\x9cenough leeway to provide a different provision where it is absolutely necessary\xe2\x80\x9d because \xe2\x80\x9cthere are some differences in the services.\xe2\x80\x9d Hearings on H.\xc2\xa0R. 2498 before the Subcommittee No. 1 of the House Committee on Armed Services, 81st Cong., 1st Sess., 1015 (1949). A further statement explained that \xe2\x80\x9cthere might be some slight differences that would pertain as to the Navy in contrast to the Army, but at least [Article 36(b)] is an expression of the congressional intent that we want it to be as uniform as possible.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\nFootnote 18

\xc2\xa0In addition to being foreclosed by the text of the provision, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion that 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7839(c) (Supp. 2006) applies to military commissions is untenable because it would require, in military commission proceedings, that the accused be present when the members of the commission voted on his guilt or innocence.

\nFootnote 19

\xc2\xa0The Court does not dispute the conclusion that Common Article 3 cannot be violated unless and until Hamdan is convicted and sentenced. Instead, it contends that \xe2\x80\x9cthe Geneva Conventions d[o] not direct an accused to wait until sentence is imposed to challenge the legality of the tribunal that is to try him.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 62, n.\xc2\xa055. But the Geneva Contentions do not direct defendants to enforce their rights through litigation, but through the Conventions\xe2\x80\x99 exclusive diplomatic enforcement provisions. Moreover, neither the Court\xe2\x80\x99s observation respecting the Geneva Conventions nor its reference to the equitable doctrine of abstention bears on the constitutional prohibition on adjudicating unripe claims.

\nFootnote 20

\xc2\xa0Notably, a prosecutor before the Quirin military commission has described these procedures as \xe2\x80\x9ca substantial improvement over those in effect during World War II,\xe2\x80\x9d further observing that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]hey go a long way toward assuring that the trials will be full and fair.\xe2\x80\x9d National Institute of Military Justice, Procedures for Trials by Military Commissions of Certain Non-United States Citizens in the War Against Terrorism, p.\xc2\xa0x (2002) (hereinafter Procedures for Trials) (foreword by Lloyd N. Cutler).

\n\n
\n
\n
\n
\n\n
\nSCALIA, J., DISSENTING
\nHAMDAN V. RUMSFELD
\n548 U. S. ____ (2006)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 05-184
\n
\n

SALIM AHMED HAMDAN, PETITIONER v. DONALD\nH. RUMSFELD, SECRETARY OF DEFENSE, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the district of columbia circuit

\n

[June 29, 2006]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Scalia, with whom Justice Thomas and Justice Alito join, dissenting.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0On December 30, 2005, Congress enacted the Detainee Treatment Act (DTA). It unambiguously provides that, as of that date, \xe2\x80\x9cno court, justice, or judge\xe2\x80\x9d shall have jurisdiction to consider the habeas application of a Guantanamo Bay detainee. Notwithstanding this plain directive, the Court today concludes that, on what it calls the statute\xe2\x80\x99s most natural reading, every \xe2\x80\x9ccourt, justice, or judge\xe2\x80\x9d before whom such a habeas application was pending on December 30 has jurisdiction to hear, consider, and render judgment on it. This conclusion is patently erroneous. And even if it were not, the jurisdiction supposedly retained should, in an exercise of sound equitable discretion, not be exercised.

\n

I

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The DTA provides: \xe2\x80\x9c[N]o court, justice, or judge shall have jurisdiction to hear or consider an application for a writ of habeas corpus filed by or on behalf of an alien detained by the Department of Defense at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005(e)(1), 119 Stat. 2742 (internal division omitted). This provision \xe2\x80\x9ct[ook] effect on the date of the enactment of this Act,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005(h)(1), id., at 2743, which was December 30, 2005. As of that date, then, no court had jurisdiction to \xe2\x80\x9chear or consider\xe2\x80\x9d the merits of petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s habeas application. This repeal of jurisdiction is simply not ambiguous as between pending and future cases. It prohibits any exercise of jurisdiction, and it became effective as to all cases last December 30. It is also perfectly clear that the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cno court, justice, or judge\xe2\x80\x9d includes this Court and its Members, and that by exercising our appellate jurisdiction in this case we are \xe2\x80\x9chear[ing] or consider[ing] \xe2\x80\xa6 an application for a writ of habeas\ncorpus.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0An ancient and unbroken line of authority attests that statutes ousting jurisdiction unambiguously apply to cases pending at their effective date. For example, in Bruner v. United States, 343 U. S. 112 (1952), we granted certiorari to consider whether the Tucker Act\xe2\x80\x99s provision denying district court jurisdiction over suits by \xe2\x80\x9cofficers\xe2\x80\x9d of the United States barred a suit by an employee of the United States. After we granted certiorari, Congress amended the Tucker Act by adding suits by \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98employees\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to the provision barring jurisdiction over suits by officers. Id., at 114. This statute narrowing the jurisdiction of the district courts \xe2\x80\x9cbecame effective\xe2\x80\x9d while the case was pending before us, ibid., and made no explicit reference to pending cases. Because the statute \xe2\x80\x9cdid not reserve jurisdiction over pending cases,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 115, we held that it clearly ousted jurisdiction over them. Summarizing centuries of practice, we said: \xe2\x80\x9cThis rule\xe2\x80\x94that, when a law conferring jurisdiction is repealed without any reservation as to pending cases, all cases fall with the law\xe2\x80\x94has been adhered to consistently by this Court.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 116\xe2\x80\x93117. See also Landgraf v. USI Film Products, 511 U. S. 244, 274 (1994) (opinion for the Court by Stevens, J.) (\xe2\x80\x9cWe have regularly applied intervening statutes conferring or ousting jurisdiction, whether or not jurisdiction lay when the underlying conduct occurred or when the suit was filed\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This venerable rule that statutes ousting jurisdiction terminate jurisdiction in pending cases is not, as today\xe2\x80\x99s opinion for the Court would have it, a judge-made \xe2\x80\x9cpresumption against jurisdiction,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 11, that we have invented to resolve an ambiguity in the statutes. It is simple recognition of the reality that the plain import of a statute repealing jurisdiction is to eliminate the power to consider and render judgment\xe2\x80\x94in an already pending case no less than in a case yet to be filed.

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cWithout jurisdiction the court cannot proceed at all in any cause. Jurisdiction is power to declare the law, and when it ceases to exist, the only function remaining to the court is that of announcing the fact and dismissing the cause. And this is not less clear upon authority than upon principle.\xe2\x80\x9d Ex parte McCardle, 7\xc2\xa0Wall. 506, 514 (1869) (emphasis added).

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To alter this plain meaning, our cases have required an explicit reservation of pending cases in the jurisdiction-repealing statute. For example, Bruner, as mentioned, looked to whether Congress made \xe2\x80\x9cany reservation as to pending cases.\xe2\x80\x9d 343 U. S., at 116\xe2\x80\x93117; see also id., at 115 (\xe2\x80\x9cCongress made no provision for cases pending at the effective date of the Act withdrawing jurisdiction and, for this reason, Courts of Appeals ordered pending cases terminated for want of jurisdiction\xe2\x80\x9d). Likewise, in Hallowell v. Commons, 239 U. S. 506 (1916), Justice Holmes relied on the fact that the jurisdiction-ousting provision \xe2\x80\x9cmade no exception for pending litigation, but purported to be universal,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 508. And in Insurance Co. v. Ritchie, 5 Wall. 541 (1867), we again relied on the fact that the jurisdictional repeal was made \xe2\x80\x9cwithout any saving of such causes as that before us,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 544. As in Bruner, Hallowell, and Ritchie, the DTA\xe2\x80\x99s directive that \xe2\x80\x9cno court, justice, or judge shall have jurisdiction,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005(e)(1), 119 Stat. 2742, is made \xe2\x80\x9cwithout any reservation as to pending cases\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cpurport[s] to be universal.\xe2\x80\x9d What we stated in an earlier case remains true here: \xe2\x80\x9c[W]hen, if it had been the intention to confine the operation of [the jurisdictional repeal] \xe2\x80\xa6 to cases not pending, it would have been so easy to have said so, we must presume that Congress meant the language employed should have its usual and ordinary signification, and that the old law should be unconditionally repealed.\xe2\x80\x9d Railroad Co. v. Grant, 98 U. S. 398, 403 (1879).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court claims that I \xe2\x80\x9crea[d] too much into\xe2\x80\x9d the Bruner line of cases, ante, at 12, n.\xc2\xa07, and that \xe2\x80\x9cthe Bruner rule\xe2\x80\x9d has never been \xe2\x80\x9can inflexible trump,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 19. But the Court sorely misdescribes Bruner\xe2\x80\x94as if it were a kind of early-day Lindh v. Murphy, 521 U. S. 320 (1997), resolving statutory ambiguity by oblique negative inference. On the contrary, as described above, Bruner stated its holding as an unqualified \xe2\x80\x9crule,\xe2\x80\x9d which \xe2\x80\x9chas been adhered to consistently by this Court.\xe2\x80\x9d 343 U. S., at 116\xe2\x80\x93117. Though Bruner referred to an express savings clause elsewhere in the statute, id., at 115, n.\xc2\xa07, it disavowed any reliance on such oblique indicators to vary the plain meaning, quoting Ritchie at length: \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98It is quite possible that this effect of the [jurisdiction-stripping statute] was not contemplated by Congress\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. [B]ut when terms are unambiguous we may not speculate on probabilities of intention.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 343 U. S., at 116 (quoting 5 Wall., at 544\xe2\x80\x93545).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court also attempts to evade the Bruner line of cases by asserting that \xe2\x80\x9cthe \xe2\x80\x98presumption\xe2\x80\x99 [of application to pending cases] that these cases have applied is more accurately viewed as the nonapplication of another presumption\xe2\x80\x94viz., the presumption against retroactivity\xe2\x80\x94in certain limited circumstances.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 11. I have already explained that what the Court calls a \xe2\x80\x9cpresumption\xe2\x80\x9d is simply the acknowledgment of the unambiguous meaning of such provisions. But even taking it to be what the Court says, the effect upon the present case would be the same. Prospective applications of a statute are \xe2\x80\x9ceffective\xe2\x80\x9d upon the statute\xe2\x80\x99s effective date; that is what an effective-date provision like \xc2\xa71005(h)(1) means.[Footnote 1] \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[S]hall take effect upon enactment\xe2\x80\x99 is presumed to mean \xe2\x80\x98shall have prospective effect upon enactment,\xe2\x80\x99 and that presumption is too strong to be overcome by any negative inference [drawn from other provisions of the statute].\xe2\x80\x9d Landgraf, 511 U. S., at 288 (Scalia, J., concurring in judgments). The Court\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cnonapplication of \xe2\x80\xa6 the presumption against retroactivity\xe2\x80\x9d to \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) is thus just another way of stating that the statute takes immediate effect in pending cases.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Though the Court resists the Bruner rule, it cannot cite a single case in the history of Anglo-American law (before today) in which a jurisdiction-stripping provision was denied immediate effect in pending cases, absent an explicit statutory reservation. By contrast, the cases granting such immediate effect are legion, and they repeatedly rely on the plain language of the jurisdictional repeal as an \xe2\x80\x9cinflexible trump,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 19, by requiring an express reservation to save pending cases. See, e.g., Bruner, supra, at 115; Kline v. Burke Constr. Co., 260 U. S. 226, 234 (1922); Hallowell, 239 U. S., at 508; Gwin v. United States, 184 U. S. 669, 675 (1902); Gurnee v. Patrick County, 137 U. S. 141, 144 (1890); Sherman v. Grinnell, 123 U. S. 679, 680 (1887); Railroad Co. v. Grant, supra, at 403, Assessors v. Osbornes, 9 Wall. 567, 575 (1870); Ex parte McCardle, 7 Wall., at 514; Ritchie, supra, at 544; Norris v. Crocker, 13 How. 429, 440 (1852); Yeaton v. United States, 5 Cranch 281 (1809) (Marshall, C.\xc2\xa0J.), discussed in Gwin, supra, at 675; King v. Justices of the Peace of London, 3 Burr. 1456, 1457, 97 Eng. Rep. 924, 925 (K.\xc2\xa0B. 1764). Cf. National Exchange Bank of Baltimore v. Peters, 144 U. S. 570, 572 (1892).

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Disregarding the plain meaning of \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) and the requirement of explicit exception set forth in the foregoing cases, the Court instead favors \xe2\x80\x9ca negative inference \xe2\x80\xa6 from the exclusion of language from one statutory provision that is included in other provisions of the same statute,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 13. Specifically, it appeals to the fact that \xc2\xa71005(e)(2) and (e)(3) are explicitly made applicable to pending cases (by \xc2\xa71005(h)(2)). A negative inference of the sort the Court relies upon might clarify the meaning of an ambiguous provision, but since the meaning of \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) is entirely clear, the omitted language in that context would have been redundant.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even if \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) were at all ambiguous in its application to pending cases, the \xe2\x80\x9cnegative inference\xe2\x80\x9d from \xc2\xa71005(h)(2) touted by the Court would have no force. The numerous cases in the Bruner line would at least create a powerful default \xe2\x80\x9cpresumption against jurisdiction,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 11. The negative inference urged by the Court would be a particularly awkward and indirect way of rebutting such a longstanding and consistent practice. This is especially true since the negative inference that might be drawn from \xc2\xa71005(h)(2)\xe2\x80\x99s specification that certain provisions shall apply to pending cases is matched by a negative inference in the opposite direction that might be drawn from \xc2\xa71005(b)(2), which provides that certain provisions shall not apply to pending cases.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court\xe2\x80\x99s reliance on our opinion in Lindh v. Murphy, 521 U. S. 320 (1997), is utterly misplaced. Lindh involved two provisions of the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (AEDPA): a set of amendments to chapter 153 of the federal habeas statute that redefined the scope of collateral review by federal habeas courts; and a provision creating a new chapter 154 in the habeas statute specially to govern federal collateral review of state capital cases. See 521 U. S., at 326\xe2\x80\x93327. The latter provision explicitly rendered the new chapter 154 applicable to cases pending at the time of AEDPA\xe2\x80\x99s enactment; the former made no specific reference to pending cases. Id., at 327. In Lindh, we drew a negative inference from chapter 154\xe2\x80\x99s explicit reference to pending cases, to conclude that the chapter 153 amendments did not apply in pending cases. It was essential to our reasoning, however, that both provisions appeared to be identically difficult to classify under our retroactivity cases. First, we noted that, after Landgraf, there was reason for Congress to suppose that an explicit statement was required to render the amendments to chapter 154 applicable in pending cases, because the new chapter 154 \xe2\x80\x9cwill have substantive as well as purely procedural effects.\xe2\x80\x9d 521 U. S., at 327. The next step\xe2\x80\x94and the critical step\xe2\x80\x94in our reasoning was that Congress had identical reason to suppose that an explicit statement would be required to apply the chapter 153 amendments to pending cases, but did not provide it. Id., at 329. The negative inference of Lindh rested on the fact that \xe2\x80\x9c[n]othing \xe2\x80\xa6 but a different intent explain[ed] the different treatment.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Here, by contrast, there is ample reason for the different treatment. The exclusive-review provisions of the DTA, unlike both \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) and the AEDPA amendments in Lindh, confer new jurisdiction (in the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit) where there was none before. For better or for worse, our recent cases have contrasted jurisdiction-creating provisions with jurisdiction-ousting provisions, retaining the venerable rule that the latter are not retroactive even when applied in pending cases, but strongly indicating that the former are typically retroactive. For example, we stated in Hughes Aircraft Co. v. United States ex rel. Schumer, 520 U. S. 939, 951 (1997), that a statute \xe2\x80\x9cthat creates jurisdiction where none previously existed\xe2\x80\x9d is \xe2\x80\x9cas much subject to our presumption against retroactivity as any other.\xe2\x80\x9d See also Republic of Austria v. Altmann, 541 U. S. 677, 695 (2004) (opinion for the Court by Stevens, J.); id., at 722 (Kennedy, J., dissenting). The Court gives our retroactivity jurisprudence a dazzling clarity in asserting that \xe2\x80\x9csubsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) \xe2\x80\x98confer\xe2\x80\x99 jurisdiction in a manner that cannot conceivably give rise to retroactivity questions under our precedents.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 2] Ante, at 17\xe2\x80\x9318. This statement rises to the level of sarcasm when one considers its author\xe2\x80\x99s description of the governing test of our retroactivity jurisprudence:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cThe conclusion that a particular rule operates \xe2\x80\x98retroactively\xe2\x80\x99 comes at the end of a process of judgment concerning the nature and extent of the change in the law and the degree of connection between the operation of the new rule and a relevant past event. Any test of retroactivity will leave room for disagreement in hard cases, and is unlikely to classify the enormous variety of legal changes with perfect philosophical clarity. However, retroactivity is a matter on which judges tend to have \xe2\x80\x98sound \xe2\x80\xa6 instinct[s],\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\xa6 and familiar considerations of fair notice, reasonable reliance, and settled expectations offer sound guidance.\xe2\x80\x9d Landgraf, 511 U. S., at 270 (opinion for the Court by Stevens, J.).

\n

\nThe only \xe2\x80\x9cfamiliar consideration,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9creasonable reliance,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9csettled expectation\xe2\x80\x9d I am aware of pertaining to the present case is the rule of Bruner\xe2\x80\x94applicable to \xc2\xa71005(e)(1), but not to \xc2\xa71005(e)(2) and (3)\xe2\x80\x94which the Court stubbornly disregards. It is utterly beyond question that \xc2\xa71005(e)(2)\xe2\x80\x99s and (3)\xe2\x80\x99s application to pending cases (without explicit specification) was not as clear as \xc2\xa71005(e)(1)\xe2\x80\x99s. That is alone enough to explain the difference in treatment.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Another obvious reason for the specification was to stave off any Suspension Clause problems raised by the immediately effective ouster of jurisdiction brought about by subsection (e)(1). That is to say, specification of the immediate effectiveness of subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) (which, unlike subsection (e)(1), would not fall within the Bruner rule and would not automatically be deemed applicable in pending cases) could reasonably have been thought essential to be sure of replacing the habeas jurisdiction that subsection (e)(1) eliminated in pending cases with an adequate substitute. See infra, at 16\xe2\x80\x9318.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These considerations by no means prove that an explicit statement would be required to render subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) applicable in pending cases. But they surely gave Congress ample reason to doubt that their application in pending cases would unfold as naturally as the Court glibly assumes. In any event, even if it were true that subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98confer\xe2\x80\x99 jurisdiction in a manner that cannot conceivably give rise to retroactivity questions,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 17\xe2\x80\x9318, this would merely establish that subsection (h)(2)\xe2\x80\x99s reference to pending cases was wholly superfluous when applied to subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3), just as it would have been for subsection (e)(1). Lindh\xe2\x80\x99s negative inference makes sense only when Congress would have perceived \xe2\x80\x9cthe wisdom of being explicit\xe2\x80\x9d with respect to the immediate application of both of two statutory provisions, 521 U. S., at 328, but chose to be explicit only for one of them\xe2\x80\x94not when it would have perceived no need to be explicit for both, but enacted a redundancy only for one.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In short, it is simply untrue that Congress \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98should have been just as concerned about\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d specifying the application of \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) to pending cases, ante, at 14 (quoting Lindh, 521 U. S., at 329). In fact, the negative-inference approach of Lindh is particularly inappropriate in this case, because the negative inference from \xc2\xa71005(h)(2) would tend to defeat the purpose of the very provisions that are explicitly rendered applicable in pending cases, \xc2\xa71005(e)(2) and (3). Those provisions purport to vest \xe2\x80\x9cexclusive\xe2\x80\x9d jurisdiction in the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit to consider the claims raised by petitioners here. See infra, at 16\xe2\x80\x9318. By drawing a negative inference \xc3\x80 la Lindh, the Court supplants this exclusive-review mechanism with a dual-review mechanism for petitioners who were expeditious enough to file applications challenging the CSRTs or military commissions before December 30, 2005. Whatever the force of Lindh\xe2\x80\x99s negative inference in other cases, it surely should not apply here to defeat the purpose of the very provision from which the negative inference is drawn.

\n

C

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Worst of all is the Court\xe2\x80\x99s reliance on the legislative history of the DTA to buttress its implausible reading of \xc2\xa71005(e)(1). We have repeatedly held that such reliance is impermissible where, as here, the statutory language is unambiguous. But the Court nevertheless relies both on floor statements from the Senate and (quite heavily) on the drafting history of the DTA. To begin with floor statements: The Court urges that some \xe2\x80\x9cstatements made by Senators preceding passage of the Act lend further support to\xe2\x80\x9d the Court\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation, citing excerpts from the floor debate that support its view, ante, 15\xe2\x80\x9316, n.\xc2\xa010. The Court immediately goes on to discount numerous floor statements by the DTA\xe2\x80\x99s sponsors that flatly contradict its view, because \xe2\x80\x9cthose statements appear to have been inserted into the Congressional Record after the Senate debate.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Of course this observation, even if true, makes no difference unless one indulges the fantasy that Senate floor speeches are attended (like the Philippics of Demosthenes) by throngs of eager listeners, instead of being delivered (like Demosthenes\xe2\x80\x99 practice sessions on the beach) alone into a vast emptiness. Whether the floor statements are spoken where no Senator hears, or written where no Senator reads, they represent at most the views of a single Senator. In any event, the Court greatly exaggerates the one-sidedness of the portions of the floor debate that clearly occurred before the DTA\xe2\x80\x99s enactment. Some of the statements of Senator Graham, a sponsor of the bill, only make sense on the assumption that pending cases are covered.[Footnote 3] And at least one opponent of the DTA unmistakably expressed his understanding that it would terminate our jurisdiction in this very case.[Footnote 4] (Of course in its discussion of legislative history the Court wholly ignores the President\xe2\x80\x99s signing statement, which explicitly set forth his understanding that the DTA ousted jurisdiction over pending cases.[Footnote 5])

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0But selectivity is not the greatest vice in the Court\xe2\x80\x99s use of floor statements to resolve today\xe2\x80\x99s case. These statements were made when Members of Congress were fully aware that our continuing jurisdiction over this very case was at issue. The question was divisive, and floor statements made on both sides were undoubtedly opportunistic and crafted solely for use in the briefs in this very litigation. See, e.g., 151 Cong. Rec. S14257\xe2\x80\x93S14258 (Dec. 21, 2005) (statement of Sen. Levin) (arguing against a reading that would \xe2\x80\x9cstri[p] the Federal courts of jurisdiction to consider pending cases, including the Hamdan case now pending in the Supreme Court,\xe2\x80\x9d and urging that Lindh requires the same negative inference that the Court indulges today (emphasis added)). The Court\xe2\x80\x99s reliance on such statements cannot avoid the appearance of similar opportunism. In a virtually identical context, the author of today\xe2\x80\x99s opinion has written for the Court that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he legislative history discloses some frankly partisan statements about the meaning of the final effective date language, but those statements cannot plausibly be read as reflecting any general agreement.\xe2\x80\x9d Landgraf, 511 U. S., at 262 (opinion for the Court by Stevens, J.). Likewise, the handful of floor statements that the Court treats as authoritative do not \xe2\x80\x9creflec[t] any general agreement.\xe2\x80\x9d They reflect the now-common tactic\xe2\x80\x94which the Court once again rewards\xe2\x80\x94of pursuing through floor-speech ipse dixit what could not be achieved through the constitutionally prescribed method of putting language into a bill that a majority of both Houses vote for and the President signs.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0With regard to the floor statements, at least the Court shows some semblance of seemly shame, tucking away its reference to them in a half-hearted footnote. Not so for its reliance on the DTA\xe2\x80\x99s drafting history, which is displayed prominently, see ante, at 14\xe2\x80\x9315. I have explained elsewhere that such drafting history is no more legitimate or reliable an indicator of the objective meaning of a statute than any other form of legislative history. This case presents a textbook example of its unreliability. The Court, ante, at 14, trumpets the fact that a bill considered in the Senate included redundant language, not included in the DTA as passed, reconfirming that the abolition of habeas jurisdiction \xe2\x80\x9cshall apply to any application or other action that is pending on or after the date of the enactment of this Act.\xe2\x80\x9d 151 Cong. Rec. S12655 (Nov. 10, 2005). But this earlier version of the bill also differed from the DTA in other material respects. Most notably, it provided for postdecision review by the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit only of the decisions of CSRTs, not military commissions, ibid.; and it limited that review to whether \xe2\x80\x9cthe status determination \xe2\x80\xa6 was consistent with the procedures and standards specified by the Secretary of Defense,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., not whether \xe2\x80\x9cthe use of such standards and procedures \xe2\x80\xa6 is consistent with the Constitution and laws of the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(2)(C)(ii), 119 Stat. 2742. To say that what moved Senators to reject this earlier bill was the \xe2\x80\x9caction that is pending\xe2\x80\x9d provision surpasses the intuitive powers of even this Court\xe2\x80\x99s greatest Justices.[Footnote 6] And to think that the House and the President also had this rejection firmly in mind is absurd. As always\xe2\x80\x94but especially in the context of strident, partisan legislative conflict of the sort that characterized enactment of this legislation\xe2\x80\x94the language of the statute that was actually passed by both Houses of Congress and signed by the President is our only authoritative and only reliable guidepost.

\n

D

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0A final but powerful indication of the fact that the Court has made a mess of this statute is the nature of the consequences that ensue. Though this case concerns a habeas application challenging a trial by military commission, DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) strips the courts of jurisdiction to hear or consider any \xe2\x80\x9capplication for a writ of habeas corpus filed by or on behalf of an alien detained by the Department of Defense at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba.\xe2\x80\x9d The vast majority of pending petitions, no doubt, do not relate to military commissions at all, but to more commonly challenged aspects of \xe2\x80\x9cdetention\xe2\x80\x9d such as the terms and conditions of confinement.
