See Rasul v. Bush, 542 U. S. 466, 498 (2004) (Scalia, J., dissenting). The Solicitor General represents that \xe2\x80\x9c[h]abeas petitions have been filed on behalf of a purported 600 [Guantanamo Bay] detainees,\xe2\x80\x9d including one that \xe2\x80\x9cseek[s] relief on behalf of every Guantanamo detainee who has not already filed an action,\xe2\x80\x9d Respondents\xe2\x80\x99 Motion to Dismiss for Lack of Jurisdiction 20, n.\xc2\xa010 (hereinafter Motion to Dismiss). The Court\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation transforms a provision abolishing jurisdiction over all Guantanamo-related habeas petitions into a provision that retains jurisdiction over cases sufficiently numerous to keep the courts busy for years to come.

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Because I would hold that \xc2\xa71005(e)(1) unambiguously terminates the jurisdiction of all courts to \xe2\x80\x9chear or consider\xe2\x80\x9d pending habeas applications, I must confront petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s arguments that the provision, so interpreted, violates the Suspension Clause. This claim is easily dispatched. We stated in Johnson v. Eisentrager, 339 U. S. 763, 768 (1950):

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cWe are cited to no instance where a court, in this or any other country where the writ is known, has issued it on behalf of an alien enemy who, at no relevant time and in no stage of his captivity, has been within its territorial jurisdiction. Nothing in the text of the Constitution extends such a right, nor does anything in our statutes.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\nNotwithstanding the ill-considered dicta in the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion in Rasul, 542 U. S., at 480\xe2\x80\x93481, it is clear that Guantanamo Bay, Cuba, is outside the sovereign \xe2\x80\x9cterritorial jurisdiction\xe2\x80\x9d of the United States. See id., at 500\xe2\x80\x93505 (Scalia, J., dissenting). Petitioner, an enemy alien detained abroad, has no rights under the Suspension Clause.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0But even if petitioner were fully protected by the Clause, the DTA would create no suspension problem. This Court has repeatedly acknowledged that \xe2\x80\x9cthe substitution of a collateral remedy which is neither inadequate nor ineffective to test the legality of a person\xe2\x80\x99s detention does not constitute a suspension of the writ of habeas corpus.\xe2\x80\x9d Swain v. Pressley, 430 U. S. 372, 381 (1977); see also INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U. S. 289, 314, n. 38 (2006) (\xe2\x80\x9cCongress could, without raising any constitutional questions, provide an adequate substitute through the courts of appeals\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Petitioner has made no showing that the postdecision exclusive review by the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit provided in \xc2\xa71005(e)(3) is inadequate to test the legality of his trial by military commission. His principal argument is that the exclusive-review provisions are inadequate because they foreclose review of the claims he raises here. Though petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s brief does not parse the statutory language, his argument evidently rests on an erroneously narrow reading of DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(D)(ii), 119 Stat. 2743. That provision grants the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit authority to review, \xe2\x80\x9cto the extent the Constitution and laws of the United States are applicable, whether the use of such standards and procedures to reach the final decision is consistent with the Constitution and laws of the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d In the quoted text, the phrase \xe2\x80\x9csuch standards and procedures\xe2\x80\x9d refers to \xe2\x80\x9cthe standards and procedures specified in the military order referred to in subparagraph (A),\xe2\x80\x9d namely \xe2\x80\x9cMilitary Commission Order No. 1, dated August 31, 2005 (or any successor military order).\xe2\x80\x9d DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(D)(i), (e)(3)(A), ibid. This Military Commission Order (Order No. 1) is the Department of Defense\xe2\x80\x99s fundamental implementing order for the President\xe2\x80\x99s order authorizing trials by military commission. Order No. 1 establishes commissions, \xc2\xa72; delineates their jurisdiction, \xc2\xa73; provides for their officers, \xc2\xa74(A); provides for their prosecution and defense counsel, \xc2\xa74(B), (C); lays out all their procedures, both pretrial and trial, \xc2\xa75(A)\xe2\x80\x93(P), \xc2\xa76(A)\xe2\x80\x93(G); and provides for posttrial military review through the Secretary of Defense and the President, \xc2\xa76(H). In short, the \xe2\x80\x9cstandards and procedures specified in\xe2\x80\x9d Order No. 1 include every aspect of the military commissions, including the fact of their existence and every respect in which they differ from courts-martial. Petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claims that the President lacks legal authority to try him before a military commission constitute claims that \xe2\x80\x9cthe use of such standards and procedures,\xe2\x80\x9d as specified in Order No. 1, is \xe2\x80\x9c[in]consistent with the Constitution and laws of the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d DTA \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(D)(ii), 119 Stat. 2743. The D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit thus retains jurisdiction to consider these claims on postdecision review, and the Government does not dispute that the DTA leaves unaffected our certiorari jurisdiction under 28 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71254(1) to review the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit\xe2\x80\x99s decisions. Motion to Dismiss 16, n.\xc2\xa08. Thus, the DTA merely defers our jurisdiction to consider petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claims; it does not eliminate that jurisdiction. It constitutes neither an \xe2\x80\x9cinadequate\xe2\x80\x9d nor an \xe2\x80\x9cineffective\xe2\x80\x9d substitute for petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s pending habeas application.[Footnote 7]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Though it does not squarely address the issue, the Court hints ominously that \xe2\x80\x9cthe Government\xe2\x80\x99s preferred reading\xe2\x80\x9d would \xe2\x80\x9crais[e] grave questions about Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to impinge upon this Court\xe2\x80\x99s appellate jurisdiction, particularly in habeas cases.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 10\xe2\x80\x9311 (citing Ex parte Yerger, 8 Wall. 85 (1869); Felker v. Turpin, 518 U. S. 651 (1996); Durousseau v. United States, 6 Cranch 307 (1810); United States v. Klein, 13 Wall. 128 (1872); and Ex parte McCardle, 7 Wall. 506). It is not clear how there could be any such lurking questions, in light of the aptly named \xe2\x80\x9cExceptions Clause\xe2\x80\x9d of Article III, \xc2\xa72, which, in making our appellate jurisdiction subject to \xe2\x80\x9csuch Exceptions, and under such Regulations as the Congress shall make,\xe2\x80\x9d explicitly permits exactly what Congress has done here. But any doubt our prior cases might have created on this score is surely chimerical in this case. As just noted, the exclusive-review provisions provide a substitute for habeas review adequate to satisfy the Suspension Clause, which forbids the suspension of the writ of habeas corpus. A fortiori they provide a substitute adequate to satisfy any implied substantive limitations, whether real or imaginary, upon the Exceptions Clause, which authorizes such exceptions as \xc2\xa71005(e)(1).

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even if Congress had not clearly and constitutionally eliminated jurisdiction over this case, neither this Court nor the lower courts ought to exercise it. Traditionally, equitable principles govern both the exercise of habeas jurisdiction and the granting of the injunctive relief sought by petitioner. See Schlesinger v. Councilman, 420 U. S. 738, 754 (1975); Weinberger v. Romero-Barcelo, 456 U. S. 305, 311 (1982). In light of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s provision of an alternate avenue for petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claims in \xc2\xa71005(e)(3), those equitable principles counsel that we abstain from exercising jurisdiction in this case.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In requesting abstention, the Government relies principally on Councilman, in which we abstained from considering a serviceman\xe2\x80\x99s claim that his charge for marijuana possession was not sufficiently \xe2\x80\x9cservice-connected\xe2\x80\x9d to trigger the subject-matter jurisdiction of the military courts-martial. See 420 U. S., at 740, 758. Admittedly, Councilman does not squarely control petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s case, but it provides the closest analogue in our jurisprudence. As the Court describes, ante, at 21, Councilman \xe2\x80\x9cidentifie[d] two considerations of comity that together favor[ed] abstention pending completion of ongoing court-martial proceedings against service personnel.\xe2\x80\x9d But the Court errs in finding these considerations inapplicable to this case. Both of them, and a third consideration not emphasized in Councilman, all cut in favor of abstention here.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, the Court observes that Councilman rested in part on the fact that \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary discipline and, therefore, the efficient operation of the Armed Forces are best served if the military justice system acts without regular interference from civilian courts,\xe2\x80\x9d and concludes that \xe2\x80\x9cHamdan is not a member of our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s Armed Forces, so concerns about military discipline do not apply.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 22. This is true enough. But for some reason, the Court fails to make any inquiry into whether military commission trials might involve other \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary necessities\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cunique military exigencies,\xe2\x80\x9d 420 U. S., at 757, comparable in gravity to those at stake in Councilman. To put this in context: The charge against the respondent in Councilman was the off-base possession and sale of marijuana while he was stationed in Fort Sill, Oklahoma, see id., at 739\xe2\x80\x93740. The charge against the petitioner here is joining and actively abetting the murderous conspiracy that slaughtered thousands of innocent American civilians without warning on September 11, 2001. While Councilman held that the prosecution of the former charge involved \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary necessities\xe2\x80\x9d counseling against our interference, the Court does not even ponder the same question for the latter charge.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The reason for the Court\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cblinkered study\xe2\x80\x9d of this question, ante, at 19, is not hard to fathom. The principal opinion on the merits makes clear that it does not believe that the trials by military commission involve any \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary necessity\xe2\x80\x9d at all: \xe2\x80\x9cThe charge\xe2\x80\x99s shortcomings \xe2\x80\xa6 are indicative of a broader inability on the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s part here to satisfy the most basic precondition \xe2\x80\xa6 for establishment of military commissions: military necessity.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 48. This is quite at odds with the views on this subject expressed by our political branches. Because of \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary necessity,\xe2\x80\x9d a joint session of Congress authorized the President to \xe2\x80\x9cuse all necessary and appropriate force,\xe2\x80\x9d including military commissions, \xe2\x80\x9cagainst those nations, organizations, or persons [such as petitioner] he determines planned, authorized, committed, or aided the terrorist attacks that occurred on September 11, 2001.\xe2\x80\x9d Authorization for Use of Military Force, \xc2\xa72(a), 115 Stat. 224, note following 50 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71541 (2000 ed., Supp. III). In keeping with this authority, the President has determined that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o protect the United States and its citizens, and for the effective conduct of military operations and prevention of terrorist attacks, it is necessary for individuals subject to this order \xe2\x80\xa6 to be detained, and, when tried, to be tried for violations of the laws of war and other applicable laws by military tribunals.\xe2\x80\x9d Military Order of Nov. 13, 2001, 3 CFR \xc2\xa7918(e) (2002). It is not clear where the Court derives the authority\xe2\x80\x94or the audacity\xe2\x80\x94to contradict this determination. If \xe2\x80\x9cmilitary necessities\xe2\x80\x9d relating to \xe2\x80\x9cduty\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cdiscipline\xe2\x80\x9d required abstention in Councilman, supra, at 757, military necessities relating to the disabling, deterrence, and punishment of the mass-murdering terrorists of September 11 require abstention all the more here.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court further seeks to distinguish Councilman on the ground that \xe2\x80\x9cthe tribunal convened to try Hamdan is not part of the integrated system of military courts, complete with independent review panels, that Congress has established.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 22. To be sure, Councilman emphasized that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress created an integrated system of military courts and review procedures, a critical element of which is the Court of Military Appeals consisting of civilian judges completely removed from all military influence or persuasion, who would gain over time thorough familiarity with military problems.\xe2\x80\x9d 420 U. S., at 758 (internal quotation marks and footnote omitted). The Court contrasts this \xe2\x80\x9cintegrated system\xe2\x80\x9d insulated from military influence with the review scheme established by Order No. 1, which \xe2\x80\x9cprovides that appeal of a review panel\xe2\x80\x99s decision may be had only to the Secretary of Defense himself, \xc2\xa76(H)(5), and then, finally, to the President, \xc2\xa76(H)(6).\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 23.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even if we were to accept the Court\xe2\x80\x99s extraordinary assumption that the President \xe2\x80\x9clack[s] the structural insulation from military influence that characterizes the Court of Appeals for the Armed Forces,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 23,[Footnote 8] the Court\xe2\x80\x99s description of the review scheme here is anachronistic. As of December 30, 2005, the \xe2\x80\x9cfina[l]\xe2\x80\x9d review of decisions by military commissions is now conducted by the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit pursuant to \xc2\xa71005(e)(3) of the DTA, and by this Court under 28 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71254(1). This provision for review by Article III courts creates, if anything, a review scheme more insulated from Executive control than that in Councilman.[Footnote 9] At the time we decided Councilman, Congress had not \xe2\x80\x9cconferred on any Art[icle]\xc2\xa0III court jurisdiction directly to review court-martial determinations.\xe2\x80\x9d 420 U. S., at 746. The final arbiter of direct appeals was the Court of Military Appeals (now the Court of Appeals for the Armed Forces), an Article I court whose members possessed neither life tenure, nor salary protection, nor the constitutional protection from removal provided to federal judges in Article III, \xc2\xa71. See 10 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7867(a)(2) (1970 ed.).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Moreover, a third consideration counsels strongly in favor of abstention in this case. Councilman reasoned that the \xe2\x80\x9cconsiderations of comity, the necessity of respect for coordinate judicial systems\xe2\x80\x9d that motivated our decision in Younger v. Harris, 401 U. S. 37 (1971), were inapplicable to courts-martial, because \xe2\x80\x9cthe particular demands of federalism are not implicated.\xe2\x80\x9d 420 U. S., at 756, 757. Though military commissions likewise do not implicate \xe2\x80\x9cthe particular demands of federalism,\xe2\x80\x9d considerations of interbranch comity at the federal level weigh heavily against our exercise of equity jurisdiction in this case. Here, apparently for the first time in history, see Motion to Dismiss 6, a District Court enjoined ongoing military commission proceedings, which had been deemed \xe2\x80\x9cnecessary\xe2\x80\x9d by the President \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o protect the United States and its citizens, and for the effective conduct of military operations and prevention of terrorist attacks.\xe2\x80\x9d Military Order of Nov. 13, 3 CFR \xc2\xa7918(e). Such an order brings the Judicial Branch into direct conflict with the Executive in an area where the Executive\xe2\x80\x99s competence is maximal and ours is virtually nonexistent. We should exercise our equitable discretion to avoid such conflict. Instead, the Court rushes headlong to meet it. Elsewhere, we have deferred exercising habeas jurisdiction until state courts have \xe2\x80\x9cthe first opportunity to review\xe2\x80\x9d a petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claim, merely to \xe2\x80\x9creduc[e] friction between the state and federal court systems.\xe2\x80\x9d O\xe2\x80\x99Sullivan v. Boerckel, 526 U. S. 838, 844, 845 (1999). The \xe2\x80\x9cfriction\xe2\x80\x9d created today between this Court and the Executive Branch is many times more serious.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In the face of such concerns, the Court relies heavily on Ex parte Quirin, 317 U. S. 1 (1942): \xe2\x80\x9cFar from abstaining pending the conclusion of military proceedings, which were ongoing, [in Quirin] we convened a special Term to hear the case and expedited our review.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 24. It is likely that the Government in Quirin, unlike here, preferred a hasty resolution of the case in this Court, so that it could swiftly execute the sentences imposed, see Hamdi v. Rumsfeld, 542 U. S. 507, 569 (2004) (Scalia, J., dissenting). But the Court\xe2\x80\x99s reliance on Quirin suffers from a more fundamental defect: Once again, it ignores the DTA, which creates an avenue for the consideration of petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s claims that did not exist at the time of Quirin. Collateral application for habeas review was the only vehicle available. And there was no compelling reason to postpone consideration of the Quirin application until the termination of military proceedings, because the only cognizable claims presented were general challenges to the authority of the commissions that would not be affected by the specific proceedings. See supra, at 8\xe2\x80\x939, n.\xc2\xa02. In the DTA, by contrast, Congress has expanded the scope of Article III review and has channeled it exclusively through a single, postverdict appeal to Article III courts. Because Congress has created a novel unitary scheme of Article III review of military commissions that was absent in 1942, Quirin is no longer governing precedent.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I would abstain from exercising our equity jurisdiction, as the Government requests.

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the foregoing reasons, I dissent.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0The Court apparently believes that the effective-date provision means nothing at all. \xe2\x80\x9cThat paragraph (1), along with paragraphs (2) and (3), is to \xe2\x80\x98take effect on the date of enactment,\xe2\x80\x99 DTA \xc2\xa71005(h)(1), 119 Stat. 2743, is not dispositive,\xe2\x80\x9d says the Court, ante, at 14, n.\xc2\xa09. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s authority for this conclusion is its quote from INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U. S. 289, 317 (2001), to the effect that \xe2\x80\x9ca statement that a statute will become effective on a certain date does not even arguably suggest that it has any application to conduct that occurred at an earlier date.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 14, n.\xc2\xa09 (emphasis added, internal quotation marks omitted). But this quote merely restates the obvious: An effective-date provision does not render a statute applicable to \xe2\x80\x9cconduct that occurred at an earlier date,\xe2\x80\x9d but of course it renders the statute applicable to conduct that occurs on the effective date and all future dates\xe2\x80\x94such as the Court\xe2\x80\x99s exercise of jurisdiction here. The Court seems to suggest that, because the effective-date provision does not authorize retroactive application, it also fails to authorize prospective application (and is thus useless verbiage). This cannot be true.

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0A comparison with Lindh v. Murphy, 521 U. S. 320 (1997), shows this not to be true. Subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) of \xc2\xa71005 resemble the provisions of AEDPA at issue in Lindh (whose retroactivity\nas applied to pending cases the Lindh majority did not rule upon,\nsee 521 U. S., at 326), in that they \xe2\x80\x9cg[o] beyond \xe2\x80\x98mere\xe2\x80\x99 procedure,\xe2\x80\x9d\nid., at 327. They impose novel and unprecedented disabilities on\nthe Executive Branch in its conduct of military affairs. Subsection (e)(2) imposes judicial review on the Combatant Status Review\nTribunals (CSRTs), whose implementing order did not subject them\nto review by Article III courts. See Memorandum from Deputy Secretary of Defense Paul Wolfowitz re: Order Establishing Com-\nbatant Status Review Tribunals, at 3 \xc2\xa7h (July 7, 2004), avail-\nable at http://www.defenselink.mil/news/Jul2004/d20040707review.pdf (all Internet materials as visited June 27, 2006, and available\nin Clerk of Court\xe2\x80\x99s case file). Subsection (e)(3) authorizes the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit to review \xe2\x80\x9cthe validity of any final decision rendered pursuant to Military Commission Order No. 1,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(A), 119 Stat. 2743. Historically, federal courts have never reviewed the validity of the final decision of any military commission; their jurisdiction has been restricted to considering the commission\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9clawful authority to hear, decide and condemn,\xe2\x80\x9d In\xc2\xa0re Yamashita, 327 U. S. 1, 8 (1946) (emphasis added). See also Johnson v. Eisentrager, 339 U. S. 763, 786\xe2\x80\x93787 (1950). Thus, contrary to the Court\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion, ante, at 17, subsections (e)(2) and (e)(3) confer new jurisdiction: They impose judicial oversight on a traditionally unreviewable exercise of military authority by the Commander in Chief. They arguably \xe2\x80\x9cspea[k] not just to the power of a particular court but to . . . substantive rights . . . as well,\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes Aircraft Co. v. United States ex rel. Shumer, 520 U. S. 939, 951 (1997)\xe2\x80\x94namely, the unreviewable powers of the President. Our recent cases had reiterated that the Executive is protected by the presumption against retroactivity in such comparatively trivial contexts as suits for tax refunds and increased pay, see Landgraf v. USI Film Products, 511 U. S. 244, 271, n. 25 (1994).

\nFootnote 3

\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I have described how outrageous these claims are\xe2\x80\x94about the exercise regime, the reading materials\xe2\x80\x94most Americans would be highly offended to know that terrorists are suing us in our own courts about what they read.\xe2\x80\x9d 151 Cong. Rec. S12756 (Nov. 14, 2005). \xe2\x80\x9cInstead of having unlimited habeas corpus opportunities under the Constitution, we give every enemy combatant, all 500, a chance to go to Federal court, the Circuit Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. It will be a one-time deal.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at S12754. \xe2\x80\x9cThis Levin-Graham-Kyl amendment allows every detainee under our control to have their day in court. They are allowed to appeal their convictions.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at S12801 (Nov. 15, 2005); see also id., at S12799 (rejecting the notion that \xe2\x80\x9can enemy combatant terrorist al-Qaida member should be able to have access to our Federal courts under habeas like an American citizen\xe2\x80\x9d).

\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cAn earlier part of the amendment provides that no court, justice, or judge shall have jurisdiction to consider the application for writ of habeas corpus\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Under the language of exclusive jurisdiction in the DC Circuit, the U. S. Supreme Court would not have jurisdiction to hear the Hamdan case \xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at S12796 (statement of Sen. Specter).

\nFootnote 6

\xc2\xa0The Court asserts that \xe2\x80\x9cit cannot be said that the changes to subsection (h)(2) were inconsequential,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 15, n.\xc2\xa010, but the Court\xe2\x80\x99s sole evidence is the self-serving floor statements that it selectively cites.

\nFootnote 7

\xc2\xa0Petitioner also urges that he could be subject to indefinite delay if military officials and the President are deliberately dilatory in reviewing the decision of his commission. In reviewing the constitutionality of legislation, we generally presume that the Executive will implement its provisions in good faith. And it is unclear in any event that delay would inflict any injury on petitioner, who (after an adverse determination by his CSRT, see 344 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 152, 161 (DC 2004)) is already subject to indefinite detention under our decision in Hamdi v. Rumsfeld, 542 U. S. 507 (2004). Moreover, the mere possibility of delay does not render an alternative remedy \xe2\x80\x9cinadequate [o]r ineffective to test the legality\xe2\x80\x9d of a military commission trial. Swain v. Pressley, 430 U. S. 372, 381 (1977). In an analogous context, we discounted the notion that postponement of relief until postconviction review inflicted any cognizable injury on a serviceman charged before a military court-martial. Schlesinger v. Councilman, 420 U. S. 738, 754\xe2\x80\x93755 (1975); see also Younger v. Harris, 401 U. S. 37, 46 (1971).

\nFootnote 8

\xc2\xa0The very purpose of Article II\xe2\x80\x99s creation of a civilian Commander in Chief in the President of the United States was to generate \xe2\x80\x9cstructural insulation from military influence.\xe2\x80\x9d See The Federalist No. 28 (A. Hamilton); id., No. 69 (same). We do not live under a military junta. It is a disservice to both those in the Armed Forces and the President to suggest that the President is subject to the undue control of the military.

\nFootnote 9

\xc2\xa0In rejecting our analysis, the Court observes that appeals to the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit under subsection (e)(3) are discretionary, rather than as of right, when the military commission imposes a sentence less than 10 years\xe2\x80\x99 imprisonment, see ante, at 23, n.\xc2\xa019, 52\xe2\x80\x9353; \xc2\xa71005(e)(3)(B), 119 Stat. 2743. The relevance of this observation to the abstention question is unfathomable. The fact that Article III review is discretionary does not mean that it lacks \xe2\x80\x9cstructural insulation from military influence,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 23, and its discretionary nature presents no obstacle to the courts\xe2\x80\x99 future review these cases.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court might more cogently have relied on the discretionary nature of review to argue that the statute provides an inadequate substitute for habeas review under the Suspension Clause. See supra, at 16\xe2\x80\x9318. But this argument would have no force, even if all appeals to the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit were discretionary. The exercise of habeas jurisdiction has traditionally been entirely a matter of the court\xe2\x80\x99s equitable discretion, see Withrow v. Williams, 507 U. S. 680, 715\xe2\x80\x93718 (1993) (Scalia, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part), so the fact that habeas jurisdiction is replaced by discretionary appellate review does not render the substitution \xe2\x80\x9cinadequate.\xe2\x80\x9d Swain, 430 U. S., at 381.
